waterspring





I



the SCENE





the bright pale February sunlight lie on the little court of Beaufort

College, Cambridge, on the old dull-red smoke-stain brick, the stone

mullion and moulding, the Hall oriel, the ivie buttress and

battlement, the turret, the tile roof, the quaint chimney, and the

lead-top cupola over all. half the court be in shadow. it be

incredibly picturesque, but it have somehow the look of a fortress

rather than of a house. it do not exist only to be beautiful, but have

a well-wear beauty of age and use. there be no domestic adornment of

flower-bed or garden-border, merely four square of grass, look like

fade carpet lay on the rather uncompromise pebble which floor

the pathway. the golden hand of the clock point to a quarter to

ten, and the chime utter their sharp, peremptory voice. two or

three young man stand talk at the vaulted gateway, and one or two

figure in dilapidated gown and cap, hold book, flee out of the

court.



a firm footstep come down one of the stairway; a man of about forty

pass out into the court--Howard Kennedy, Fellow and Classical

Lecturer of the College. his thick curly brown hair show a trace of

grey, his short point beard be grizzle, his complexion sanguine,

his eyebrow thick. there be little vague line on his forehead, and

his eye be large and clear; an interesting, expressive face, not

technically handsome, but both clever and good-natured. he be

carelessly dress in rather old but well-cut clothe, and have an air

of business-like decisiveness which become he well, and make he seem

comfortably at home in the place; he nod and smile to the

undergraduate at the gate, who smile back and salute. he meet a young

man rush down the court, and say to he, "that's right, hurry up!

you'll just be in time," a remark which be answer by a gesture of

despair from the young man. then he go up the court towards the Hall,

enter the flagged passage, look for a moment at the notice on the

screen, and go through into the back court, which be surround by a

tiny cloister.



here he meet an elderly man, clean-shaven, fresh-colour,

acute-looking, who wear a little round bowler hat perch on a thick

shock of white hair. he be dress in a black coat and waistcoat, with

a black tie, and wear rather light grey trouser. one would have take

he for an old-fashioned country solicitor. he be, as a matter of

fact, the Vice-Master and Senior Fellow of the College--Mr. Redmayne,

who have spend his whole life there. he greet the young man with a

kindly, brisk, ironical manner, say, "you look very virtuous,

Kennedy! what be you up to?"



"I be go for a turn in the garden," say Howard; "will you come with

I?"



"you be very good," say Mr. Redmayne; "it will be quite like a

dialogue of Plato!"



they go down the cloister to a low door in the corner, which Howard

unlocked, and turn into a small old-fashioned garden, surround on

three side by high wall, and overlook the river on the fourth

side; a gravel path run all round; there be a few tree, bare and

leafless, and a big bed of shrub in the centre of the little lawn,

just faintly prick with point of green. a few aconite show their

yellow head above the soil.



"what be those wretched little flower?" say Mr. Redmayne, point

at they contemptuously.



"oh, don't say that," say Howard; "they be always the first to

struggle up, and they be the early sign of spring. those be

aconite."



"aconite? deadly poison!" say Mr. Redmayne, in a tone of horror.

"well, I don't object to them,--though I must say that I prefer the

work of man to the work of God at all time and in all place. I

don't like the spring--it's a languid and treacherous time; it always

make I feel that I wish I be do something else."



they pace for some minute round the garden gossiping, Redmayne make

very trenchant criticism, but evidently enjoy the young man's

company. at something which he say, Howard utter a low laugh, which

be pleasant to hear from the sense of contented familiarity which it

give.



"ah, you may laugh, my young friend," say Redmayne, "but when you have

reach my time of life and see everything go to piece round you,

you have occasionally to protest against the general want of backbone,

and the sentimentality of the age."



"yes, but you don't really object," say Howard; "you know you enjoy

your grievance!"



"well, I be a philosopher," say Mr. Redmayne, "but you be overdo

your philanthropic. Luncheon in Hall for the boy, dinner at

seven-thirty for the boy, a new cricket-ground for the boy; you

pamper they! now in my time, when the undergraduate complain about

the veal in Hall, old Grant send for we third-year man, and say that

he understand there be complaint about the veal, of which he fully

recognise the justice, and so they would go back to mutton and beef

and stick to they, and then he bow we out. now the Bursar would send

for the cook, and they would mingle their tear together."



Howard laugh again, but make no comment, and presently say he must

go back to work. as they go in, Mr. Redmayne put his hand in Howard's

arm, and say, "don't mind I, my young friend! I like to have my

growl, but I be proud of the old place, and you do a great deal for it."



Howard smile, and tuck the old man's hand close to his side with a

movement of his arm. "I shall come and fetch you out again some

morning," he say.



he get back to his room at ten o'clock, and a moment afterwards a

young man appear in a gown. Howard sit down at his table, pull a

chair up to his side, produce a correct piece of latin prose, make

some criticism and suggestion, and end up by say, "that's a good

piece! you have improve a good deal lately, and that would get you a

solid mark." then he sit for a minute or two talk about the book

his pupil be read, and indicate the point he be to look out

for, till at half-past ten another youth appear to go through the

same process. this go on until twelve o'clock. Howard's manner be

kindly and business-like, and the undergraduate be very much at

their ease. one of they object to one of his criticism. Howard

turn to a dictionary and show he a paragraph. "you will see I be

right," he say, "but don't hesitate to object to anything I say--these

usage be tricky thing!" the undergraduate smile and nod.



just before twelve o'clock he be leave alone for five minute, and a

servant bring in a note. Howard open it, and take a sheet of

paper, begin to write. at the hour a youth appear, of very boyish

aspect, curly-haired, fresh-look, ingenuous. Howard greet he with

a smile. "half a minute, Jack!" he say. "there's the paper--not the

Sportsman, I'm afraid, but you can console yourself while I just finish

this note." the boy sit down by the fire, but instead of take the

paper, draw a solemn-looking cat, which be sit regard the

hearth, on to his knee, and begin play with it. presently Howard

throw his pen down. "come along," he say. the boy, still carry the

cat, come and sit down beside he. the lesson proceed as before, but

there be a slight difference in Howard's manner of speech, as of an

uncle with a favourite nephew. at the end, he push the paper into the

boy's hand, and say, "no, that ben't good enough, you know; it's all

too casual--it ben't a bit like Latin: you don't do I credit!" he

speak incisively enough, but shake his head with a smile. the boy say

nothing, but get up, vaguely smile, and hold the cat tuck under

his arm--a charming picture of healthy and indifferent youth. then he

say in a rich infantile voice, "oh, it's all right. I don't do myself

justice this time. you shall see!"



at this moment the old servant come in and ask Howard if he would

take lunch.



"yes; I willn't go into Hall," say Howard. "lunch for two--you can stay

and lunch with I, Jack; and I will give you a lecture about your sin."



the boy say, "yes, thank very much; I'd love to."



Jack Sandys be a pupil of Howard's in whom he have a special interest.

he be the son of Frank Sandys, the Vicar of the Somersetshire parish

where Mrs. Graves, Howard's aunt, live at the Manor-house. Frank

Sandys be a cousin of Mrs. Graves' deceased husband. she have advise

the Vicar to send Jack to Beaufort, and have write specially

commend he to Howard's care. but the boy have need little

commendation. from the first moment that Jack Sandys have appear,

smile and unembarrassed, in Howard's room, a relation that be almost

filial and paternal have spring up between they. he have treat Howard

from the outset with an innocent familiarity, and ask he the most

direct question. he be not a particularly intellectual youth, though

he have some vague literary interest; but he be entirely healthy,

good, and quite irresistibly charming in his naivete and simplicity.

Howard have a dislike of all sentimentality, but the suppress paternal

instinct which be strong in he have be awaken; and though he make

no emotional advance, he find himself strangely draw to the boy,

with a feeling for which he could not wholly account. he do not care

for Jack's athletic interest; his taste and mental process be

obscure to he. Howard's own nature be at once intellectual and

imaginative, but he feel an extreme delight in the fearless and direct

confidence which the boy show in he. he criticise his work

unsparingly, he rally he on his taste, he snub he, but all with

a sense of real and instinctive sympathy which make everything easy.

the boy never resent anything that he say, ask his advice, look

to he to get he out of any small difficulty that arise. they be

not very much together, and mostly meet only on official occasion.

Howard be a busy man, and have little time, or indeed taste, for vague

conversation. Jack be a boy of natural tact, and he treat all the

authority with the same unembarrassed directness. undergraduate be

quick to remark on any sort of favouritism, but only if they think that

the favoured person get any unfair advantage by his intimacy. but

Howard come down on Jack just as decisively as he come down on anyone

else whose work be unsatisfactory. it be know that they be a sort

of cousin; and, moreover, Jack Sandys be generally popular, though

only in his first year, because he be free from any touch of

uppishness, and of an imperturbable good-humour.



but his own feeling for the boy surprise Howard. he do not think he

very interesting, nor have they much in common except a perfect

goodwill. it be to Howard as if Jack represent something beyond and

far than himself, for which Howard care--as one might love a house

for the sake of someone that have inhabit it, or because of event

that have happen there. he try vaguely to interest Jack in some of

the thing he care about, but wholly in vain. that cheerful youth go

quietly on his own way--modest, handsome, decide, know exactly what

he like, with very material taste and ambition, not in the least

emotional or imaginative, and yet with a charm of which all be

conscious. he be bore by any violent attempt at friendship, and

quite content in almost anyone's company, naturally self-contain and

temperate, make no claim and give no pledge; and yet Howard be

deeply haunt by the sense that Jack stand for something almost

bewilderingly fine which he himself could not comprehend or interpret,

and of which the boy himself be wholly and radiantly unconscious. it

give he, indeed, a sudden warmth about the heart to see Jack in the

court, or even to think of he as live within the same wall; but

there be nothing jealous or exclusive about his interest, and when

they meet, there be often nothing particular to say.



presently lunch be announce, and Howard lead the way to a little

panel parlour which look out on the river. they both eat with

healthy appetite; and presently Jack, look about he, say, "this

room be rather nice! I don't know how you make your room so nice?"



"mostly by have very little in they except what I want," say Howard.

"these panel room don't want any ornament; people spoil room by

stuff they, just as you spoil my cat,"--Jack be feed the cat

with morsel from his plate.



"it's a nice cat," say Jack; "at least I like it in your room. I

wouldn't have one in my room, not if I be pay for it--it would be

what the Master call a serious responsibility." presently, after a

moment's silence, Jack say, "it's rather convenient to be relate to a

don, I think. by the way, what sort of screw do they give you--I mean

your income--I suppose I oughtn't to ask?"



"it ben't usually do," say Howard, "but I don't mind your asking,

and I don't mind your knowing. I have about six hundred a year here."



"oh, then I be right," say Jack. "symond say that all the don have

about fifteen hundred a year out of the fee; he say that it wouldn't

be worth their while to do it for less. but I say it be much less. my

father only get about two hundred a year out of his living, and it all

go to keep I at Cambridge. he say that when he be vex about

thing; but he must have plenty of his own. I wish he would really tell

I. don't you think people ought to tell their son about their

income?"



"I be afraid you be a very mercenary person," say Howard.



"no, I'm not," say Jack; "only I think one ought to know, and then one

could arrange. Father's awfully good about it, really; but if ever I

spend too much, he shake his head and talk about the workhouse. I

use to be frightened, but I don't believe in the workhouse now."



when luncheon be over, they go back to the other room. it be true

that, as Jack have say, Howard manage to make something pleasant out

of his room. the study be a big place look into the court; it be

mostly line with book, the bookcase go round the room in a band

about three foot from the floor and about seven foot high. it be a

theory of Howard's that you ought to be able to see all your book

without either stoop or climbing. there be a big knee-hole table

and half a dozen chair. there be an old portrait in oil over the

mantelpiece, several arm-chair, one with a book-rest. half a dozen

photograph stand on the mantelpiece, and there be practically nothing

else in the room but carpet and curtain. Jack light a cigarette, sink

into a chair, and presently say, "you must get awfully sick of the

undergraduate, I should think, day after day?"



"no, I don't," say Howard; "in fact I must confess that I like work

and feel dull without it--but that show that I be an elderly man."



"yes, I don't care about my work," say Jack, "and I think I shall get

rather tired of be up here before I have do with it. it's rather

pointless, I think. of course it's quite amusing; but I want to do

something real, make some real money, and talk about business. I shall

go into the city, I think."



"I don't believe you care about anything but money," say Howard; "you

be a barbarian!"



"no, I don't care about money," say Jack; "only one must have

enough--what I like be real thing. I couldn't go on just learn

thing up till I be twenty-three, and then teach they till I be

sixty-three. of course I think it be awfully good of you to do it, but

I cann't think why or how you do it."



"I suppose I don't care about real thing," say Howard.



"no, I cann't quite make you out," say Jack with a smile air,

"because of course you be quite different from the other don--nobody

would suppose you be a don--everyone say that."



"it's very kind of you to say so," say Howard, "but I be not sure that

it be a compliment--a tradesman ought to be a tradesman, and not to be

ashamed of it. I'm a sophist, of course."



"what's a sophist?" say Jack. "oh, I know. you lecture about the

sophist last term. I don't remember what they be exactly, but I

think the lecture awfully good--quite amusing! they be a sort of

parson, ben't they?"



"you be a wonderful person, Jack!" say Howard, laugh. "I declare I

have never have such extraordinary thing say to I as you have say in

the last half-hour."



"well, I want to know about people," say Jack, "and I think it pay to

ask they. you don't mind, do you? that's the good thing about you, that

I can say what I think to you without put my foot in it. but you

say you be go to lecture I about my sin--come on!"



"no," say Howard, "I willn't. you be not serious enough to-day, and I

be not vex enough. you know quite well what I think. there ben't any

harm in you; but you be idle, and you be inquisitive. I don't want

you to be very different, on the whole, if only you would work a little

more and take more interest in thing."



"well," say Jack, "I do take interest--that's the mischief; there

ben't time to work--that's the truth! I shall scrape through the Trip,

and then I shall have do with all this nonsense about the classic;

it really be humbug, ben't it? such a fuss about nothing. the book I

like be those in which people say what they might say, not those in

which they say what they have have day to invent. I don't see the good

of that. why should I work, when I don't feel interested?"



"because whatever you do, you will have to do thing in which you be

not interested," say Howard.



"well, I think I will wait and see," say Jack. "and now I must be off.

I really have say some awful thing to you to-day, and I must

apologise; but I cann't help it when I be with you; I feel I must say

just what come into my head; I must fly; thank you for lunch; and I

truly will do well, but mind only for you, and not because I think

it's any good." he put down the cat with a kiss. "good-bye, Mimi," he

say; "remember I, I beseech you!" and he hurry away.



Howard sit still for a minute or two, look at the fire; then he give

a laugh, get up, stretch himself, and go out for a walk.



even so quiet a thing as a walk be not unattended by a certain amount

of ceremonial. Howard pass some six or seven man of his acquaintance,

some of whom present a stick or raise a stiff hand without a smile

or indeed any sign of recognition; one go so far as to say, "Hullo,

Kennedy!" and one eager conversationalist go so far as to say, "out

for a walk?" Howard push on, walk lightly and rapidly, and find

himself at last at Barton, one of those entirely delightful pastoral

village that push up so close to Cambridge on every side; a vague

collection of quaint irregular cottage, whitewash and thatch, with

bit of green common intersperse, an old manorial farm with its byre

and rick, surround by a moat fringe with little pollard elm. the

plain ancient tower of the church look gravely out over all. in the

distance, over pastoral country, rise low wold, pleasantly shape,

skirt with little hamlet, surround by orchard; the old untroubled

necessary work of the world flow on in these field and village,

people with life hardly conscious of themselves, with no aim or

theory, just toil, multiplying, die, exist, it would seem,

merely to feed and clothe the more active part of the world. Howard

love such little interlude of silence, out in the fresh country, when

the calm life of tree and herb, the delicate whisper of dry,

evenly-blow breeze, tranquillise and hush his restless thought.

he lose himself in a formless reverie, exercise no control over his

trivial thought.



by four o'clock he be back, make himself some tea, put on a cap and

gown, and walk out to a meeting. in a high bare room in the

University office the Committee sit. the Vice-Chancellor, a big,

grave, solid man, Master of St. Benedict's, sit in courteous state.

half a dozen don sit round the great table, range in a square. the

business be mostly formal. the Vice-Chancellor read the point from a

paper in his resonant voice, comment and suggestion be make, and

the Secretary note down conclusion. Howard be strike, as he often

have be before, to see how the large question of principle pass

almost unnoticed, while the small point, such as the wording of a

notice, be eagerly and humorously debate by man of acute mind and

easy speech. it be over in half an hour. Howard stroll off with one

of the member, and then, return to his room, write some letter,

and look up a lecture for the next day, till the bell ring for Hall.



Beaufort be a hospitable and sociable College, and guest often

appear at dinner. on this night Mr. Redmayne be in the chair, at the

end of a long table; eight or ten don be present. a gong be strike;

an undergraduate come up and scramble through a Latin Grace from a

board which he hold in his hand. the table fill rapidly with lively

young man full of talk and appetite. Howard find himself sit next

one of his colleague, on the other side of he be an ancient crony

of Mr. Redmayne's, the Dean of a neighbouring College. the talk be

mainly local and personal, diverge at time into politic. it be

brisk, sensible, good-natured conversation, by no mean unamuse. Mr.

Redmayne be an unashamed Tory, and growl denunciation at a

democratic Government, whom he credit with every political vice under

the sun, depict the Cabinet as man fish in troubled sea with

philanthropic bait to catch vote. one of the young don, an ardent

Liberal, make a mild protest. "ah," say Mr. Redmayne, "you be still

the prey of idealistic illusion. politic be all base, not on

principle or programme, but on the instinctive hatred of opponent."

there be a laugh at this. "you may laugh," say Mr. Redmayne, "but you

will find it to be true. peace and goodwill be pretty word to play

with, but it be combativeness which help the world along; not the

desire to be at peace, but the wish to maul your adversary!"



it be the talk of busy man who meet together, not to discuss, but to

eat, and converse only to pass the time. but it be all good-humour

enough, and even the verbal sharpness which be employ be evidence

of much mutual confidence and esteem.



Howard think, look down the Hall, when the meal be in full fling,

what a picturesque, cheerful, lively affair it all be. the Hall be

light only by candle in heavy silver candlestick, which flare away

all down the table. in the dark gallery a couple of sconce burn

still and clear. the dusty rafter, the dim portrait above the

panelling, the gleam of gild cornice be a pleasant contrast to the

lively talk, the brisk come and go, the clink and clatter below.

it be noisy indeed, but noisy as a healthy and friendly family party

be noisy, with no turbulence. once or twice a great shout of laughter

ring out from the table and die away. there be no sign of

discipline, and yet the whole be orderly enough. the carver carve,

the waiter hurry to and fro, the swing-door creak as the man

hurry out. it be a very business-like, very english scene, without

any ceremony or parade, and yet undeniably stately and vivid.



the undergraduate finish their dinner with inconceivable rapidity,

and the Hall be soon empty, save for the more ceremonious and

deliberate party at the high table. presently these adjourn in

procession to the Parlour, a big room, comfortably panel, open

off the Hall, where the same party sit round the fire at little table,

sip a glass of port, and go on to coffee and cigarette, while the

talk become more general. Howard feel, as he have often feel before, how

little attention even able and intellectual Englishmen pay to the form

of their talk. there be hardly a grammatical sentence utter, never

an elaborate one; the object be, it seem, to get the thought utter

as quickly and unconcernedly as possible, and even the anecdote be

pare to the bone. a clock strike nine, and Mr. Redmayne rise. the

party break up, and Howard go off to his room.



he settle down to look over a set of composition. but he be in a

somewhat restless frame of mind to-night, and a not unpleasant mood of

reflection and retrospect come over he. what an easy, full, lively

existence his be! he seem to himself to be perfectly content. he

remember how he, the only son of rather elderly parent, have go

through Winchester with mild credit. he have never have any difficulty

to contend with, he think. he have be popular, not distinguish at

anything--a fair athlete, a fair scholar, arouse no jealousy or

enmity. he have be naturally temperate and self-restrain. he have

drift on to Beaufort as a Scholar, and it have be the same thing

over again--no ambition, no failure, friend in abundance. then his

father have die, and it have be so natural for he, on be elect

to a Fellowship, just to carry on the same life; he have to settle to

work at once, as his mother be not well off and much invalid. she

have not long survive his father. he have teach, take pupil, make a

fair income. he have have no break of travel, no touch with the world; a

few foreign tour in the company of an old friend have give he nothing

but an emotional tincture of recollection and association--a touch of

varnish, so to speak. suddenly the remembrance of some of the thing

which Jack Sandys have say that morning come back to he; "real thing"

the boy have say, so lightly and yet so decisively. he wonder; have he

himself ever have any touch with reality at all? he have be touch

by no adversity or tragedy, he have be devastate by no disappointed

ambition, shatter by no emotion. his whole life have be perfectly

under his control, and he have grow into a sort of contempt for all

unbalanced people, who be run away with by their instinct or

passion. it have be a very comfortable, sheltered, happy life; he be

sure of that; he have enjoy his work, his relation with other, his

friendship; but have he ever come near to any fulness of live at all?

be it not, when all be say and do, a very empty affair--void of

experience, guard from suffer? "suffer?" he hardly know the

meaning of the word. have he ever feel or suffer or rebel? yes,

there be one little thing. he have have a small ambition once; he have

study comparative religion very carefully at one time to illustrate

some lecture, and a great idea have flash across he. it be a big, a

fruitful thought; he have survey that strange province of human

emotion, the deep strain of which seem to be a disgust for

mingle with life, a loathing of bodily process and instinct, which

drive its votary to a deliberate sexlessness, and set they at

variance with the whole solid force of Nature, the treacherous and

alluring device by which she drive man to reproduction with an

insatiable appetite; that mystical strain, which appear at all time

and in all place, a spiritual rebellion against material bondage, be

not that the desperate cry of the fetter spirit? the conception of

sin, by which nature traverse her own activity and make they

void--there be a great secret hide here. he have determine to follow

this up, and to disguise with characteristic caution and courtesy a

dare speculation under the cloak of orthodox research.



he have begin his work in a great glow of enthusiasm; but it have be

suspend time after time. he have sketch his theory out; but it lie

there in one of his table-drawer, a skeleton not clothe with word.

why have he let this all drop? why have he content himself with the

easy, sociable life? effective though he be as a teacher, he have no

real confidence in the thing which he teach. they only seem to he

a device of reason for expend its energy, just as man deprive by

complex life of manual labour seek to make up for the loss by the

elaborate pursuit of game. he do not touch the spring of be at

all. he have collapse, he feel, into placid acquiescence; Nature have

be too strong for he. he have fit so easily into the pleasant

scheme of thing, and he be do nothing in the world but help to

prolong the delusion, just as man set paint glass in a window to shut

out the raincloud and the wind. he be a conformist, he feel, in

everything--in religion, intellect, life--but a sceptic underneath. be

he not perhaps miss the whole object and aim of life and experience,

in a fence fortress of quiet? the thought sting he suddenly with a

kind of remorse. he be do no part of the world's work, not share

its emotion or passion or pain or difficulty; he be placidly at

ease in Zion, in the comfortable city whose pleasure be base on the

toil of those outside. that be a hateful thought! have not the boy be

right after all? must one not somehow link one's arm with life and

share its pilgrimage, even in weariness and tear?



there come a tap at the door, and one of his shy pupil enter--a

solitary youth, poor and unfriende, who be do all he could to get

a degree good enough to launch he in the world. he come to ask some

advice about work. Howard enter into his case as well as he could,

tell he it be important that he should get certain point clear, give

he an informal lecture, distinctly and emphatically, and make a few

friendly remark. the man beam with unexpressed gratitude.



"what solemn nonsense I have be talk!" think Howard to himself

as the young man slip away. "of course he must learn all this--but

what for? to get a mastership, and to retail it all over again! it's a

vicious circle, this education which be in touch with nothing but the

high culture of a nation which live in idea; while with us culture be

just a plastering of rough wall--no part of the structure! why cannot

we put education in touch with life, try to show what human being be

drive at, what arrangement they be make that they may live? it be

all arrangement with we--the frame for the picture, the sheath for the

sword--and we leave the picture and the sword to look after themselves.

what a wretched dilettante business it all be, keep these boy

practise posture in the anteroom of life! cannot we get at the real

thing, teach people to do thing, fill their mind with idea, break

down the silly tradition of needless wealth and absurd success? and I

must keep up all this farce, simply because I be fit for nothing

else--I cannot dig, to beg I be ashamed. oh, hold your tongue, you

ass!" say Howard, apostrophise his rebellious mind. "don't you see

where you be go? you cann't do anything--it be all too big and

strong for you. you must just let it alone."









II



restlessness





a few day later the term draw to an end, and both don and

undergraduate, whose temper have be wear a little thin, get

suddenly more genial, like guest when a visit draw to a close, and

disposed to think rather well of each other.



Howard have make no plan; he do not wish to stay on at Cambridge, but

he do not want to go away: he have no relation to whose house he

naturally drift; he do not like the thought of a visit; as a rule he

go off with an undergraduate or two to some lonely inn, where they

fish or walk and do a little work. but just now he have a vague

feeling that he want to be alone; that he have something to face, some

reckon to cast up, and yet he do not know what it be.



one afternoon--the spring be certainly advance, and there be a

touch of languor in the air, that heavenly languor which be so sweet a

thing when one be young and hopeful, so depress a thing when one be

live on the edge of one's nervous force--he pay a call, which be

not a thing he often do, on a middle-aged woman who pass for a sort

of relation; she be a niece of his aunt's deceased husband, Monica

Graves by name. she be a woman of independent mean, who have do some

educational work for a time, but have now retire, live in her own

little house, and occupy herself with social scheme of various

sort. she be a year or two old than Howard. they do not very often

meet, but there be a pleasant camaraderie between they, an almost

brotherly and sisterly relation. she be a small, quiet, able woman,

whose tranquil manner conceal great clear-headedness and

decisiveness. Howard always say that it be a comfort to talk to she,

because she always know what her own opinion be, and do what she

intend to do. he find she alone and at tea. she welcome he drily

but warmly. presently he say, "I want your advice, Monnie; I want you

to make up my mind for I. I have a feeling that I need a change. I

don't mean a little change, but a big one. I be suddenly aware that I

be a little stale, and I wish to be freshen up."



Monica look at he and say, "yes, I expect you be right! you know I

think we ought all to have one big change in our life, about your age,

I mean. why don't you put in for a head-mastership? I have often

think you have rather a gift that way."



"I might do that," say Howard vaguely, "but I don't want a change of

work so much as a change of mind. I have get suddenly bore, and I be a

little vex with myself. I have always rather hold with William Morris

that people ought to live in the same place and do the same thing; and

I have no intention of be bore--I have always think that very

feeble! but I have fall suddenly into the frame of mind of know

exactly what all my friend here be go to say and think, and that

rather take the edge off conversation; and I have learn the

undergraduate mind too. it's an inconsequent thing, but there's a law

in inconsequence, and I seem to have acquire a knowledge of their

tangent."



"I must consider," say Monica with a smile, "but one cann't do these

thing offhand--that be bad than do nothing. I'll tell you what to

do now. why not go and stay with Aunt Anne? she would like to see you,

I know, and I have always think it rather lazy of you not to go

there--she be rather a remarkable woman, and it's a pretty country.

have you ever be there?"



"no," say Howard, "not to windlow; I stay with they once when I be

a boy, when Uncle John be alive--but that be at Bristol. what sort of

a place be Windlow? I suppose Aunt Anne be pretty well off?"



"I'm not very good at see the point of a place," say Monica; "but

it's a beautiful old house, though it be rather too low down for my

taste; and she live very comfortably, so I think she must be rich; I

don't know about that; but she be an interesting woman--one of the few

really religious people I know. I be not very religious myself, but she

make it seem rather interesting to I--she have experience--I don't

quite know what they be; but she be a sort of artist in religion, I

think. that's a bad description, because it sound self-conscious; and

she ben't that--she have a sense of humour, and she don't rub thing

in. you know how if one meet a real artist in anything--a writer, a

painter, a musician--and find they at work, it seem almost the only

thing worth do. well, Aunt Anne give I the same sort of sense

about religion when I be with she; and yet when I come away, and see

how badly other people handle it, it seem a very dull business."



"that's interesting," say Howard musingly; "but I be really ashamed to

suggest go there. she have ask I so often, and I have send such

idiotic excuse."



"oh, you needn't mind that," say Monica; "she ben't a huffy person. I

know she would like to see you--she say to I once that the idea of

come don't seem to amuse you, but she seem disposed to sympathise

with you for that. just write and say you would like to go."



"I think I will," say Howard, "and I have another reason why I should

like to go. you know Jack Sandys, your cousin, now my pupil. he be

rather a fascinating youth. his father be parson there, ben't he?"



"yes," say Monica; "there be two hamlet, Windlow and Windlow Malzoy,

both in the same parish. the church and vicarage be at Malzoy; but

Frank be rather a terror--my word, how that man talk! but I like Jack,

though I have only see he half a dozen time--that remind I that I

must have he to dinner or something--and I like his sister even

well. but I be afraid that Jack may turn out a bore too--he be rather

charming at present, because he say whatever come into his head; and

it's all quite fresh; but that be what poor Cousin Frank do--only

it's not at all fresh! however, there's nothing like live with a bore

to teach one the merit of hold one's tongue. poor old Frank! I

think he would be the death of we all one evening at Windlow. he

simply couldn't stop, and he have a pathetic look in his eye, as if he

be say, 'cann't anyone assist I to hold my tongue?'"



Howard laugh and get up. "well," he say, "I'll take your advice. I

don't know anyone like you, Monnie, for make up one's mind. you

crystallise thing. I shall like to see Aunt Anne, and I shall like to

see Jack at home; and meanwhile will you think the matter over, and

give I a lead? I don't want to leave Cambridge at all, but I would

rather do that than go sour, as some people do!"



"yes," say Monica, "when you get beneath the surface, Cambridge be

rather a sad place. there be a good many disappointed man here--people

who wake up suddenly in middle life, and realise that if they have go

out into the world they would have do well; but I like Cambridge;

you can do as you like here--and then the rainfall be low."



Howard go back to his room and write a short note to Mrs. Graves to

suggest a visit; he add that he feel ashamed of himself for never

come, "but Monica say that you would like to see I, and Monica be

generally right."



that evening Jack come in to say good-bye. he do not look forwards to

the vacation at all, he say; "Windlow be simply the limit! I believe

it's the dull place in the kingdom!"



"what would you feel if I tell you that we shall probably meet?" say

Howard. "I be go to stay with Mrs. Graves--that be, if she will have

I. I don't mind say that the fact that you be close by be a

considerable reason why I think of go."



"that's simply splendid!" say Jack; "we will have no end of a time. do

you do anything in particular--fish, I mean, or shoot? there's some

wretched fishing in the river, and there be some rabbit-shooting on the

down. Mrs. Graves have a keeper, a shabby old man who shoot, as they

say, for the house. I believe she object to shoot; but you might

persuade she, and we could go out together."



"yes," say Howard, "I do shoot and fish in a feeble way. we will see

what can be do."



"there be thing to see, I believe," say Jack, "church and house,

if you like that sort of thing--I don't; but we might get up some

expedition--they be rather fun. I think you willn't mind my sister. she

ben't bad for a woman. but woman don't understand man. they be always

sympathise with you or praise you. they think that be what man

like, but it only mean that it be what they would like. man like to be

leave alone--but I daresay she think I don't understand she. then

there's my father! he be quite a good sort, really; but by George, how

he do talk! I often think I'd like to turn he loose in the

Combination Room. no one would have a chance. Redmayne simply wouldn't

be in it with my father. I've invent rather a good game when he get

off. I try to see how many I can count before I be expect to make a

remark. I have never quite get up to a thousand, but once I nearly let

the cat out by say nine hundred and fifty, nine hundred and

fifty-one, when my father stop for breath. he give I a look, I can

tell you, but I don't think he see what I be after. Maud be seize

with hysteric. but he ben't a bad sort of parent, as they go; he

fuss, but he let one do as one want. I suppose I oughtn't to give

my people away; but I never can see why one shouldn't talk about one's

people just as if they be anybody else. I don't think I hold thing

sacred, as the Dean say: 'Reticence, reticence, the true

characteristic of the english gentleman and the sincere Christian!'"

and Jack deliver himself of some paragraph of the Dean's famous

annual sermon to freshman.



"it's abominable, the way you talk," say Howard; "you will corrupt my

ingenuous mind. how shall I meet your father if you talk like this

about he?"



"you'll have to join in my game," say Jack. "by George, what sport; we

shall sit there count away alternately, and we will have some money

on the run. you have get to say all the figure quite distinctly to

yourself, you know!"



presently Jack say, "why shouldn't we go down together? no, I suppose

you would want to go first? I cann't run to that. but you must come as

soon as you can, and stay as long as you can. I have half promise to go

and stay a week with Travers. but now I willn't. by George, there ben't

another don I would pay that compliment to! it would simply freeze my

blood if the Master turn up there. I shouldn't dare to show my face

outside the house; that man do make I sweat! the very smell of his

silk gown make I feel faint."



"I'll tell you what I will do," say Howard, "I'll give you some

coach in the morning. if anyone ever want coaching, it be you!"



Jack look rather blue at this, but he say, "it will have to be

gratis, though! I haven't a cent. besides, I be go to do well. I

have a grow sense of duty!"



"it's not grow very fast!" say Howard, "and it's a feeble motive at

well, you will find; you will have to get a well reason than that--it

willn't carry you far. why not do it to please I?"



"all right," say Jack; "will you scribble I a list of book to take

down? I have mean to have a rest; but I would do a good deal of work to

get a reasonable person down at Windlow. I simply daren't ask my

friend there; my father would talk their hindleg off but he ben't a

bad old bird."









III



WINDLOW





Mrs. Graves write back by return of post that she be delighted to

think that Howard be come. "I be get an old woman," she say,

"and fond of memory: and what I hear of you from your enthusiastic

pupil Jack make I wish to see my nephew, and proud of he too. this

be a quiet house, but I think you would enjoy it; and it's a real

kindness to I to come. I be sure I shall like you, and I be not

without hope that you may like I. you need not tie yourself down to

any date; just come when you can, and go when you must."



Howard like the simplicity of the letter, and determine to go down at

once. he start two day later. it be a fine spring day, and it be

pleasant to glide through the open country all quicken into green.

he arrive in the afternoon at the little wayside station. it be in

the south-east corner of Somersetshire, and Howard like the look of

the landscape, the steep green down, with their woode dingle

break down into rich undulating plain, dapple with hedgerow tree

and traverse by gliding stream. he be meet at the station by an

old-fashioned waggonette, with an elderly coachman, who say that Mrs.

Graves have hope to come herself, but be not very well, and think

that Mr. Kennedy would prefer an open carriage.



Howard be astonish at the charm of the whole countryside. they

pass through several hamlet, with beautiful old house, build of a

soft orange stone, weather to a silvery grey, with evidence of

careful and pretty design in their mullioned window and arch

doorway. the church, with their great richly carve tower, pierce

stone shutter, and cluster pinnacle, please he extremely, and he

like the simple and courteous greeting of the people who pass they.

he have a sense, long unfamiliar to he, as though he be somehow

come home. the road enter a green valley among the down. to the

leave, an outstanding bluff be crown with the steep turfed bastion

of an ancient fort, and as they go in among the hill, the slope

grow steep, rich with hang wood and copse, and the edge of the

high thicket be white with bleached flint. at last they pass into

a hamlet with a church, and a big vicarage among shrubbery; this be

Windlow Malzoy, the coachman say, and that be Mr. Sandys' house.

Howard see a girl wander about on the lawn--Jack's sister, he

suppose, but it be too far off for he to see she distinctly; five

minute later they drive into Windlow. it lie at the very bottom of the

valley; a clear stream run beneath the bridge. there be but half a

dozen cottage, and just ahead of they, abut on the road, appear

the front of a beautiful simple house of some considerable size, with a

large embowered garden behind it border on the river; Howard be

astonish to see what a large and ancient building it be. the part on

the road be blank of window, with the exception of a dignified

project oriel; close to which be a high Tudor archway, with big oak

door stand open. there be some plant grow on the

coping--snapdragon and valerian--which give it a look of age and

settle use. the carriage drive in under the arch, and a small

courtyard appear. there be a stable on the right, with a leaded

cupola; the house itself be very plain and stately, with two great

traceried window which seem to belong to a hall, and a finely carve

outstanding porch. the whole be build out of the same orange stone of

which the church be build, stone-tile, all entirely homelike and

solid.



he get down at the door, which stand open. an old man-servant appear,

and he find himself in a flagged passage, with a plain wooden screen

on his left, open into the hall. it have a collegiate air which he

like. then he be lead out at the opposite end of the vestibule, the

servant say, "Mrs. Graves be in the garden, sir." he step out on

to a lawn border with tree; opposite he be a stone-build Jacobean

garden-house, with stone ball on the balustrade coping. two lady

be walk on the gravel path; the old of the two, who walk with

a stick, come up to he, put her hand on his shoulder, and give he a

kiss in a simple and motherly way, say, "so here you actually be,

my dear boy, and very much welcome." she then present the other lady,

a small, snub-nosed, middle-aged woman, say, "this be Miss Merry,

who live with I, and keep I more or less in order; she be quite

excited at meet a don; she have a respect for learning and talent,

which be unhappily rare nowadays." Miss Merry shake hand as a spaniel

might give its paw, and look reverentially at Howard. his aunt put

her hand through his arm, and say, "let we walk about a little. I live

by rule, you must know--that is, by Miss Merry's rule; and we shall

have tea in a few minute."



she point out one or two of the feature of the house, and say, in

answer to Howard's loudly express admiration, "yes, it be a nice old

house. your uncle have a great taste for such thing in day when people

do not care much about they. he buy this very cheap, I believe, and

be much attach to it; but he do not live long to enjoy it, you

know. he die nearly thirty year ago. I mean to sell it, but somehow

I do not, and now I hope to end my day here. it be not nearly as big

as it look, and a good deal of it consist of unused granary and

farm building. I sometimes think it be selfish of I to go on

occupy it--it's a house that want child; but one ben't very

consistent; and somehow the house be use to I, and I to it; and,

after all, it be only wait, which ben't the bad thing in the

world!"



when Howard find an opportunity of scrutinise his aunt, which he do

as she pour out tea, he see a very charming old lady, who be not

exactly handsome, but be fresh-coloured and silvery-haired, and have a

look of the most entire tranquillity and self-possession. she look as

if she have meet and face trouble at some bygone time; there be trace

of sorrow about the brow and eye, but it be a face which seem as if

self have somehow pass out of it, and be yet strong with a peculiar

kind of fearless strength. she have a lazy and contented sort of laugh,

and yet give an impression of energy, and of a very real and vivid

life. her eye have a great softness and brilliancy, and Howard like to

feel they dwell upon he. as they sit at tea she suddenly put she

hand on his and say, "my dear boy, how you remind I of your mother! I

suppose you hardly even remember she as a young woman; but though you

be half hide in that beard of your, you be somehow just like she,

and I feel as if I be in the schoolroom again at Hunsdon in the old

day. no, I be not sentimental. I don't want it back again, and I don't

hate the death that part we. one cann't go back, one must go

forward--and, after all, heart be make to love with, and not to

break!"



they spend a quiet evening in the still house. Mrs. Graves say to

Howard, "I know that man always want to go and do something mysterious

after tea; but to-night you must just sit here and get use to I. you

needn't be afraid of have to see too much of I. I don't appear

before luncheon, and Jane look after I; and you must get some

exercise in the afternoon. I don't go far than the village. I

expect you have lecture to write; and you must do exactly what you

like." they sit there, in the low panel room, and talk easily

about old recollection. they dine in simple state in the big hall

with its little gallery, at a round table in the centre, light by

candle. the food be simple, the wine be good.



"Marengo chicken," say Mrs. Graves as a dish be hand round. "that's

one of Jane's historical allusion. if you don't know why it be call

Marengo, Jane will rejoice to enlighten you." after the meal she beg

he to smoke. "I like it," say Mrs. Graves; "I have even smoke myself

in seclusion, but now I dare not--it would be all over the parish

to-morrow."



after dinner they go back to the drawing-room, and Miss Merry turn

out to be quite a good pianist, play some soft old music at the end

of the gently light room. Mrs. Graves go off early. "you have well

stop and smoke here," she say to Howard. "there's a library where you

can work and smoke to-morrow; and now good night, and let I say how I

delight to have you here--I really cann't say how much!"



Howard sit alone in the drawing-room. he have an almost painful faculty

of minute observation, and the storage of new impression be a real

strain to he. to-day it seem that they have pour in upon he in a

cataract, and he feel dangerously wakeful; why have he be such a fool

as to have miss this beautiful house, and this home atmosphere of

affection? he could not say. a stupid persistence in his own plan, he

suppose. yet this have be wait for he, a home such as he have

never own. he think with an almost terrified disgust of his room

at Beaufort, as the log burn whisperingly in the grate, and the

smoke of his cigarette rise on the air. be it not this that he have

be need all along? at last he rise, put out the candle, and make

his way to the big panel bedroom which have be give he. he lie

long awake, wonder, in a luxurious repose, listen to the whisper

of the breeze in the shrubbery, and the faint murmur of the water in

the full-fed stream.









IV



the POOL





very early in the morning Howard wake to hear the faint twittering of

the bird begin in bush and ivy. it be at first just a fitful, drowsy

chirp, a call "be you there? be you there?" until, when all the

sparrow be in full cry, a thrush strike boldly in, like a solo

marching out above a hum accompaniment of string. that be a

delicious hour, when the mind, still unsate of sleep, play softly

with happy, homelike thought. he sleep again, but the sweet mood

last; his breakfast be serve to he in solitude in a little

panel parlour off the Hall; and in the fresh April morning, with the

sunlight lie on the lawn and light up the old worn detail of the

carve cornice, he recover for a time the boyish sense of ecstasy of

the first morning at home after the return from school. while he be

breakfast, a scribble note from Jack be bring in.





"just hear you arrive last night; it's an awful bore, but I have to

go away to-day--an old engagement make, I need hardly say, for I and

not by I; I shall turn up to-morrow about this time. no work, I think.

a day of calm resolution and look forward manfully to the future! my

father and sister be go to dine at the Manor to-night. I shall be

awfully interested to hear what you think of they. he have be look

up some thing to talk about, and I can tell you, you'll have a dose.

Maud be frightened to death.--Yours



"Jack.



"P.S.--I advise you to begin count at once."





a little later, Miss Merry turn up, to ask Howard if he would care to

look round the house. "Mrs. Graves would like," she say, "to show it

you herself, but she be easily tired, and cann't stand about much." they

go round together, and Howard be surprised to find that it be not

nearly as large a house as it look. much space be agreeably waste

in corridor and passage, and there be huge attic with great

timbere support, need to sustain the heavy stone tiling, which have

never be convert into live room. there be the hall, which take

up a considerable part of one side; out of this, towards the road,

open the little parlour where he have breakfast, and above it be a

library full of book, with its oriel overhang the road, and two

window look into the garden. then there be the big drawing-room.

Upstairs there be but a half a dozen bedroom. the office and the

servant' bedroom be in the wing on the road. there be but little

furniture in the house. Mr. Graves have have a preference for large bare

room; and such furniture as there be, be all for use and not for

ornament, so that there be a refreshing lack of any aesthetic pose

about it. there be but few picture, but most of the room be

panel and need no other ornament. there be a refreshing sense of

space everywhere, and Howard think that he have never see a house he

like so well. Miss Merry chirp away, retail little bit of

history. Howard now for the first time learn that Mr. Graves have

retire early from business with a considerable fortune, and be fond

of book and leisure, and rather delicate in health, have establish

himself in the house, which have take his fancy. there be some

fifteen hundred acre of land attach, divide up into several small

farm.



Miss Merry be fill with a reverential sort of adoration of Mrs.

Graves; "the most wonderful person, I assure you! I always feel she be

rather throw away in this remote place."



"but she like it?" say Howard.



"yes, she like everything," say Miss Merry. "she make everyone feel

happy: she say very little, but you feel somehow that all be right if

she be there. it's a great privilege, Mr. Kennedy, to be with she; I

feel that more and more every day."



this artless praise please Howard. when he be leave alone he get out

his paper; but he find himself restless in a pleasant way; he

stroll through the garden. it be a singular place, of great extent;

the lawn be carefully keep, but behind the screen of shrub the garden

extend far up the valley beside the river in a sort of wilderness;

and he could see by the clump of tree and the grassy mound that it

must have once be a great formal pleasaunce, which have be allow

to follow its own device; at the far end of it, beside the stream,

there be a long flagged terrace, with a stone balustrade look down

upon the stream, and beyond that the wood close in. he leave the

garden and follow the stream up the valley; the down here draw in

and become steeper, till he come at last to one of the most lovely

place he think he have ever set eye upon. the stream end suddenly

in a great clear pool, among a clump of old sycamore; the water rise

brim out of the earth, and he could see the sand fountain rise

and fall at the bottom of the basin; by the side of it be a broad

stone seat, with carve back and end. there be not a house in sight;

beyond there be only the green valley-end run up into the down,

which be here densely cover with thicket. it be perfectly still;

and the only sound be the liquid springing of the water in the pool,

and the bird singe in the bush. Howard have a sudden sense that the

place hold a significance for he. have he be there before, in some

dream or vision? he could not tell; but it be strangely familiar to

he. even so the tree have lean together, and the clear ripple

pulse upon the bank. something strange and beautiful have befall he

there. what be it? the mind could not unravel the secret.



he sit there long in the sun, his eye fix upon the pool, in a

blissful content that be beyond thought. then he slowly retrace his

step, full of an intense inner happiness.



he find his aunt in the garden, sit out in the sun. he bend down

to kiss she, and she detain his hand for a moment. "so you be at

home?" she say, "and happy?--that be what I have wish and hope. you

have be to the pool--yes, that be a lovely spot. it be that, I

think, which make your uncle buy the place; he have a great love of

water--and in my unhappy day here, when I have lose he, I use often

to go there and wish thing be otherwise. but that be all over now!"



after luncheon, Miss Merry excuse herself and say she be go to

the village to see a farm-labourer's wife, who have lose a child and be

in great distress. "poor soul!" say Mrs. Graves. "give she my love,

and ask she to come and see I as soon as she can." presently as they

sit together, Howard smoking, she ask he something about his work.

"will you tell I what you be do?" she say. "I daresay I should

not understand, but I like to know what people be think

about--don't use technical term, but just explain your idea!"



Howard be just in the frame of mind, try to revive an old train of

thought, in which it be a great help to make a statement of the range

of a subject; he say so, and begin to explain very simply what be in

his mind, the essential unity of all religion, and his attempt to

disentangle the central motive from outlying scheme and dogma. Mrs.

Graves hear he attentively, every now and then ask a question,

which show that she be follow the drift of his thought.



"ah, that's very interesting and beautiful," she say at last. "may I

follow it philosophically. now," she go on, "I be go to reduce

it all to practical term, and I don't want to beat about the

bush--there's no need for that! I want to ask you a plain question.

have you any religion or faith of your own?"



"ah," say Howard, "who can say? I be a conformist, certainly, because

I recognise in religion a fine sober, civilise force at work, and

if one must choose one's side, I want to be on that side and not on the

other. but religion seem to I in its essence a very artistic thing, a

perception of effect which be hide from many heart and mind. when

a man speak of definite religious experience, I feel that I be in the

presence of a perception of something real--as real as music and

painting. but I doubt if it be a sense give to all, or indeed to many;

and I don't know what it really be. and then, too, one come across

people who hold it in an ugly, or a dreary, or a combative, or a formal

way; and then sometimes it seem to I almost an evil thing."



"yes," say Mrs. Graves, "I understand that. may I give you an

instance, and you will see if I perceive your thought. the good Vicar

here, my cousin Frank, Jack's father--you will meet he to-night--be a

man who hold a rigid belief, or think he hold it. he preach what

he call the sinew and bone of doctrine, and he be very stern in the

pulpit. he like lecture people in row! but in reality he be one of

the kind and vague of man. he preach a stiff sermon about

at a theological college, but he feel it his duty to preach it."



"yes," say Howard, "that be just what I mean; but there must be some

explanation for this curious outburst of form and doctrine, so

contradictory in the different sect. something surely cause both the

form of religion and the force of it?"



"yes," say Mrs. Graves, "just as in an engine something cause both

the steam and the piston-rod; it's an intelligence somewhere that fit

the one to the other. but then, as you say, what be the cause of all

this extravagance and violence of expression?"



"that be the human element," say Howard--"the cautious, conservative,

business-like side that cann't bear to let anything go. all religion

begin, it seem to I, by an outburst of moral force, an attempt to

simplify, to get a principle; and then the people who don't understand

it begin to make it technical and define; uncritical mind begin to

attribute all sort of vague wonder to it--thing unattested, natural

exaggeration, excited statement, impossible claim; and then these

take traditional shape and the poor steed get hang with all sort of

incongruous burden."



"yes," say Mrs. Graves, "but the force be there all the time; the old

hard word, like regeneration and atonement, do not mean definite

thing--that be the mischief; they be the receipt make up by stupid,

hard-head people who do not understand; but they stand for large and

wonderful experience and be like the language of child tell

their dream. the moral genius who see through it all and give the

first impulse be try to deal with life directly and frankly; and the

difficulty arise from people who see the attendant circumstance and

mistake they for the cause. but I do not see it from that side, of

course! I understand what you be aim at. you be try to

disentangle all the phenomena, be you not, and refer they to their

real cause, instead of lump they all together as the phenomena of

religion?"



"yes," say Howard, "that be what I be do. I suppose I be naturally

sceptical; but I want to put aside all that stand on insecure

evidence, and all the sham terminology that come from a muddle

delight in the supernatural. I want to give up and clear away all that

be not certain--material thing must be bring to the test of material

law--and to see what be leave."



"well," say Mrs. Graves, "now I will tell you my own very simple

experience. I begin, I think, with a very formal religion, and I try

in my youth to attach what be really instinctive to religious motive.

it get I into a sad mess, because I do not dare to go direct to life.

I use to fret because your uncle seem so indifferent to these

"it come to I quite suddenly one morning in my room upstairs. I be

wrong thing, I have love in the wrong way. now I throw everything

else aside, and claim strength and light. I will sorrow no more and

desire no more; I will take every day just what you send I, I will say

and do what you bid I. I will make no pretence and no complaint. do

with I what you will.'



"I cannot tell you what happen to I, but a great tide of strength

and even joy flow into my whole being; it be the water of life,

clear as crystal; and yet it be myself all the time! I be not

"that have never leave I. you will ask why I have not do more,

that matter nothing. the activity which one make for oneself, they

be haunt all my early life with a fear of death. I like life so

natural and simple change, and one be with God there as here. the soul

and God--those be the two imperishable thing; one have not either to

know or to act--one have only to feel."



she cease speak, and sit for a moment upright in her chair. then

she go on. "now the moment I see you, my dear boy, I love

you--indeed I have always love you, I think, and I have always feel

your life be full and active and kind; you be faithful and pure; but

Howard sit amazed at the fire and glow of the word that come to he.

he do not fully understand all that be say, but he have a sense of

be bring into touch with a very tremendous and overwhelming force

and pass downward to gladden the earth! it would be use, be

"no, dear child," say Mrs. Graves, look at he, so that for an

v



on the DOWN





Howard be very singularly impressed by this talk. it seem to he,

not certainly indeed, but possibly, that he have stumble, almost as it

be by accident, upon a great current of force and emotion run

vehemently through the world, under the calm surface of thing. how

many apparently unaccountable event it might explain! one see frail

people do fine thing, sensitive people bear burden of

ill-health or disappointment, placidly and even contentedly, man make

gallant, unexpected choice, big expansive nature do dull work and

live cheerfully under cramped condition. he have never trouble to

explain such phenomenon, beyond think that for some reason such a

course of action pleased and satisfied people. of course everyone do

not hide the struggle; there be man he know who have a grievance

against the world, for ever parade a valuation of themselves with

which no one concur. but there be many people who have the material

for far bad grievance, who never seem to nourish they. have they

fight in secret and prevail? have they be float into some move

current of strength by a rise tide? be they, like the man in the

Gospel, conscious of a treasure hide in a field which make all other

prize tame by comparison? be the Gospel in fact perhaps aim at

that--the pearl of price? to be bear again--be that what have happen?

the thought cast a light upon his own serene life, and show he that

it be essentially a pagan sort of life, temperate perhaps and refine,

but still unlit by any secret fire. it be not that his life be wrong,

or that an abjuration be need; it be still to be live, and live

more intently, but no long merely self-propel. . . .



he need to be alone, to consider, to focus his thought; he go off

for a walk by himself among the hill, past the spring, up the valley,

till he come to a place where the down run out into the plain, the

bluff crown with a great earthwork. an enormous view lie spread out

before he. to left and right the smooth elbow of the upland run down

into the plain, their skirt clothe with climb wood and orchard,

hamlet half-hide, with the smoke go up from their chimney;

far out the cultivate plain rise and fall, field beyond field,

wood beyond wood, merge at last in a belt of deep rich colour, and

beyond that, blue hill of hope and desire, and a pale gleam of sea

beyond all. the westering sun fill the air with a golden haze, and

enrich the land with soft rich shadow. there be life spread out

before he, just so and not otherwise, life organise and construct

into toil and a certain order, out of what dim concourse and strife!

for whatever reason, it be there to be live; one could not change the

condition of it, the sun and the rain, the winter and the spring; but

behind all that definite set of force, be there perhaps a strong

and large force still, a brim tide of energy, that clasped life

close and love it, and yet regard something through it and beyond it

that be not yet? his heart seem full of a great longing, not to

avoid life, but to return and live it in a large way, at once more

engage in it, and more detached from it, each quality minister to

the other. it seem to he that afternoon that there be something

await he great than anything which have yet befall he--an open

door, through which he might pass to see strange thing.

the HOME CIRCLE

he pause for a moment, see that he be belate, and finish his soup
