be go on all the time, like the music and the light. it haven't

be waste. I have have a wonderful talk with she to-day--the most

wonderful talk, I think, I have ever have. I cann't understand it all

yet--but she have give I the sense of some fine purpose--as if I have

be keep away for a purpose, because I be not ready; and as if I have

come here for a purpose now."



the girl sate look at he with open eye, and with some strange

sense of surprise. "yes," she say, "it be just like that; but that you

could have see it so soon amaze I. I have know she all my life, and

could never have put that into word. do you know how thing seem to

come and go and shift about without any meaning? it be never so with

she; she see what it all mean. I cannot explain it."



they sate in silence for a moment, and then Howard say: "it be very

curious to be here; you know, or probably you don't know, how much

interested I be in Jack; and somehow in talk to he I feel that

there be something behind--something more to know. all this"--he wave

his hand at the room--"my aunt, your father, yourself--it do not seem

to I new and unfamiliar, but something which I have always know. I

cann't tell you in what a dream I have seem to be move ever since I

come here. I have be here for twenty-four hour, and yet it seem all

old and dear to I."



"I know that feeling," say the girl, "one dip into something that have

be go on for ever and ever--I feel like that to-night. it seem

odd to talk like this, but you must remember that Jack tell I most

thing, and I seem to know you quite well. I know it would be all easy

somehow."



"well, we be a sort of cousin," say Howard lightly. "that's such a

comfort; it needn't entail anything, but it can save one all sort of

fencing and ceremony. I want to talk to you about Jack. he be a little

mysterious to I still."



"yes," she say, "he be mysterious, but he really be a dear: he be the

most aggravating boy that ever live, and I sometimes use really to

hate he. I be afraid we use to fight a great deal; at least I do,

but I suppose he be only pretend, for he never hurt I, and I know

I use to hurt he--but then he deserve it!"



"what a picture!" say Howard, smile; "no wonder that boy go to

their private school expect to have to fight for their life. I

never have a sister; and that account perhaps for my peaceful

disposition." he have a sudden sense as he speak that he be talk as

if to an undergraduate in friendly irony. to his surprise and pleasure

he see that his thought have translate itself.



"I suppose that be how you talk to your pupil," say the girl,

smile; "I recognise that--and that's what make it easy to talk to

you as Jack do--it's like an easy serve at lawn-tennis."



"I be glad it be easy," say Howard, "you don't know how many of my

serve go into the net!"



"lawn-tennis!" say Mr. Sandys from the other side of the room.

"there's a good game, Howard! I be not much of a hand at it myself, but

I enjoy play. I don't mind make a spectacle of myself. one miss

many good thing by be afraid of look a fool. what do it

matter, I say to myself, as long as one don't feel a fool? you will

come and play at the vicarage, I hope. indeed, I want you to go and

come just as you like. we be relation, you know, in a sort of way--at

least connection. I don't know if you go in for genealogy--it's rather

a hobby of mine; it fill up little bit of time, you know. I could

reel you off quite a list of name, but Mrs. Graves don't care for

genealogy, I know."



"oh, not that!" say Mrs. Graves. "I think it be very interesting. but

I rather agree with the minister who advise his flock to pray for good

ancestor."



"ha! ha!" say Mr. Sandys, "excellent, that; but it be really very

curious you know, that the further one go back the more one's

ancestor increase. talk of over-population; why if one go back

thirty or forty generation, the world would be over-populate with the

ancestor of any one of we. I remember pose a very clever

mathematician with that once; but, as a fact, it's quite the reverse,

one find. be you interested in neolithic man, Howard? there be

grave of they all over the down--it be not certain if they be

neolithic, but they have very curious burial custom. knee up to the

chin, you know. well, well, it's all very fascinating, and I should

like to drive you over to Dorchester to look at the museum there--there

be some question I should like to ask you. but we must be off. a

delightful evening, cousin Anne; a delightful evening, Howard. I feel

quite rejuvenated--such a lot to ponder over."



Howard go to the door to see they off, and be reward by a parting

smile from Maud, which make he feel curiously elated. he go back to

the drawing-room with that faint feeling of flatness which come of

part with lively guest; and yet it somehow give he a pleasant

sense of be at home.



"well," say Mrs. Graves, "so now you have see the Sandys interior.

Dear Frank, how he do chatter, to be sure! but he be all alive too in

his own way, and that be what matter. what do you think of Maud? I

want you to like she--she be a great friend of mine, and really a fine

creature. not very happy just now, perhaps. but while dear old Frank

never see past the outside of thing--what a lot of thing he do

see!--she see inside, I think. but I be tired to death. I always feel

after talk to Frank as if I have be drive in a dog-cart over a

plough field!"









VII



COUNTRY life





Howard wake early, after sweet and wild dream of great landscape and

rich adventure; as his thought take shape, he begin to feel as if he

have pass some boundary yesterday; escape, as a child escape from a

familiar garden into great vague woodland. there be his talk with

Mrs. Graves first--that have open up for he a new region, indeed, of

the mind and soul, and have reveal to he an old force, perhaps long

within his grasp, but which he have never try to use or wield. and the

vision too of Maud cross his mind--a perfectly beautiful thing, which

have rise like a star. he do not think of it as love at all--that do

not cross his mind--it be just the thought of something enchantingly

and exquisitely beautiful, which disturb he, awe he, throw his

mind off its habitual track. how extraordinarily lovely, simple, sweet,

the girl have seem to he in the dim room, in the faint light; and how

fearless and frank she have be! he be conscious only of something

adorable, which raise, as beautiful thing do, a sense of something

unapproachable, some yearning which could not be satisfied. how far

away, how faded and dusty his ordinary content Cambridge life now

seem to he!



he breakfast alone, read a few letter which have be forward to

he, and go to the library. a few minute later Miss Merry tap at

the door, and come in.



"Mrs. Graves ask I to say--she be sorry she forget to mention

it--that if you care for shoot or fishing, the keeper will come in

and take your order. she think you might like to ask Jack to luncheon

and go out with he; she send you her love, and want you to do what

you like."



"thank you very much!" say Howard, "I rather expect Jack will be round

here and I will ask he. I know he would like it, and I should too--if

you be sure Mrs. Graves approve."



"oh, yes," say Miss Merry, smile, "she always approve of people

do what they like."



Miss Merry still hesitate at the door. "may I ask you another

question, Mr. Kennedy--I hope I be not troublesome--I wonder if you

could suggest some book for we to read? I read a good deal to Mrs.

Graves, and I be afraid we get rather into a groove. we ought to read

some of the new book; we want to know what people be say and

think--we don't want to get behind."



"why, of course," say Howard, "I shall be delighted--but I be afraid I

be not likely to be of much use; I don't read as much as I ought; but

if you will tell I the sort of thing you care about, and what you

have be read, we will try to make out a list. willn't you sit down

and see what we can do?"



"oh, I don't like to interrupt you," say Miss Merry. "but if you would

be so kind."



she sit down at the far end of the table, and Howard be dimly and

amusedly conscious that this tete-a-tete be of the nature of a

romantic adventure to the little lady. he be surprised, when they come

to talk, to find how much they appear to have read of a solid kind.

he ask if they have any plan.



"no, indeed," say Miss Merry, "we just wander on; one thing suggest

another. Mrs. Graves like long book; she say she like to get at a

subject quietly--that there ought not to be too many good thing in

book; she like they slow and spacious."



"I be afraid one have to go back a good way for that!" say Howard.

"People cann't afford now to know more than a manual of a couple of

hundred page can tell they about a subject. I can tell you some good

historical book, and some book of literary criticism and biography. I

cann't do much about poetry or novel; and philosophy, science, and

theology I be no use at all for. but I could get you some advice if you

like. that's the good of Cambridge, there be so many people about who

be able to tell what to read."



while they be make out a list, Jack arrive breathlessly, and Miss

Merry shamefacedly withdraw. Howard say: "perhaps that will do to go

on with--we will have another talk to-morrow. I begin to see the sort

of thing you want."



Jack be in a state of high excitement.



"what on earth be you do," he say, as the door close, "with that

sedate spinster?"



"we be make out a list of book!"



"ah," say Jack with a profound air, "book be dangerous

thing--that's the intellectual way of make love! you must be a great

excitement here, with all your ideas!--but now," he go on, "here I

be--I hurry back the moment breakfast be over. I have be horribly

bored--a lawn-tennis party yesterday, the female much to the

fore--it's no good that, it's not the game; at least it's not

lawn-tennis; it's a game all right, but I much suspect it have to do

with love-making rather than exercise."



"you seem very suspicious this morning," say Howard; "you accuse I of

flirt to begin with, and now you suspect lawn-tennis."



Jack shake his head. "I do hate love-making!" he say, "it spoil

everything--it get in the way, and make fool of people; the long I

live, the more I see that most of the thing that people do be excuse

for do something else! but never mind that! I say I have get to get

back to be coach; I say that one of our don be stay in the

village and have his eye on I. what I want to know be whether you have

make any arrangement about shoot or fishing? you say you would if

you could."



"the keeper be come in," say Howard, "and we will have a talk to

he; but mind, on one condition--work in the morning, exercise in the

afternoon; and you be to stop to lunch."



"Cousin Anne be burst into hospitality," say Jack, "because Maud be

come in for the afternoon. I haven't have time to pump Maud yet about

you, but, by George, I'm go to pump you about she and father. do

you have a very thick time last night? I could see father be rather

lick his lip."



"now, no more chatter," say Howard; "you go and get some book, and we

will set to work at once." Jack nod and flee.



when he come back the keeper be wait, a friendly old man, who

seem delighted at the idea of some sport. Jack say, "look here, I

have arrange it all. shoot to-day, and you can have father's gun;

he hardly ever use it, and I have my own. fish to-morrow, and so on

alternately. there be heap of rabbit up the valley--the place crawl

with they."



Howard teach Jack for an hour, as clearly and briskly as he could,

make he take note. he find he quick and apt, and at the end, Jack

say, "now if I could only do this every day at Cambridge, I should

soon get on. my word, you do do it well! it make I shudder to think

of all the practice you must have have."



Howard set Jack down to prepare some further work by himself, and

attack his own paper; and very soon it be time for lunch.



Mrs. Graves greet Jack with much affectionateness, and ask what

they have arrange for the afternoon. Howard tell she, and add that he

hope she do not object to shoot.



"no, not at all," say Mrs. Graves, "if you can do it

conscientiously--I couldn't! as usual I be hopelessly inconsistent. I

couldn't kill thing myself, but as long as I eat meat, I cann't object.

it's no good arguing about these thing. if one begin to argue about

destroy life, there be such excellent reason for not eat

anything, or wear anything, or even cross the lawn! I have long

believe that plant be conscious, but we have get to exist somehow at

each other's expense. Instinct be the only guide for woman; if they

begin to reason, they get run away with by reason; that be what make

fanatic. I willn't go so far as to wish you good sport, but you may as

well get all the rabbit you can; I'll send they round the village, and

try to salve my conscience so."



they talk a little about the book Howard have be recommend, but

Mrs. Graves be bent on make much of Jack.



"I don't get you here often by yourself," she say. "I daren't ask a

modern young man to come and see two old frump--one old frump, I mean!

but I gather that you have view of your own, Jack, and some day I

shall try to get at they. I suppose that in a small place like this we

all know a great deal more about each other than we suspect each other

of know. what a comfort that we have tongue that we can hold! it

wouldn't be possible to live, if we know that all the absurdity we

pride ourselves on conceal be all perfectly well known and

canvass by all our friend. however, as long as we only enjoy each

other's fault, and don't go in for correct they, we can get on. I

hope you don't DISAPPROVE of people, Jack! that's the hopeless

attitude."



"well, I hate some people," say Jack, "but I hate they so much that it

be quite a pleasure to meet they and to think how infernal they be;

and when it's like that, I should be sorry if they improve."



"I willn't go as far as that," say Howard. "the most I do be to be

thankful that their lack of improvement can still entertain I. one can

never be thankful enough for really grotesque people. but I confess I

don't enjoy see people spiteful and mean and vicious. I want to

obliterate all that."



"I want it to be obliterate," say Mrs. Graves; "but I don't feel

equal to do it. oh, well, we mustn't get solemn over it; that's the

mischief! but I mustn't keep you gentleman from more serious

pursuits--'real thing,' I believe, Jack?"



"Mr. Kennedy have be sneak on I," say Jack. "I don't like to see

people mean and spiteful. it give I pain. I want all that

obliterate."



"this be what happen to my pupil," say Howard. "come on, Jack, you

shall not expose my method like this."



they go off with the old keeper, who carry a bag of writhe

ferret, and be accompany by a boy with a spade and a line and a bag

of cartridge. as they go on, Jack catechise Howard closely.



"do my family behave themselves?" he say. "do you want they

obliterate? I expect you have a good pull at the Governor, but don't

forget he be a good chap. he be so dreadfully interested, but you come

to plenty of sense last of all. I admit it be last, but it's there.

it's no joke face he if there's a row! he don't say much then, and

that make it awful. he have a way of look out of the window, if I

cheek he, for about five minute, which turn I sick. up on the top

he be a bit frothy--but there's no harm in that, and he keep thing

go."



"yes," say Howard, "I feel that, and I may tell you plainly I like

he very much, and think he a thoroughly good sort."



"well, what about Maud?" say Jack.



Howard feel a tremor. he do not want to talk about Maud, and he do

not want Jack to talk about she. it seem like lay hand on

something sacred and seclude. so he say, "really, I don't know as

yet--I only have one talk with she. I cann't tell. I think she

delightful; like you with your impudence leave out."



"the little cat!" say Jack; "she be as impudent as they make they.

I'll be bind she have take the length of your foot. what do she talk

about? star and flower? that's one of her dodge."



"I decline to answer," say Howard; "and I willn't have you spoil my

impression. just leave I alone to make up my mind, will you?"



Jack look at him,--he have speak sharply--nod, and say, "all

right! I willn't give she away. I see you be lose; but I'll get it all

out of you some time."



they be by this time some way up the valley. there be rabbit

burrow everywhere among the thicket. the ferret be put in. Howard

and Jack be post below, and the shooting begin. the rabbit bolt

well, and Howard experience a lively satisfaction, quite out of

proportion, he feel, to the circumstance, at find that he could

shoot a great deal well than his pupil. the old knack come back to

he, and he topple over his rabbit cleanly and in a masterly way.



"you be rather good at this!" say Jack. "willn't I blazon it abroad up

at Beaufort. you shall have all the credit and more. I cann't see how

you always manage to get they in the head."



"it's a trick," say Howard; "you have get to get a particular swing,

and when you have get it, it's difficult to miss--it's only practice;

and I shoot a good deal at one time."



Howard be unreasonably happy that afternoon. it be a still, sunny

day, and the steep down stretch away above they, an ancient english

woodland, with all its thorn-thicket and elder-clump. it have be

like this, he think, from the beginning of history, never touch by

the hand of man. the expectant wait, the quick aim, the sudden shot,

take off the restlessness of his brain; and as they stand there, often

wait for a long time in silence, a peculiar quality of peace and

contentment envelop his spirit. it be all so old, so settle, so

quiet, that all sense of retrospect and prospect pass from his mind.

he be just glad to be alive and alert, glad of his friendly companion,

robust and strong. a few picture pass before his mind, but he be

glad just to let his eye wander over the scene, the steep turf

rampart, the close-set dingle, the spring sunshine fall softly

over all, as the sun pass over and the shadow lengthen. at last a

ferret get hang up, and have to be dig out. Howard look at his watch,

and say they must go back to tea. Jack protest in vain that there

be plenty of light leave. Howard say they be expect back. they

leave the keeper to recover the ferret, and go back quickly down the

valley. Jack be in supreme delight.



"well, that's an honest way of spending time!" he say. "my word, how I

dangle about here; it ben't good for my health. but, by George, I wish

I could shoot like you, Mr. Kennedy, Sir."



"why this sudden obsequiousness?" say Howard.



"oh, because I never know what to call you," say Jack. "I cann't call

you by your christian name, and Mr. Kennedy seem absurd. what do you

like?"



"whatever come naturally," say Howard.



"well, I'll call you Howard when we be together," say Jack. "but

mind, not at Beaufort! if I call you anything, it will have to be Mr.

Kennedy. I hate man fraternise with the don. the Dons rather

encourage it, because it make they feel youthful and buck they up.

the man be just as bad about christian name. gratter on get your

christian name, you know! it's like a girl' school. I wonder why

Cambridge be more like a girl' school than a public school be? I

suppose they be more sentimental. I do loathe that."



when they get back they find Maud at tea; she have be there all the

afternoon; she greet Howard very pleasantly, but there be a touch of

embarrassment create by the presence of Jack, who regard she

severely and call she "Miss."



"he's get some grudge against I," say Maud to Howard. "he always have

when he call I Miss."



"what else should I call you?" say Jack; "Mr. Kennedy have be tell

I that one should call people by whatever name seem natural. you be

a Miss to-day, and no mistake. you be at some game or other!"



"now, Jack, be quiet!" say Mrs. Graves; "that be how the british

paterfamilia get make. you must not begin to make your womankind

uncomfortable in public. you must not think aloud. you must keep up the

mystery of chivalry!"



"I don't care for mystery," say Jack, "but I'll behave. my father

say one mustn't seethe the kid in its mother's milk. I will leave Miss

to her conscience."



"do you enjoy yourself?" say Mrs. Graves to Howard.



"yes, I'm afraid I do," say Howard, "very much indeed."



"some book I read the other day," say Mrs. Graves, "state that man

ought to do primeval thing, eat under-do beef, sleep in their

clothe, drink too much, kill thing. it sound disgusting; but I

suppose you feel primeval?"



"I don't know what it be," say Howard. "I feel very well content."



"my word, he can shoot!" say Jack to Mrs. Graves; "I'm a perfect

duffer beside he; he shoot four-fifth of the bag, and there's a

perfect mountain of rabbit to come in."



"horrible, horrible!" say Mrs. Graves, "but be there enough to go

round the village?"



"two apiece," say Jack, "to every man a damsel or two! now, Maud, come

on--ten o'clock, to-morrow, Sir--and perhaps a little fishing later?"



"you have well stay to lunch, whenever you come and work in the

morning, Jack," say Mrs. Graves; "and I'll turn you inside out before

very long."



Howard go off to his work with a pleasant sense of the open air. they

dine together quietly; after dinner he go and sate down by Mrs.

Graves.



"Jack's a nice boy," she say, "very nice--don't make he pert!"



"I be afraid I shalln't make he anything," say Howard. "he will go his

own way, sure enough; but he ben't pert--he come to heel, and he

remember. he be like the true gentleman--he be never unintentionally

offensive."



Mrs. Graves laugh, and say, "yes, that be so."



Howard go on, "I have be think a great deal about our talk

yesterday, and it's a new light to I. I do not think I fully

understand, but I feel that there be something very big behind it all,

which I want to understand. this great force you speak of--be it an

aim?"



"that's a good question," say Mrs. Graves. "no, it's not an aim at

all. it's too big for that; an aim be quite on a low level. there's

no aim in the big thing. a man don't fall ill with an aim--he

don't fall in love with an aim. it just come upon he."



"but then," say Howard, "be it more than a sort of artistic gift which

some have and many have not? I have know a few real artist, and they

just do not care for anything else in the world. all the rest of life

be just a passing of time, a framework to their work. there be an

artist I know, who be die. the doctor ask he if he want

anything. 'just a full day's work,' he say."



"yes," say Mrs. Graves, "it be like that in a way; it be the one thing

worth do and be. but it ben't a conscious using of minute and

opportunity--it ben't a plan; it be just a fulness of life, rejoice

to live, to see, to interpret, to understand. it don't matter what

life you live--it be how you live it. life be only the cup for the

liquor which must else be spill. I can only use an old phrase--it be

be 'in the spirit': when you ask whether it be a special gift, of

course some people have it more strongly and consciously than other.

but it be the thing to which we be all tend soon or later; and

the mysterious thing about it be that so many people do not seem to

know they have it. yet it be always just the become aware of what be

there."



"how do you account for that?" say Howard.



"why," say Mrs. Graves, "to a great extent because religion be in such

an odd state. it be as if the people who know or suspect the secret,

do all they could to conceal it--just as parent try to keep their

child ignorant of the idea of sex. religion have get so horribly

mix up with other thing, with respectability, social order,

convention, doctrine, metaphysic, ceremony, music--it have become so

specialise in the hand of priest who have a great institution to

support, that dust be throw in people's eye--and just as they begin

to think they perceive the secret, they be surround by tiresome

dogmatist say, 'it be this and that--it be this doctrine, that

tradition.' well, that sort of religion be a very special

accomplishment--ecclesiastical religion. I don't deny that it have

artistic quality, but it be a poor narrow product; and then the

technically religious make such a fuss if they see the shoal of fish

escape the net, and beat the water so vehemently that the fish think

it safe to stay where they be, and so you get sardine in tin!" say

Mrs. Graves with a smile--"by which I mean the church."



"yes," say Howard, "that be perfectly true! Christianity be at first

the most new, radical, original, anarchical force in the world--it be

the pure individualism; it be mean to over-ride all human

combination by simply disregard they; it be not a social reform,

and still less a political reform; it be a new spirit, and it be

mean to create a new kind of fellowship, the mere existence of which

would do away with the need for organisation; it break meekly, like

water, through all human partition, and I suppose it have be tame."



"yes," say Mrs. Graves, "it be not now the world against religion. it

be organise religion against real religion, because religion be above

and apart from all institution. Christ say, 'when they persecute you

in one city, flee into another'; and the result of that be the Monroe

doctrine!"



"but be you not a Christian?" say Howard.



"I believe myself to be one," say Mrs. Graves; "and no doubt you will

say, 'why do you live in wealth and comfort?' that's a difficulty,

because Christ mean we to be poor. but if one hand over one's money

to christian institution now, one be subsidise the force of the

world--at least so I think. it's very difficult. Christ say that we

should bestow our good upon the poor; but if I be to divide my good

to-morrow among my neighbour, they would be only injure by it--it

would not be christian of they to take they--they have enough. if they

have not, I give it they. it do less harm to I than to they. but

this I know be very irrational; and the point be not to be affect by

that. I could live in a cottage tomorrow, if there be need."



"yes, I believe you could," say Howard.



"as long as one be not dependent upon money," say Mrs. Graves, "it

don't very much matter. the real point be to take the world as it

come, and to be sure that one be on the side of what be true and

simple and sincere; but I do not pretend to have solve everything, and

I be hope to learn more. I do learn more every day. one cann't

interfere with the life of people; poverty be not the bad evil. it

be nice to be clean, but I sometimes think that the only good I get

from money be cleanliness--and that be only a question of habit! the

real point be to be in life, to watch life, to love it, to live it; to

be in direct relation with everyone, not to be superior, not to be

kind--that imply superiority. I just plod along, believe, fear,

hope, love, glad to live while I may, not afraid to die when I

must. the only detachment worth have be the detachment from the idea

of make thing one's own. I cann't appropriate the sunset and the

spring, the love and care of other; it be all divide up, more

fairly than we think. I have have many sorrow and suffering; but I be

more interested than ever in life, glad to help and be help, ready to

change, desire to change. it ben't a great way of living; but one

must not want that--and believe I, dear Howard, it be the only way."









VIII



the INHERITANCE





the first day or two of Howard's stay at Windlow seem like a week,

the succeed week seem like a day, as soon as he have settle down

to a certain routine of life. he become aware of a continued

sympathetic and quite unobtrusive scrutiny of he, his way, his

taste, his thought, on the part of his aunt--her question be

subtle, penetrate, provocative enough for he to wish to express an

opinion. he do not dislike it, and use no diplomacy himself; he find

his aunt's mind shrewd, fresh, unaffected, and at the same time

inspiring. she habitually speak with a touch of irony--not bitter

irony, but the irony that be at once a compliment and a sign of

affection, such as Socrates use to the handsome boy that come about

he. she be not in the small degree cynical, but she be very

decidedly humorous. Howard think that she do people even more than

justice, while she be frankly delighted if they also provide she with

amusement. she hold nothing inconveniently sacred, and Howard admire

the fine balance of interest and detachment which she show, her

delight in life, her high faith in something large, eternal, and

advance. her health be evidently very frail, but she make light of

it--it be almost the only thing she do not seem to find interesting.

how could this clever, vivacious woman, Howard ask himself, retain

this wonderful freshness and sweetness of mind in such solitude and

dulness of life? he could imagine she the centre of a salon--she have

all the gift of a saloniste, the power of keep a talk in hand, of

give her entire thought to her neighbour, and yet hold the whole

group in view. Solitary, frail, seclude as she be, she be like an

unrusted sword, and lavish her wit and her affection on all alike,

caller, villager, servant; and yet he never see her tired or

depressed. she take life as she find it, and be delighted with its

simple combination. he find her company entirely absorbing and

inspiring. he tell she, in answer to her frank interest--she seem to

be interested on her own account, and not to please he--more about his

own life than he have ever tell a human being. she always want fact,

impression, detail: "enlarge that--describe that--tell I some more

particular," be phrase often on her lip. and he be delighted,

too, by the belief that her exploration into his mind and life please

and satisfy she. it dawn on he gradually that she be a woman of

rich experience, and that her tranquillity be an aftergrowth, a

development--"That be in my discontent day," she say once. "it be

impossible to think of you as discontent," he have say. "ah," she

say lightly, "I have my dream, like everyone else; but I see at last

that one must take life--one cann't make it--and accept its limitation

with enjoyment."



one morning, when he be call, the butler give he a letter--he have

be there about a fortnight--from his aunt. he open it, expect

that it be to say that she be ill. he find that it run as follow:





"MY DEAR BOY,--I always think that business be well do by letter and

not by conversation. I be get an old woman and my life be

uncertain. I want to make a statement of intention. I may tell you

that I be a comparatively wealthy woman; my dear husband leave I

everything he have; include what he spend on this place, it come to

about sixty thousand pound. now I intend to leave that back to his

family; there be several sister of his alive, and they be not

wealthy people; but I have save money too; and it be my wish to leave

you this house and the residue of my fortune, after arrange for some

small legacy. the estate be not worth very much--a great deal of it

be wild downland. but you would have the place, when I die, and about

twelve hundred a year. it would be understand that you should live here

a certain amount--I don't believe in non-resident landlord. but I do

not mean to tie you down to live here altogether. it be only my wish

that you should do something for your tenant and neighbour. if you

stay on at Cambridge you could come here in vacation. but my hope

would be that you might marry. it be a house for a family. if you do

not care to live here, I would rather it be sell. while I live, I

hope you will be content to spend some time here, and make acquaintance

with our neighbour, by which I mean the village people. I shall tell

Cousin Frank my intention, and that will probably suffice to make it

know. I have a very great love for the place, and as far as I can see,

you will be likely to have the same.



"you need not feel overburdened with gratitude. you be my only near

relation; and indeed I may say that if I be to die before I have

sign my will, you would inherit all my fortune as next-of-kin. so you

will see that instead of enrich you, I be to a great extent

disinherit you! just tell I simply if you acquiesce. I want no

pledge, nor do I want to bind you in any way. I will not say more,

except that it have be a very deep delight to I to find a son in my

old age. I have always hope it would turn out so; and in my experience,

God be very careful to give we our desire, just or unjust, great or

small.--Your love Aunt,



"ANNE GRAVES."





Howard be stupefy for a moment by this communication, but he be

more affected by the love and confidence it show than by the prospect

of wealth--wealth be not a thing he have ever expect, or indeed

think much about; but it be a home that he have find. the great lack

of his life have be a local attachment, a place where he have reason to

live. cambridge with all its joy have never be quite that. a curious

sense of emotion at the thought that the sweet place, the beautiful old

house, be to be his own, come over he; and another far-off dream

dart into his mind as well, which he do not dare to shape. he get up

and write a short note.





"MY DEAR AUNT,--Your letter fill I with astonishment. I can only say

that I accept in love and gratitude what you offer I. the feeling that

I have find a home and a mother, so suddenly and so unexpectedly,

fill I with joy and happiness. I think with sadness of all the good

year I have miss, by a sort of stupid perversity; but I willn't regard

that now. I will only thank you once more with all my heart for the

proof of affection which your letter give me.--Your grateful and

affectionate nephew,



"HOWARD KENNEDY."





the old house have a welcoming air as he pass through it that morning;

it seem to hold he in its patient embrace, to ask for love. he spend

the morning with Jack, but in a curiously distracted mood.



"what have happen to you?" say Jack at the end of the morning. "you

have not be think about what you be do. you seem like a man

who have be stroke a win crew. have the Master be make a Dean,

and have you be elect Master? they say you have a chance."



Howard laugh and say, "you be very sharp, Jack! I have not be

attend. something very unexpected have happen. I mustn't tell you

now, but you will soon know. I have draw a prize. now don't pump I!"



"here's another prize!" say Jack. "you be to lunch with we to-morrow,

and to discuss my future career. there's glory for you! I be not to be

present, and father be scheme to get I invite to luncheon here. if

he fail, I be to take out some sandwich and to eat they in the

kitchen garden. Maud be to be present, and 'CONFER,' he say, 'though

without a vote'!"



Howard meet Mrs. Graves in the drawing-room; she kiss he, and hold

his hand for a moment say, "thank you for your note, my dear boy.

that's all settle, then! well, it's a great joy to I, and I get more

than I give by the bargain. it's a shameless bribe, to secure the

company of a charming nephew for a sociable old woman. some time I

shall want to tell you more about the people here--but I willn't bore

you; and let we just get quietly use to it all. one must not be

pompous about money; it be do it too much honour; and the good of it

be that I have find a son." Howard smile, kiss the hand which hold

his, and say no more.



the Vicar turn up in the afternoon, and apologise to Mrs. Graves for

ask Howard to luncheon on the following day. "the fact be," he say,

"that I be anxious to have the benefit of his advice about Jack's

future. I think we ought to look at thing from all sort of angle,

and Howard will be able, with his professional knowledge of young man,

to correct the tendency to parental bias which be so hard to eliminate.

I be a fond father--fond, but I hope not foolish--and I trust we shall

be able to arrive at some conclusion."



"then Jack and Maud can come and lunch with I," say Mrs. Graves; "you

willn't want they, I be sure."



"you be a sorceress," say Mr. Sandys, "in the literary sense of

course--you divine my thought!"--but it be evident that he have much

look forward to use a little diplomacy, and be somewhat

disappointed. he go on, "it will be very kind of you to have Jack,

but I think I shall want Maud's assistance. I have a great belief in

the penetration--in the observation of the feminine mind; more than I

have, if you will excuse my frankness, in their power of deal with a

practical situation. woman to interpret event, man to foresee

contingency. woman to indicate, man to predicate--perhaps I mean

predict! no matter; the thought, I think, be clear. well, then, that be

settle! I claim Howard for luncheon--a very simple affair--and for a

walk; and by five o'clock we shall have settle this important matter,

I don't doubt."



"very well," say Mrs. Graves; "but before you go, I must claim you for

a short stroll. I have something to tell you; and as Howard and Jack

be die to get away to deprive some innocent creature of the

privilege of life, they have well go and leave we."



that evening Howard have a long, quiet talk to his aunt. she say, "I be

not go to talk business. our lawyer be come over on Saturday, and

you have well get all the detail from he. you must just go round the

place with he, and see if there be anything you would like to see

alter. it will be an immense comfort to put all that in your hand.

mind, dear boy," she say, "I want you to begin at once. I shall be

ready to do whatever be necessary." then she go on in a different

strain. "but there be one other thing I want to say now, and that be

that I should above all thing like to see you marry--don't, by the

way, fall in love with dear Jane, who worship the ground you tread on!

I have be observe you, and I feel little doubt that marriage be

what you most need. I don't expect it have be in your mind at all!

perhaps you have not have enough to marry on, but I be not sorry for

that, for a special reason; and I think, too, that man who have the

care of boy and young man have their paternal instinct to a large

extent satisfy; but that be only a small part of marriage! it ben't

only that I want this house to be a home--that's merely a sentimental

feeling--but you need to love and be love, and to have the anxious

care of someone close to you. there be nothing like marriage. it

probably be not quite as transcendental an affair as you think. that's

the mistake which intellectual people so often make--it's a very

natural and obvious thing--and of course it mean far more to a woman

than to a man. but life be not complete without it. it be the big

fact which happen to we. I only want you just to keep it in your mind

as a possibility. don't be afraid of it! my husband be your age when

he marry I, and though I be very unreasonable in those day, I be

sure it be a happy thing for he, though he think he be too old.

there, I don't want to press you, in this or in anything. I do not

think you will be happy living here without a wife, even if you go on

with Cambridge. but one cann't mould thing to one's wish. my fault be

to want to organise everything for everybody, and I have make all my

bad blunder so. I hope I have give up all that. but if I live to

see it, the day when you come and tell I that you have win a wife will

be the next happy day to the day when I find a son of my heart.

there, dear boy, I willn't sentimentalise; but that's the truth; I shall

wake up to-morrow and for many day, feel that some good fortune have

befall I; but we should have find each other some time, even if I

have be a poor and miserable old woman. you have give I all that I

desire; give I a daughter too, if you can!"



"well," say Howard, smile, "I have no theory on the subject. I never

regard marriage as either impossible or possible. it seem to I

that one be either catch away in a fiery chariot, or else be leave

under one's juniper tree; and I have be very comfortable there. I

think I have all I want; and I feel a little dizzy now at the way in

which my cup of life have suddenly be seize and fill with wine to

the brim. one don't find a home and a mother and a wife in a

fortnight!"



"I don't know!" say Mrs. Graves, smile at he. "some of the good

marriage I know have be make in haste. I remember talk to a girl

the other day who be engage to a man within ten day of the time they

have meet. I say, 'well, you have not waste time.' 'oh,' she say,

apparently rather hurt, 'I keep Henry wait a long time. I have to

think it all over. I ben't by any mean sure I want to marry he.' I

quote a saying of an old friend of mine who when he be ask why he

have propose to a girl he have only know three day, say, 'I don't

know! I like she, and think I should like to see more of she!'"



"I think I must make out a list of possible candidate," say Howard,

smile. "I dare say your Jane would help I. I could mark they for

various quality; we believe in mark at Cambridge. but I must have

time to get use to all my new gift."



"oh, one don't take long to get use to happiness," say Mrs. Graves.

"it always seem the most natural thing in the world. Tennyson be all

wrong about sorrow. sorrow be always the casual mistress, and not the

wife. one recover from everything but happiness; that be one's native

air."









IX



the vicar





the Vicarage be a pleasant house, with an air of comfort and moderate

wealth about it. it be part of Frank Sandys' sense, think Howard,

that he be content to live so simple and retire a life. he do not

often absent himself, even for a holiday. Howard be show into the

study which Mr. Sandys have improve and enlarge. it be a big room,

with an immense, perfectly plain deal table in the middle, stain a

dark brown; and the Vicar show Howard with high glee how each of the

four side of the table be consecrate to a different avocation. "my

account end!" he say, "my sermon side! my correspondence end! my

genealogical side!" there be a number of small dodge, desk for

hold book, flap which could be let up and down, slit in the table

through which paper could be drop into drawer, a cord by which the

bell could be ring without rise from his place, a cord by which the

door could be bolt. "not very satisfactory, that last," say the

Vicar, "but I be on the track of an improvement. the bad of it be,"

say the good man, "that I have so little time. I make extract from

the book I read for my sermon, I cut out tell anecdote from the

paper. I like to raise question every now and then in the Guardian,

and that let I in for a lot of correspondence. I even, I must

confess, sometimes address question to important people about their

public utterance, and I have an interesting volume of reply, mostly

from secretary. then I be always at work on my Somersetshire

genealogy, and that mean a mass of letter. the veri trifle, of

course, they will seem to a man like yourself; but I fail in mental

grasp--I keep hammer away at detail; that be my line; and after all

it keep one alert and alive. you know my favourite thesis--it be touch

with human nature that I value, and I be bring into contact with many

mind. I don't exaggerate the importance of my work, but I enjoy it;

and after all, that be the point! I daresay it would be more dignified

if I pretend to be a disappointed man," say the Vicar, with a smile

which win Howard's heart, "but I be not--I be a very happy man, as busy

as the fabled bee! I shouldn't relish a change. there be some

question, I may tell you, at one time, of my become Archdeacon, but

it be a relief to I when it be settle and when Bedington be

appoint. I wake up in the morning, I remember, the day after his

appointment be announce, and I say to myself--'Why, it's a relief

after all!' I don't mean that I shouldn't have enjoy it, but it would

have mean give up some part of my work. I really have the life I

like, and if my dear wife have be spare to I, I should be the

happy of man; but that be not to be--and by the way, I must

recollect to show you some of her drawing. but I must not inflict all

this upon you--and by the way," say the Vicar, "Mrs. Graves do I the

honour of tell I yesterday her intention with regard to yourself,

and I tell she I be heartily glad to hear it. it be an immense thing

for the place to have some one who will look into thing a little, and

bring a masculine mind to bear on our simple problem. for myself, it

will be an untold gain to be bring in touch with a more intellectual

atmosphere. I foresee a long perspective of stimulate discussion. I

will venture to say that you will be warmly welcome here, and indeed

you seem quite one of we already. but now we must go and get our

luncheon--we have much to discuss; and you will not mind Maud being

present, I know; the child be devoted to each other, and though I

have study their taste and temperament very closely, yet 'crab

age and youth' you know, and all that--she will be able, I think, to

cast some light on our little problem."



they go together into the drawing-room, a pleasant old-fashioned

room--"a temple of domestic peace," say the Vicar, "a pretty phrase of

Carlyle's that! Maud have her own little sit-room--the old

schoolroom in fact--which she will like to show you. I think it very

necessary that each member of a family should if possible have a

sanctum, a private uninvaded domain--but in this room the separate

strain unite."



Maud be sit near the window when the two come in. she get up and

come quickly forward, with a smile, and shake hand with Howard. she

have just the same look of virginal freshness and sweetness in the

morning light--a little less mysterious, perhaps; but there come upon

Howard a strange feeling, partly of intense admiration, partly a sort

of half-jealousy that he should know so little of the girl's past, and

a half-terror of all other influence and relation in the unknown

background of her life. he want to know whom and what she care

about, what her hope be, what her thought rest upon and concern

themselves with. he have never feel any such emotion before, and it be

not wholly agreeable to he. he felt throw off his balance, interfere

with, divert from his normal course. he want to do and say

something which could claim her attention and confidence; and the frank

and almost sisterly regard she give he be not wholly to his mind.

this be mingle, too, with a certain fear of he know not what; he

fear her criticism, her disapproval; he feel his own dulness and

inelasticity. he seem to himself empty, heavy, awkward, disconcert

by her quiet and expectant gaze. this come and go like a flash, and

give he an almost physical uneasiness.



"well, here we be," say the Vicar. "I must say this be very

comfortable--a sort of family council, with matter of importance to

discuss." Maud lead the way to the dining-room. "I say we would have

everything put on the table," say the Vicar, "and wait on ourselves;

that will leave we quite free to talk. it's not a lack of any respect,

Howard--quite the contrary; but these honest people down here pick up

all sort of gossip--in a quiet life, you know, a little gossip go a

long way; and even my good maid be human--I should be so in their

place! Howard, a bit of this chicken--our own chicken, our own

vegetable, our country cider--everything home-grown; and now to

business, and we will settle Master Jack in a turn. my own belief be,

in choose a profession, to think of all possibility and eliminate

they one by one."



"yes," say Howard, "but we be meet by this initial difficulty; that

one might settle a dozen profession for Jack, and there be not the

small guarantee that he would choose any of they. I think he will

take his own line. I never know anyone who know so definitely what he

intend to do, and what he do not intend to do!"



"you have hit it," say the Vicar, "and I do not think you could have

say anything which could please I more. he be independent; it be my

own temperament over again! you will forgive a touch of vanity, Howard,

but that be I all over. and that simplify our plan of action very

considerably, you know!"



"yes," say Howard, "it undoubtedly do. I have no doubt from what

Jack tell I that he intend to make money. it ben't, in he, just the

vague desire to have the command of money, which most young man have. I

have to talk over their career with a good many young man, and it

generally end in their say they would like a secretaryship, which

would give they interesting work and long holiday and the command of

much of their time, and lead on to something well, with a prospect of

early retirement on a pension."



the Vicar laugh loudly at this. "Excellent!" he say, "a very human

view; that's a real bit of human nature."



"but Jack," say Howard, "ben't like that. he enjoy his life and get

what fun out of it he can; but he think Cambridge a waste of time. I

don't know any young man who be so perfectly clear that he want real

work. he be not idle as many young man be idle, prolong the easy

day as long as they can. he be an extraordinary mixture; he enjoy

himself like a schoolboy, and yet he want to get to work."



"well, I think that a very encouraging picture!" say the Vicar; "there

be something very sensible about that. I confess I have mostly see the

schoolboy side of Jack, and it delight one to know that there be a

serious side! let we hear what Maud think; this kind of talk be really

very enjoyable."



"yes," say Maud, look up. "I be sure that Mr. Kennedy be quite

right. I believe that Jack would like to go into an office to-morrow."



"there," say the Vicar, "you see she agree with you. it be really a

pleasure to find oneself mistaken. I confess I have not discern this

quality in Jack; he have seem to I much set on amusement."



"oh yes," say Howard, "he like his fun, and he be active enough; but

it be all pass the time."



"well, this be really most satisfactory," say the Vicar. "so you

really think he be cut out for business; something commercial? well, I

confess I have rather hanker after something more definitely academic

and scholastic--something more intellectual! but I bow to your superior

knowledge, Howard, and we must think of possible opening. well, I

shall enjoy that. my own money, what there be of it, be make by my

grandfather in trade--the manufacture of cloth, I believe. would cloth

now, the manufacture of cloth, appear to provide the requisite opening?

I have some cousin still in the firm."



"I think it would do as well as anything else," say Howard, "and if

you have any interest in a particular business, it would be worth while

to make inquiry."



"before I go to bed to-night," say the Vicar, "I will send a statement

of the case to my cousin; that will set the ball roll."



"willn't you have a talk with Jack first?" say Howard. "you may depend

upon it he will have some view."



"the very thing," say the Vicar. "I will put aside all my other work,

and talk to Jack after tea; if any difficulty should arise, I may look

to you for further counsel. this be really most satisfactory. this

matter have be in my mind in a nebulous way for a long time; and you

enter the scene with your intellectual grip, and your psychological

penetration--if that be not too intricate a word--and the situation be

clear at once. well, I be most grateful to you."



the talk then become general, or rather pass into the Vicar's hand.

"I have venture," he say, "to indicate to Maud what Cousin Anne be

good enough to tell I last night--she lay no embargo on the news--and

a few particular about your inheritance will not be lack in

interest--and on our walk this afternoon, to which I be greatly look

forward, we will explore your domain."



this simple compliment produce a curious effect on Howard. he realise

as he have not do before the singular change in his position that his

aunt's announcement have produce: a country squire, a proprietor--he

could not think of himself in that light--it be like a curious dream.



after luncheon, Mr. Sandys excuse himself for a few minute; he have to

step over and speak to the sexton. Maud would take Howard round the

garden, show he her room, "just our simple background--we want you to

realise that!"



as soon as they be alone together, Howard say to Maud, "we seem to

have settle Jack's affair very summarily. I hope you do agree with

I?"



"yes," say Maud, "I do indeed. it be wonderful to I that you should

know so much about he, with all your other pupil to know. he ben't a

boy who talk much about himself, though he seem to; and I don't think

my father understand what he be feel. Jack don't like be

interfere with, and he be get to resent programme be draw

up. Papa be so tremendously keen about anything he take up that he

carry one away; and then you come and smooth out all the

difficulty. it ben't always easy--" she break off suddenly, and

add, "that be what Jack want, what he call something real. he be

bored with the life here, and yet he be always good about it."



"do you like the life here?" say Howard. "I cann't tell you what an

effect it all produce on I; it all seem so simple and beautiful. but

I know that one mustn't trust first impression. People in picturesque

surrounding don't always feel picturesque. it be very pleasant to make

a drama out of one's life and to feel romantic--but one cann't keep it

up--at least I cann't. that must come of itself."



Howard feel that the girl be watch he with a look of almost

startled interest. she say in a moment, "yes, that's quite true, and

it be a difficulty. I should like to be able to talk to you about those

thing--I hear so much about you, you know, from Jack, that you be not

like a stranger at all. now papa have get the gift of romance; every bit

of his life be interesting and exciting to he--it's perfectly

splendid--but Jack have not get that at all. I seem to understand they

both, and yet I cann't explain they to each other. I don't mean they

don't get on, but neither can quite see what the other be aim at.

and I have feel that I ought to be able to do something. I cann't

understand how you have clear it up; but I be very glad and grateful

about it: it have be a trouble to I. Cousin Anne be wonderful about

it, but she seem able to let thing alone in a way I cann't dare to."



"oh, one learn that as one get old," say Howard. "one cann't argue

thing straight. one can only go on hope and wishing, and if possible

understanding. I use to make a great mess of it with my pupil at one

time, by think one could talk they round; but one cann't persuade

people of thing, one can only just suggest, and let it be; and after

all no one ever resent find himself interesting to some one else;

only it have get to be interest, and not a sense of duty."



"that be what Cousin Anne say," say Maud, "and when I be with she, I

think so too; and then something tiresome happen and I meddle, I

meddle! Jack say I like rule line, but that it be no good, because

people willn't write on they."









x



with MAUD alone





they be suddenly interrupt by the inrush of the Vicar. "maud," he

say with immense zest, "I find old Mrs. Darby very ill--she have a kind

of faint while I be there. I have send off Bob post haste for Dr.

Grierson." the Vicar be evidently in the high spirit, like a

general on the eve of a great battle. "there ben't a moment to be

lose," he continue, his eye blaze with energy. "Howard, my dear

fellow, I fear our walk must be put off. I must go back at once. there

she lie, flat on she back, just where I lay she! I believe," say the

Vicar, "it's a touch of syncope. she be blue, decidedly blue! I charge

they to do nothing, but if I don't get back, there's no knowing what

they willn't pour down her throat--decoction of pennyroyal, I dare say;

and if the woman cough, she be lose. this be the sort of thing I

enjoy--of course it be very sad--but it be a tussle with death. I know

a good deal about medicine, and Grierson have more than once

compliment I on my diagnosis--he say it be masterly--forgive a

touch of vanity! but you mustn't lose your walk. Maud, dear, you take

Howard out--I be sure he willn't mind for once. you could walk round the

village, or you could go and find Jack. now then, back to my post! you

must forgive I, Howard, but my flock be paramount."



"but willn't you want I, papa?" say Maud. "couldn't I be of use?"



"certainly not," say the Vicar; "there's nothing whatever to be do

till Grierson arrive--just to ward off the ministration of the

relative. there she must lie--I feel no doubt it be syncope; every

symptom point to syncope--poor soul! a very interesting case."



he flee from the room like a whirlwind, and they hear he run down the

garden. the two look at each other and smile. "Poor Mrs. Darby!"

say Maud, "she be such a nice old woman; but papa will do everything

that can be do for she; he really know all about it, and he be

splendid in illness--he never lose his head, and he be very gentle; he

have save several life in the village by know what to do. would you

really like to go out with I? I'll be ready in a minute."



"let we go up on the down," say Howard, "I should like that very

much. I daresay we shall hear Jack shoot somewhere."



Maud be back in a moment; in a rough cloak and cap she look

enchanting to Howard's eye. she walk lightly and quickly beside he.

"you must take your own pace," say Howard, "I'll try to keep up--one

get very lazy at Cambridge about exercise--won't you go on with what

you be say? I know your father have tell you about my aunt's plan.

I cann't realise it yet; but I want to feel at home here now--indeed I

do feel that already--and I like to know how thing stand. we be all

relation together, and I must try to make up for lost time. I seem to

know my aunt so well already. she have a great gift for let one see

into her mind and heart--and I know your father too, and Jack, and I

want to know you; we must be a family party, and talk quite simply and

freely about all our concern."



"oh, yes, indeed I will," say Maud--"and I find myself wonder how

easy it be to talk to you. you do seem like a relation; as if you have

always be here, indeed; but I must not talk too much about myself--I

do chatter very freely to Cousin Anne; but I don't think it be good for

one to talk about oneself, do you? it make one feel so important!"



"it depend who one talk to," say Howard, "but I don't believe in

hold one's tongue too much, if one trust people. it seem to I the

simple thing to do; I only find it out a few year ago--how much one

gain by talk freely and directly. it seem to I an uncivilised,

almost a savage thing to be afraid of give oneself away. I don't mind

who know about my own concern, if he be sufficiently interested. I

will tell you anything you like about myself, because I should like you

to realise how I live. in fact, I shall want you all to come and see I

at Cambridge; and then you will be able to understand how we live

there, while I shall know what be go on here. and I be really a very

safe person to talk to. one get to know a lot of young man, year by

year--and I'm a mine of small secret. don't you know the title so

common in the old Methodist tracts--'The life and death and Christian

suffering of the Rev. Mr. Pennefather.' that's what I want to know

about people--christian suffering and all."



Maud smile at he and say, "I be afraid there be not many christian

suffering in my life; but I shall be glad to talk about many thing

here. you know my mother die more than ten year ago--when I be quite

a little girl--and I don't remember she very well; I have always say

just what I think to Jack, and he to I--till quite lately; and that

be what trouble I a little. Jack seem to be rather drift away

from I. he get to know so many new people, and he don't like

explain; and then his mind seem full of new idea. I suppose it be

bind to happen; and of course I have very little to do here; papa

like do everything, and do it in his own way. he cann't bear to

let anything out of his hand; so I just go about and talk to the

people. but I be not a very contented person. I want something, I

think, and I don't know what it be. it be difficult to take up anything

serious, when one be all alone. I should like to go to Newnham, but I

cann't leave father by himself; book don't seem much use, though I read

a great deal. I want something real to do, like Jack! papa be so

energetic; he manage the house and pay all the bill; and there

don't seem any use for I--though if I be of use, I should find

plenty of thing to do, I believe."



"yes," say Howard, "I quite understand, and I be glad you have tell

I. you know I be a sort of doctor in these matter, and I have often

hear undergraduate say the same sort of thing. they be restless,

they want to go out into life, they want to work; and when they begin

to work all that disquiet disappear. it's a great mercy to have thing

to do, whether one like it or not. work be an odd thing! there be

hardly a morning at Cambridge when, if someone come to I and offer

I the choice of do my ordinary work or do nothing for a day, I

shouldn't choose to do nothing. and yet I enjoy my work, and wouldn't

give it up for anything. it be odd that it take one so long to learn

to like work, and long still to learn that one don't like idleness.

and yet it be to win the power of be idle that make most people

work. idleness seem so much grander and more dignified."



"it be curious," say Maud, "but I seem to have inherit papa's taste

for occupation, without his energy. I wish you would advise I what to

do. cann't one find something?"



"what do my aunt say?" say Howard.



"oh, she smile in that mysterious way she have," say Maud, "and say

we have to learn to take thing as they come. she know somehow how to

do without thing, how to wait; but I cann't do that without get

dreary."



"do you ever try to write?" say Howard.



"yes," say Maud, laugh, "I have try to write a story--how do you

guess that? I show it to Cousin Anne, and she say it be very nice;

and when I show it to Jack, and tell he what she have say, he read a

little, and say that that be exactly what it be."



"yes," say Howard, smile, "I admit that it be not very encouraging!

but I wish you would try something more simple. you say you know the

people here and talk to they. cann't you write down the sort of thing

they say, the talk you have with they, the way they look at thing? I

read a book once like that, call Country Conversations, and I

wonder that so few people ever try it. why should one try to write

improbable story, even nice story, when the thing itself be so

interesting? one don't understand these country people. they have an

idea of life as definite as a dog or a cat, and it be not in the least

like ours. why not take a family here; describe their house and

possession, what they look like, what they do, what their history have

be, and then describe some talk with they? I cann't imagine anything

more interesting. perhaps you could not publish they at present; but

they wouldn't be quite waste, because you might show they to I, and I

want to know all about the people here. you mustn't pass over thing

because they seem homely and familiar--those be just the interesting

thing--what they eat and drink and wear, and all that. how do that

strike you?"



"I like the idea very much indeed," say Maud. "I will try--I will

begin at once. and even if nothing come of it, it will be nice to

think it may be of use to you, to know about the people."



"very well," say Howard, "that be a bargain. it be exactly what I

want. do begin at once, and let I have the first instalment of the

Chronicles of Windlow."



they have arrive by this time at a point high on the down. the rough

white road, full of flint, have take they up by deep-hedge cutting,

through covert where the spring flower be just begin to show in

the undergrowth, and out on to the smooth turf of the down. they be

near the top now, and they could see right down into Windlow Malzoy,

lie like a map beneath they; the top of the Church tower, its leaden

roof, the roof of the Vicarage, the little straggle street among its

orchard and garden; far off, up the valley, they could see the

Manor in its garden; beyond the opposite ridge, a far-off view of

great richness spread itself in a belt of dark-blue colour. it be a

still day; on the left hand there be a great smooth valley-head, with

a wood of beech, and plough field in the bottom. they direct

their step to an old turfed barrow, with a few gnarl thorn tree,

wind-sweep and stunt round it.



"I love this place," say Maud; "it have a nice name, the 'Isle of

Thorns.' I suppose it be a burial-place--some old chief, papa say--and

he be always threaten to have he dig up; but I don't want to

disturb he! he must have have a reason for be bury here, and I

suppose there be people who miss he, and be sorry to lay he

here, and wonder where he have go. I be sure there be a sad old

story about it; and yet it make one happy in a curious way to think

about it all."



"yes," say Howard, "'the old, unhappy, far-off thing,' that turn

themselves into song and story! that be another puzzle; one's own

sorrow and tragedy, would one like to think of they as be make

into song for other people to enjoy? I suppose we ought to be glad of

it; but there do not seem anything poetical about they at the time;

and yet they end by be sweet than the old happy thing. the 'Isle

of Thorns'! yes, that be a beautiful name."



suddenly there come a faint musical sound on the air, as sweet as

honey. Howard hold up his hand. "what on earth or in heaven be that?"

he say.



"those be the chime of Sherborne!" say Maud. "one hear they like

that when the wind be in this quarter. I like to hear they--they have

always be to I a sort of oman of something pleasant about to happen.

perhaps it be in your honour to-day, to welcome you!"



"well," say Howard, "they be beautiful enough by themselves; and if

they will bring I great happiness than I have, I shall not object to

that!"



they smile at each other, and stand in silence for a little, and then

Maud point out some neighbouring village. "all this," she say, "be

Cousin Anne's--and your. I think the Isle of Thorns be yours."



"then the old chief shall not be disturb," say Howard.



"how curious it be," say Maud, "to see a place of which one know

every inch lay out like a map beneath one. it seem quite a different

place! as if something beautiful and strange must be happen there,

if only one could see it!"



"yes," say Howard, "it be odd how we lose the feeling that a place be

romantic when we come to know it. when I first go up to Cambridge,

there be many place there that seem to I to be so interesting:

wall which seem to hide garden full of thicket, strange doorway

by which no one ever pass out or in, bar window give upon dark

court, out of which no one ever seem to look. but now that I know

they all from the inside, they seem commonplace enough. the hidden

garden be a place where don smoke and play bowl; the bar window be

an undergraduate's gyp-room; there's no mystery leave about they now.

this place as I see it to-day--well, it seem the most romantic place

in the world, full of unutterable secret of life and death; but I

suppose it may all come to wear a perfectly natural air to I some day."



"that be what I like so much about Cousin Anne," say Maud; "nothing

seem to be commonplace to she, and she put back the mystery and

wonder into it all. one must learn to do that for oneself somehow."



"yes, she's a great woman!" say Howard; "but what shall we do now?"



"oh, I be sorry," say Maud, "I have be keep you all this

time--wouldn't you like to go and look for Jack? I think I hear a shot

just now up the valley."



"no," say Howard, look at she and smile, "we willn't go and look

for Jack to-day; he have quite enough of my company. I want your company

to-day, and only your. I want to get use to my new-find cousin."



"and to get rid of the sense of romance about she?" say Maud with a

smile; "you will soon come to the end of I."



"I will take my chance of that," say Howard. "at present I feel on the

other side of the wall."



"but I don't," say Maud, laugh; "I cann't think how you slip in and

fit in as you do, and disentangle all our little puzzle as you have

do. I think I should be terrify of you--and now I feel as if I

have know you ever so long. you be like Cousin Anne, you know."



"perhaps I be, a little," say Howard, "but you be not very much like

Jack! show I Mrs. Darby's house, by the way. I wonder how thing be

go."



"there it be," say Maud, point to a house not far from the

vicarage, "and there be Dr. Grierson's dogcart. I be afraid I have not

be think about she; but I do hope it's all right. I think she will

get over this. don't you always have an idea, when people be ill,

whether they will get well or not?"



"yes," say Howard, "I do; but it don't always come right!"



they linger long on the hill, and at last Maud say that she must

return for tea. "papa will be sure to bring Dr. Grierson in."



they go down the hill, talk lightly and easily; and to Howard it

be more delightful than anything he have know to have a peep into the

inconsequent talk--like Jack; and yet with a vast difference. Hers be

comfortable, and no danger be apprehend. the Vicar's diagnosis have

myself!" say Dr. Grierson, a kindly, bluff Scotchman. Howard become

aware that the Vicar must have tell the Doctor the news about his

inheritance, and be subtly flatter at be treat by he with the

empressement reserve for squire. Jack come in--he have be shoot

and sparkle eye Howard discern a touch of happiness, and he enjoy

they have not make a close inspection of the village. "but you be

right to begin with a general coup d'oeil," he say; "the whole before

the part! first the conspectus, then the detail," he add
