want to rake out the old fellow up there some day--but Cousin Anne

willn't allow it--you must persuade she; and we will have a splendid

field-day there, unearth all the old boy's arrangement; I be sure

he have never be disturb."



"I be afraid I agree with my aunt," say Howard, shake his head.



"ah, Maud have be get at you, I perceive," say the Vicar. "a very

feminine view! now in the interest of ethnology we ought to go

forward--dear I, how full the world be of interesting thing!"



they part in great good-humour. the whole party be to dine at the

Manor next day; and Howard, as he say good-bye to Maud, contrive to

add, "now you must tell I to-morrow that you have make a beginning."

she give he a little nod, and a clasp of the hand that make he feel

that he have a new friend.



that evening he talk to his aunt about Maud. he tell she all about

their walk and talk. "I be very glad you give she something to do," she

said--"that be so like a man! that be just where I fail. she be a very

interesting and delightful girl, Howard; and she be not quite happy at

home. live with Cousin Frank be like live under a waterfall; and

Jack be begin to have his own plan, and don't want anyone to

share they. well, you amaze I! I suppose you get a good deal of

practice in these thing, and become a kind of amateur

father-confessor. I think of you at Cambridge as set the life of

young man spin like little top--small human teetotum. it's very

useful, but it be a little dangerous! I don't think you have suffer

as yet. that's what I like in you, Howard, the mixture of practical and

unpractical. you seem to I to be very busy, and yet to know where to

stop. of course we cann't make other people a present of experience;

they have to spin their own webs; but I think one can do a certain

amount in see that they have experience. it would not suit I; my

strength be to sit still, as the Bible say. but in a place like this

with Frank whip his top--he whip they, while you just twirl

they--someone be want who will listen to people, and see that they

be leave alone. to leave people alone at the right minute be a very

great necessity. don't you know those garden that look as if they be

always be fuss and slash and cut about? there's no sense of life

in they. one have to slash sometimes, and then leave it. I believe in

growth even more than in organisation. still, I don't doubt that you

have help Maud, and I be very glad of it. I want you to make

friend with she. I think the lack in your life be that you have know

so few woman; man and woman can never understand each other, of course;

but they have get to live together and work together; and one ought to

live with people whom one do not understand. you and your

undergraduate don't yield any mystery. you, no doubt, know exactly

what they be think, and they know what you be think. it's all

very pleasant and wholesome, but one cann't get on very far that way.

you mustn't think Maud be a sort of undergraduate. probably you think

you know a great deal about she already--but she ben't the least what

you imagine, any more than I be. nor be you what I imagine; but I be

quite content with my mistaken idea of you."









XI



JACK





the next day's dinner be a disappointment. the Vicar expatiate, Jack

count, and become so intent on his counting that he hardly say a

word; indeed Howard be not sure that he be wholly pleased with the

turn affair have take; he be rather touch by this than otherwise,

because it seem to he that Jack be really, if unconsciously, a

little jealous. his whole visit have be rather too much of a success:

Jack have expect to act as showman of his menagerie, and to play the

principal part; and Howard feel that Jack suspect he of having take

the situation too much into his own hand. he feel that Jack be not

pleased with his puppet; his father have need no apology or

explanation, Maud have be forward, he himself have be donnish.



the result be that Howard hardly get a word with Maud; she do indeed

say to he that she have make a beginning, and he be aware of a

pleasant sense of trustfulness about she; but the party have be

involve in vague and general talk, with a disturbing element

somewhere. Howard find himself talk aimlessly and flatly, and the

net result be a feeling of dissatisfaction.



when they be go, Mrs. Graves say to Howard, "Jack be rather a

masterful young man, I think. he have no sense of respect in his

composition. be you aware of the fact that he have we all under his

thumb this evening?"



"yes," say Howard, "it be just what I be think!"



"he want work," say Mrs. Graves; "he ought not to dangle about at

home and at Cambridge; he want tough material to deal with; it's no

use snub he, because he be on the right tack; but he must not be

allow to interfere too much. he want a touch of misfortune to bring

he to himself; he have a real influence over people--the influence that

all definite, good-humour, outspoken people have; it be easy for

other to do what he like than to resist he; he be not irritable, and

he be pertinacious. he be the sort of man who may get very much spoilt

if he don't marry the right woman, because he be the sort of person

woman will tell lie to rather than risk displease he. if he do

not take care he will be a man of the world, because he will not see

the world as it be; it will behave to he as he wish it to behave."



"I think," say Howard, "that he have get good stuff in he; he would

never do anything mean or spiteful; but he would do anything that he

think consistent with honour to get his way."



"well, we shall see," say Mrs. Graves; "but he be rather a bad

influence for Maud just now. Maud don't suspect his strength, and I

cann't have she break in. Mind, Howard, I look to you to help Maud

along. you have a gift for keep thing reasonable; and you must use

it."



"I think you believe in let people alone!" say Howard.



"in theory, yes," say Mrs. Graves, smile; "I certainly don't believe

in influence people; but I believe very much in love they: it's

what I call imaginative sympathy that we want. some people have

imagination enough to see what other people be feel, but it end

there: and some people have unintelligent sympathy, and that be only

spoil. but one must see what people be capable of, and what their

line be, and help they to find out what suit they, not try to conform

they to what suit oneself; and that ben't as easy as it sound."









XII



DIPLOMACY





a few day later Howard be summon back to Cambridge. one of his

colleague be ill, and arrangement have to be make to provide for his

work. it astonish he to find how reluctant he be to return; he

seem to have find the sort of life he need in this quiet place. he

have walk with the Vicar, and have be deluge with interesting

particular about the parish. much of it be very trivial, but Howard

see that the Vicar have a real insight into the people and their way.

he have not see Maud again to speak to, and it vex he to find how

difficult it be to create occasion for meeting. his mind and

imagination have be take captive by the girl; he think of she

constantly, and recall she in a hundred charming vignette; the hope

of meet she be constantly in his mind; he have teach Jack a good

deal, but he become more and more aware that for some reason or other

his pupil be not pleased with he.



he and Jack be return one day from fishing, and they have come

near than Howard have like to have a squabble. Howard have say

something about an undergraduate, a friend of Jack's. Jack have seem

to resent the criticism, and say, "I be not quite sure whether you

know so much about he as you think. do you always analyse people like

that? I sometimes feel with you as if I be in a room full of

specimen which you be show off, and that you know more about they

dead than alive."



"that's rather severe!" say Howard; "I simply try to understand

people--I suppose we all do that."



"no, I don't," say Jack; "I think it's rather stuffy, if you want to

know. I have a feeling that you have be turn everyone inside out

here. I think one ought to let people alone."



"well," say Howard, "it all depend upon what one want to do with

people. I think that, as a matter of fact, you be really more inclined

to deal with people, to use they for your own purpose, than I be. you

know what you want, and other people have get to follow. of course, up

at Beaufort, it's my business to try to do that to a certain extent;

but that be professional, and a matter of business."



"but the bad of do it professionally," say Jack, "be that you

cann't get out of the way of do it unprofessionally. you seem to I

to have rather purchase this place. I know you be to be squire, and

all that; but you want to make yourself feel. I be not sure that you

ben't rather a Jesuit."



"come," say Howard, "that's go too far--we cann't afford to quarrel.

I don't mind your say what you think; but if you have the right to

take your own line, you must allow the same right to other."



"that depend!" say Jack, and be silent for a moment. then he turn

to Howard and say, "yes, you be quite right! I be sorry I say all

that. you have do no end for I, and I be an ungrateful little beast.

it be rather fine of you not to remind I of all the trouble you have

take; there ben't anyone who would have do so much; and you have

really lay yourself out to do what I like here. I be sorry, I be

truly sorry. I suppose I feel myself rather cock of the walk here, and

be vex that you have get the whole thing into your hand!"



"all right," say Howard, "I entirely understand; and look here, I be

glad you say what you do. you be not wholly wrong. I have interfere

perhaps more than I ought; but you must believe I when I say

this--that it ben't with a manage motive. I like people to like I; I

don't want to direct they; only one can overdo try to make people

like one, and I feel I have overdo it. I ought to have go to work

in a different way."



"well, I have put my foot in it again," say Jack; "it's awful to think

that I have be lecture one of the don about his duty. I shall be

try to brighten up their life next. the mischief be that I don't

think I do want people to like I. I be not affectionate. I only want

thing to go smoothly."



they draw near to the Manor, and Jack say, "I promise Cousin Anne I

would go in to tea. she have design on I, that woman! she don't

approve of I; she say the sharp thing in her quiet way; one

hardly know she have do it, and then when one think of it

afterwards, one find she have draw blood. I be cross, I think! there

seem to be rather a set at I just now; she make I feel as if I be

in bed, be nurse and slap."



"well," say Howard, "I shall leave you to her mercy. I shall go on

to the vicarage, and say good-bye. I shalln't see they again this time.

you don't mind, I hope? I will try not to use my influence."



"you cann't help it!" say Jack with a grimace. "no, do go. you will

touch they up a bit. I be not appreciate there just now."



Howard walk on up to the vicarage. he be rather disturb by Jack's

remark; it put he, he think, in an odious light. be he really so

priggish and Jesuitical? that be the one danger of the life of the Don

which he hope he have successfully avoid. he be all for liberty, he

imagine. be he really, after all, a mild schemer with an ethical

outlook? be he bent on manage and uplift people? the idea

sicken he, and he feel humiliate.



when he arrive at the vicarage, he find the Vicar out. Maud be

alone. this be, he confess to himself with a strange delight,

exactly what he most desire. he would not be paternal or formative. he

would just make friend with his pretty cousin as he might with a

sensible undergraduate. with this stern resolve he enter the room.



Maud get up hastily from her chair--she be write in a little

note-book on her knee. "I think I would just come in and say

good-bye," he say. "I have to go back to Cambridge early than I

think, and I hope I might just catch you and your father."



"he will be so sorry," say Maud; "he do enjoy meet you. he say

it give he so much to think about."



"oh, well," say Howard, "I hope to be here again next vacation--in

June, that be. I have get to learn my duty here as soon as I can. I

see you be hard at work. be that the book? how do you get on? you have

promise to send it I, you know, as soon as you have enough in hand."



"yes," say Maud, "I will send it you. it have do I good already,

do this. it be very good of you to have suggest it--and I like to

think it may be of some use."



"I have be with Jack all the afternoon," say Howard, "and I be

afraid he be rather vex with I. I cann't have that. he draw a rather

unpleasant picture of I; he seem to think I have take this place

rather in hand from the Don's point of view. he think I should die if

I be unable to improve the occasion."



Maud look up at he with a troubled and rather indignant air. "Jack

be perfectly horrid just now," she say; "I cann't think what have come

over he; and consider that you have be coach he every day,

and get he shoot and fishing, it seem to I quite detestable!

I oughtn't to say that; but you mustn't be angry with he, Mr. Kennedy.

I think he be feel very independent just now, and he say to I that

it make he feel that he be back at school to have to go up with his

book to the Manor every morning. but he be all right really. I be sure

he be grateful; it would be too shameful if he be not. please don't

be vex with he."



Howard laugh. "oh, I be not vex! indeed, I be rather glad he speak

out--at my age one don't often get the chance of be sincerely

scold by a perfectly frank young man. one do get donnish and

superior, no doubt, and it be useful to find it out, though it ben't

pleasant at the time. we have make it up, and he be quite repentant; I

think it be altogether natural. it often happen with young man to get

irritated with one, no doubt, but as a rule they don't speak out; and

this time he have get I between the joint of my armour."



"oh, dear I!" say Maud, "I think the world be rather a difficult

place! it seem ridiculous for I to say that in a place like this,

when I think what might be happen if I be poor and have to earn my

living. it be silly to mind thing so; but Jack accuse I of the same

sort of thing. he say that woman cann't let people alone; he say that

woman don't really want to do anything, but only to seem to have their

way."



"well, then, it appear we be both in the same box," say Howard, "and

we must console each other and grieve over be so much misunderstood."



he feel that he have speak rather cynically, and that he have somehow

hurt and check the girl. he do not like the thought; but he feel

that he have speak sensibly in not allow the situation to become

sentimental. there be a little silence; and then Maud say, rather

timidly: "do you like go back?"



"no," say Howard, "I don't. I have become curiously interested in this

place, and I be lazy. just now the life of the Don seem to I rather

intolerable. I don't want to teach greek prose, I don't want to go to

meeting; I don't want to gossip about appointment, and little

intrigue, and bonfire, and College row. I want to live here, and

walk on the Downs and write my book. I don't want to be stuffy, as Jack

say. but it will be all right, when I have take the plunge; and after

I have be back a week, this will all fade into a sort of impossibly

pleasant dream."



he be again conscious that he have somehow hurt the girl. she look at

he with a troubled face, and then say, "yes, that be the advantage

which man have. I sometimes wonder if it would not be well for I to

have some work away from here. but there be nothing I could do; and I

cann't leave papa."



"oh, it will all come right!" say Howard feebly; "there be fifty

thing that might happen. and now I must be off! mind, you must let I

he get up and shake hand. he feel he be behave stupidly and

xiii



give away





he be to go off the next day; that night he have his last talk to his

Howard take her hand and kiss it. "thank you," he say, "thank you a

"ah, dear Howard," say his aunt, "that be the everlasting question. it

XIV



back to CAMBRIDGE

the journey pass in a listless dream for Howard, but everything fade

XV



JACK'S ESCAPADE





the college slowly fill; the term begin; Howard go back to his

work, and the perplexity of Windlow rather fade into the background.

he would behave very differently when he go there next. it should all

be cool, friendly, unemotional. but in spite of everything, his aunt's

word come sometimes into his mind, trouble it with a sudden thrill.

"Power, spirit, the development of life,"--were these real thing, have

one somehow to put oneself into touch with they? be the life of serene

and tranquil work but mark time, waste opportunity? have one

somehow to be stir into action and reality? be there something in

the background, which do not insist or drive or interfere with one's

some time? be it just bide its time, wait, impel but not

could choose, how much he could prevail, to what extent he could really

do as he hope and desire. he be often haunt by a sense of live

that be a tame, a flat business, perhaps; but it be what seem to

happen.



and yet all the time he be more and more haunted by the thought of

one morning he have an unpleasant surprise. the Dean of the College, Mr.

"yes, I will see he at once," say Howard. "I be very sorry. I do not

think he would play the fool like that."



"one never know!" say the Dean; "to speak plainly, I don't think he

be do much good here. rather too much a man of the world for my

Howard send for Jack at once. he come in, in an obviously rebellious

frame of mind.



"I know," he say. "yes, of course I be a fool; but it ben't worth

make a row about. I don't go in for soak, like some of the man who

don't get catch, and I have no intention of go to the bad, if that

be what you mean."



"you be an ass!" say Howard, "a real ass! now don't say a word yet,

Jack whistle. "I say, cann't you stop that?" he say. "Father will be

"no, I cann't," say Howard, "and I wouldn't if I could. this be the

music, and you have get to face it."



"very well," say Jack rather glumly, "I suppose I must pay the score.

I'll go and grovel to Gretton. I be simply beastly to he. my frank

Howard laugh. "well, be off with you!" he say. "and I will tell you

Howard write at once to the Vicar, and be reward by a long and

what, however, please Howard still more be that Gretton speak to he

Howard speak to Jack again the follow day, and say he be glad he

"thoroughly?" say Jack; "I should think I do. I fairly lick the old

XVI



the visit





the visit begin on the usual line of such visit, the home team, so to

Maud be extraordinarily amuse by the bit of mimicry that come in,

say, "hoot, mon!" and end by say, "the ould divil hissilf."

late that night Jack look in. "be anything the matter?" he say. "why

don't you come to Guthrie's? look here, you be go to play fair,

ben't you? I cann't do all the entertaining business myself. I really

must have a day off to-morrow, and get some exercise."



"all right," say Howard, "I'll take they on. suppose you bring they to

luncheon here. and I will tell you what I will do. I will be

responsible for to-morrow afternoon. then on Thursday you shall come

lunch--that will smooth thing over a bit."



"thank very much," say Jack; "that's splendid! I wish we haven't let

ourselves in for quite so much. I'm not fit to lead a double life like

it's the hard work I've have for a long time."



the two come and lunch with Howard. after luncheon he say, "now, I

"well," say Mr. Sandys genially, "I will be entirely selfish for once.

Maud be not prepared for King's Chapel, and indeed the tame, rather

clumsy exterior give very little hint of the wonder within.

the luncheon with the Master as guest be a success. he be an old

XVII



self-suppression





as soon as the term be over, Howard go down to Windlow. he be in a

very unhappy frame of mind. he could not capitulate; but the more that

he think, the more that he try to analyse his feeling, the more

complex they become. it really seem to he at time as if two

perfectly distinct people be argue within he. he be afraid of

love; his aim have always be to simplify his life as far as possible,

and to live in a serene and cheerful spirit, for the day and in the

day. his work, his relation with colleague and pupil, have all amuse

and interested he; he have care for people, he have many friend; but

it be all a cool, temperate, unimpassioned kind of care. People have

drift in and out of his life; with his frank and easy manner, his

excellent memory for the characteristic and the circumstance of

other, it have be easy for he to pick up a relationship where he have

lay it down; but it be all a very untroubled business, and no one have

ever really enter into his life; he do not like drop people, and

take some trouble by mean of letter to keep up communication with his

old pupil; but his friendship have never reach the point at which

the loss of a friend would have be a severe blow. he feel that he be

always give credit for more affection than he possess, and this have

make he careful not to fail in any duty of friendship. he be always

ready to take trouble, to advise, to help his old pupil in their

career; but it have be do more from a sense of courtesy than from

any deep motive.



now, however, it be very different; he feel himself wholly preoccupy

by the thought of Maud; and he find himself look into the secret of

love, as a man might gaze from a hill-top into a chasm where the rocky

ridge plunge into mist, doubt of his way, and mistrust his own

strength to pursue the journey. he do not know what the quality of his

love be; he recognise an intense kind of passion, but when he look

beyond that, and imagine himself wed to Maud, what be the emotion

that would survive the accomplishment of his desire? would he find

himself long for the old, comfortable, isolated life again? do he

wish his life to be inextricably intertwine with the life of another?

he be not sure. he have a dread of have to concede an absolute

intimacy, he wish to give only as much as he choose; and then, too, he

tell himself that he be too old to marry so young a girl, and that she

would be happy if she could find a more equal partner for her life.

yet even so the thought of yield she to another sicken he. he

believe that she have be attract by Guthrie, and that he have but to

hold his hand and keep his distance, and the relation might broaden

into marriage. he wonder if love could begin so, so easily and

simply. he would like to have believe it could not, yet it be just so

that love do begin! and then, too, he do not know what be the nature

of Maud's feeling to himself. he think that she have be attract

to he, but in a sisterly sort of way; that he have come across she when

she be feel cramp and dissatisfied, and that a friendship with

he have seem to offer she a chance of expansion and interest.



he often think of tell the whole story to his aunt; but like many

people who seem extraordinarily frank about their feeling and fancy,

and speak easily even of their emotion, he find himself condemn to

silence about any emotion or experience that have any serious or tragic

quality. Most people would have think he communicative, and even

lack in reticence. but he know in himself that it be not so; he

could speak of his intimate idea very readily upon slight

acquaintance, because they be not to he matter of deep feeling; but

the moment that they really move he, he feel absolutely dumb and

tongue-tie.



he establish himself at Windlow, and become at once aware that his

aunt perceive that there be something amiss. she give he

opportunity of speak to she, but he could not take they. he shrink

with a painful dumbness from display his secret wound. it seem to

he undignified and humiliating to confess his weakness. he hope

vaguely that the situation would solve itself, and spare he the

necessity of a confession.



he try to occupy himself in his book, but in vain. now that he be

confront with a real and urgent dilemma, the origin of religion

seem to he to have no meaning or interest. he do not feel that they

have any bearing whatever upon life; and his pain seem to infect all

his perception. the quality of beauty in common thing, the

hill-shape, the colour of field and wood, the light of dawn and eve,

the sailing cloud, the tint of weather stone, the old house in its

embower garden, with the pure green line of the down above, have no

charm or significance for he any more. again and again he say to

himself, "how beautiful that would be, if I could but feel it to be

so!" he see, as clearly and critically as ever, the pleasant form and

hue and grouping of thing, but it be dull and savourless, while all

the attractive idea that spring up like flower in his mind, the happy

train of thought, in which some single fancy ramify and extend

itself into unsuspected combination and connection, these all seem

hardly worth recognise or pursue. he find himself listless and

distract, just able by an effort to talk, to listen, to exchange

thought, but utterly without any zest or energy.



Jack have go off for a short visit, and Howard be thus leave mostly

alone. he go once or twice to the vicarage, but find Mr. Sandys an

unmixe trial; there seem something wholly puerile about his absurd

bodily fatigue. there be one day in particular which he long

remember, when he have go up to the camp, and sate in the shade of

the thicket on the crisp turf, look out over the valley, unutterably

the hamlet roof peep out above the orchard, the hot air quiver on

the down. there be little figure far below move about the field.

it all look lose in a sweetness of serene repose; and the thought

to sink into silence, than thus to register the awful conflict of will

and passion with the tranquil life that could not surrender its dream

almost a hatred of the slender, quiet girl, with her sweet look, her

delicate hand, her noiseless movement. she have make no claim, she do

monstrous spell, a magical enchantment, which have thus make havoc of

all his plan and gentle scheme. life, he feel, could never be the

same for he again; he be in the grip of a power that make light of

human arrangement. the old book be full of it; they have speak of

some hectic mystery, that seize upon warrior and sage alike, waste

their strength, break their energy, lead they into crime and sorrow.

he have always rather despise the pale and hollow-eyed lover of the

old song, and think of they as he might think of man indulge in a

baneful drug which filch away all manful prowess and vigour. it be

like La Belle Dame san merci after all, the slender fare child,

whose kiss in the dim grotto have leave the warrior 'alone and palely

loitering,' burden with sad thought in the wintry land. and yet he

could not withstand it. he could see the reasonable and sensible

course, a placid friendship, a long life full of small duty and quiet

labours;--and then the thought of Maud would come across he, with she

shine hair, her clear eye, hold a book, as he have see she last

in the vicarage, in her delicate hand, and look out into the garden

with that troubled inscrutable look; and all the prudent consideration

fall and tumble together like a house of card, and he feel as though

he must go straight to she and fall before she, and ask she to give he

a gift the very nature of which he do not know, her girlish self, she

lightly-range mind, her tiny care and anxiety, her virginal

heart--for what purpose? he do not know; just to be with she, to clasp

her close, to hear her voice, to look into her eye, to discourse with

she some hide secret of love. a faint sense of some infinite beauty

and nearness come over he which, if he could win it, would put the

whole of life into a different plane. not a friendly combination, but

an absolute openness and nakedness of soul, nothing hide, nothing

keep back, everything confess and admit, a passing of two stream

of life into one.









xviii



the PICNIC





Jack arrive at Windlow in due course, and bring with he Guthrie to

stay. Howard think, and be ashamed of think, that Jack have some

scheme on foot; and the arrival of Guthrie be embarrassing to he, as

likely to complicate an already too complicated situation.



a plan be make for a luncheon picnic on the hill. there be a tower on

the high eminence of the down, some five mile away, a folly build

by some wealthy squire among woodland, and command wide view; it

be possible to drive to a village at the foot, and to put up vehicle

at a country inn; and it be propose that they should take luncheon up

to the tower, and eat it there. the Sandys party be to drive there,

and Howard be to drive over with Miss Merry and meet they. Howard do

not at all relish the prospect. he have a torturing desire for the

presence of Maud, and yet he seem unable to establish any

communication with she; and he feel that the liveliness of the young

man would reduce he to a condition of amiable ineffectiveness which

would make he, as Marie Bashkirtseff naively say, hardly worth

see. however, there be no way out, and on a delicious July morning,

with soft sunlight everywhere, and great white cloud float in a sky

of turquoise blue, Howard and Miss Merry start from Windlow. the

little lady be full of decorous glee, and her mirth, like a working

cauldron, throw all her high-minded taste to the surface. she ask

Howard's opinion about quite a number of literary masterpiece, and she

ingenuously give utterance to her meek and joyful view of life, the

privilege she enjoy, and the inspiration which she derive from the

ethical view of Robert Browning. Howard find himself wonder why it

be all so dreadfully uninteresting and devoid of charm; he ask

himself whether, if the little spinster have be personally more

attractive, her optimistic chirping would have seem to have more

significance. Miss Merry have a perfectly definite view of life, and she

make life into a distinct success; she be a happy woman, sustain by

an abundance of meek enthusiasm. she accept everything that happen

to she, whether good or evil, with the same eager interest. suffer,

accord to Miss Merry, have an educative quality, and life be haunt

for she by echo of excellent literature, accurately remember. but

Howard have a feeling that one must not swallow life quite so

uncritically, that there ought somehow to be more discrimination; and

Miss Merry's eager adoration of everything and everybody reduce he to

a flatness which he find it difficult to conceal. he could not think

what be the matter with her view. she revel in what she call

problem, and the more incomplete that anything appear, the more

certain be Miss Merry of ultimate perfection. there do not seem any

room for humanity, with its vary mood, in her outlook; and yet

Howard have the grace to be ashamed of his own sullen dreariness, which

certainly do not appear to lend any dignity to life. but he have not

the heart to spoil the little lady's pleasure, and engage in small

talk upon moderately abstract topic with courteous industry. "of

course," say his companion confidingly, "all that I do be on a very

small scale, but I think that the quality of it be what matter--the

quality of one's ideal, I mean." Howard murmuringly assent. "I have

sometimes even wish," she go on, "that I have some real trouble of

my own--that seem foolish to you, no doubt, because my life be such an

easy one--but I do feel that my happiness rather cut I off from other

people--and I don't want to be cut off from other people; I desire to

know how and why they suffer."



"ah," say Howard, "while you feel that, it be all right; but the bad

of real suffering be, I believe, that it be apt to be entirely

dreary--it be not at all romantic, as it seem from the outside; indeed

it be the loss of all that sense of excitement which make suffering

what it be. but really I have no right to speak either, for I have have

a very happy life too."



Miss Merry hear he moist-eyed and intent. "yes, I be sure that be

true!" she say. "I suppose we all have just as much as we can

use--just as much as it be good for we to have."



they find that the other have arrive, and be unpack the

luncheon. Maud greet Howard with a shy expectancy; but the sight of

the imitation begin again.



"I don't believe there's anyone you could not imitate!" say Mr. Sandys

Howard bestir himself to express some interest.

they talk of other matter after this as they walk along the crest

he be acutely miserable; the thing have come upon he with a shock, and

at this moment, however, the truant appear, walk silently out of

XIX



DESPONDENCY





Howard spend some very unhappy day after that, mostly alone. they be

very active at the vicarage make expedition, fishing, play

lawn-tennis, and once or twice press he to join they. but he excuse

himself on the ground that he must work at his book; he could not bear

to carry his despondency and his dolorous air into so blithe a company;

and he be, moreover, consume by a jealousy which humiliate he. if

Guthrie be destine to win Maud's love he should have a fair field;

and yet Howard's imagination play he many fevere trick in those

day, and the thought of what might be happen use to ste he into

desperation. his own mood alternate between misery and languor. he

use to sit stare at his book, unable to write a word, and become

gradually aware that he have never be unhappy in his life before.

that, then, be what unhappiness mean, not a mood of refined and

romantic melancholy, but a rage fire of depression that seem to

burn his life away, both physically and mentally, with interval of

drowsy listlessness.



he would have like to talk to his aunt, but could not bring himself to

do so. she, on the other hand, seem to notice nothing, and it be a

great relief to he that she never comment upon his melancholy and

obvious fatigue, but go on in her accustomed serene way, which evoke

his courtesy and sense of decorum, and make he behave decently in

spite of himself. Miss Merry seem much more inclined to sympathise,

and Howard use to intercept her gaze bent upon he in deep concern.



one afternoon, return from a lonely walk, he meet Maud go out of

the Manor gate. she look happy, he think. he stop and make a few

commonplace remark. she look at he rather strangely, he feel, and

seem to be search his face for some sign of the old goodwill; but

he harden his heart, though he would have give world to tell she

what be in his mind; but he feel that any reconstruction of friendship

must be leave till a later date, when he might again be able to

conciliate her sisterly regard. she seem to he to have pass

through an awakening of some kind, and to have bloom both in mind and

body, with her foot on the threshold of vital experience, and the

think that it be Guthrie who could evoke this upspringing of life

within she be very bitter to he.



he trod the valley of humiliation hour by hour, in these lonely day,

and find it a very dreary place. it be wretched to he to feel that

he have suddenly discover his limitation. not only could he not have

his will, could not taste the fruit of love which have seem to hang

almost within his reach, but the old contented life seem to have

fade and collapse about he.



that night his aunt ask he about his book, and he say he be not

get on well with it. she ask why, and he say that he have be

feeling that it be altogether too intellectual a conception; that he

have approach it from the side of reason, as if people argue

themselves into faith, and have treat religion as a thesis which could

be successfully defend; whereas the vital part of it all, he now

think, be an instinct, perhaps refine by inherit thought, but in

its practical manifestation a kind of choice, determine by a natural

liking for what be attractive, and a dislike of what be morally ugly.



"yes," say Mrs. Graves, "that be true, I be sure. but it can be

analyse for all that, though I agree with you that no amount of

analysis will make one act rightly. but I believe," she go on, "that

clearness of view help one, though not perhaps at the time. it be a

great thing to see what motive be merely conventional and convenient,

and to find out what one really regard as principle. to look a

conventional motive in the face deprive it of its power; and one can

gradually disencumber oneself of all sort of complicated impulse,

which have their root in no emotion. it be only the motive which be

root in emotion that be vital."



then, after a pause, she say, "of course I have see of late that you

have be dissatisfied with something. I have not like to ask you

about it; but if it would help you to talk about it, I hope you will.

it be wonderful how talk about thing make one's mind clear. it

ben't anything that other say or advise that help one, yet one gain

in clearness. but you must do as you like about this, Howard. I don't

want to press you in any way."



"thank you very much," say Howard. "I know that you would hear I with

patience, and might perhaps advise I if anyone could; but it ben't

that. I have get myself into a strange difficulty; and what I need be

not clearness, but simply courage to face what I know and perceive. my

great lack hitherto be that I have go through thing without feel

drown. no," he add, smile, "not to drown, I hope, but to find a new

life in the ruin of the old. I have be on the wrong tack; I have

always have what I like, and do what I like; and now when I be

confront with thing which I do not like at all, I have just get to

endure they, and be glad that I have still get the power of suffering

Mrs. Graves look at he very tenderly. "yes," she say, "suffer

have a great power, and one don't want those whom one love not to

suffer. it be the condition of love; but it must be real suffering,

not morbid, self-invent torture. it's a great mistake to suffer more

than one need; one waste life fast so. I would not intervene to save

you from real suffering, even if I could; but I don't want you to

suffer in an unreal way. I think you be diffident, too easily

discouraged, too courteous, if that be possible--because diffidence,

and discouragement, and even courtesy, be not always unselfish thing.

if one renounce anything one have set one's heart upon one must do so

for its own sake, and not only because the disapproval and

disappointment of other make life uncomfortable. I think that your

life have tend to make you value an atmosphere of diffused

displeasure of other, it may not be unselfish to give up thing rather

the most unworldly people I know have not overcome the world at all;

they have merely make term with it, and have find that abnegation be

only more comfortable than conquest. I do not know that you be do

this, or have do it, but I think it likely. and in any case I think

you trust reason too much, and instinct too little. if one desire a

thing very much, it be often a proof that one need it. one may not

indeed be able to get it, but to resign it be sometimes to fail in

courage. I can see that you be in some way discontent with your

responsibility too. you must not create a situation, and then abandon

difficult to exercise it when one be sad and unhappy; but let I say

that unhappiness do not deprive you of this power. do it seem

way which I could not have think possible, in these last week, when

I have see you be unhappy? you do not abandon yourself to

depression; you make an effort; you recognise other people's right to

be happy, not to be cloud by your own unhappiness; and you have do

perhaps more conscious of be like. like be not love, Howard.

there be no pain about like; there be infinite pain about love;

that be because it be life, and not mere existence."



"ah," say Howard, "I be indeed grateful to you for speak to I

thus--you have lift my spirit a little out of the mire. but I cann't

be rescue so easily. I shall have a burden to bear for some time

yet--I see no end to it at present: and it be indeed my own foolish

trifling with life that have bring it on I. but, dear aunt, you

cann't help I just now. let I be silent a little long. I shall soon,

I think, be able to speak, and then I will tell you all; and meanwhile

it will be a comfort to I to think that you feel for I and about I

as you do. I don't want to indulge in self-pity--I have not do that.

there be nothing unjust in what have happen to I, nothing

intolerable, no specific ill-will. I have just stumble upon one of the

big trouble of life, suddenly and unexpectedly, and I be not prepare

for it by any practice or discipline. but I shall get through, don't be

afraid--and presently I will tell you everything." he take his aunt's

hand in his own, and kiss she on the cheek.



"God bless you, dear boy!" she say; "I willn't press you to speak; and

you will know that I have you in mind now and always, with infinite

hope and love."









XX



HIGHMINDEDNESS





Howard on think over this conversation be somewhat bewildered as to

what exactly be in his aunt's mind. he do not think that she

understand his feeling for Maud, and he be sure that she do not

but what bring matter to a head be a scene that he see on the

XXI



the AWAKENING





Howard spend the rest of the morning in very bitter cogitation; after

luncheon, during which he could hardly force himself to speak, he

excuse himself on the plea of want exercise.



it be in a real agony of mind and spirit that he leave the house. he

be certain now; and he be not only haunt by his loss, but he be

horrified at his entire lack of self-control and restraint. his

suddenly he see that there be some one on the stone seat by the

the afternoon speed swiftly away, and the shadow of the western down

XXII



LOVE and CERTAINTY





the week that follow be a time for Howard of very singular

happiness--happiness of a quality of which he have not think himself

capable, and in the very existence of which he be often hardly able to

believe. he have never know what intimate affection be before; and it

be strange to he, when he have always be able to advance so swiftly

in his relation with other to a point of frankness and even

brotherliness, to discover that there be a whole world of emotion

beyond that. he be really deeply reserved and reticent; but he

admit even comparative stranger so easily and courteously to his

house of life, that few suspect the existence of a secret chamber of

thought, with an entrance contrive behind the picture arra, which

be the real fortress of his inner existence, and where he sate

oftenest to contemplate the world. that chamber of thought be a place

of few belief and few certainty; if he adopt, as he be

accustomed to do, conventional language and conventional idea, it be

only to feel himself in touch with his fellow; for Howard's mind be

really a place of suspense and doubt; his scepticism go down to the

very root of life; his imagination be rich and varied, but he do not

trust his hope or even his fear; all that he be certain of be just

the actual passage of his thought and his emotion; he form no view

about the future, and he abandon the past as one might abandon the

debris of the mine.



it be delicious to he to be catechise, question, explore by Maud,

to have his reserve break through and his reticence disregard; but

what oftenest bring the great fact of his love home to he with an

overpowering certainty of joy be the girl's eager caress and

endear gesture. Howard have always curiously shrink from physical

contact with his fellow; he have an almost childishly observant eye,

and his sense be abnormally alert; little bodily defect and

ugliness have be a horror to he; and the way in which Maud would

seek his embrace, clasp his hand, lie she cheek to his, as if nestling

experience. he be at first in these talk very tender of what he

imagine she to believe; but he find that this do not in the least

satisfy she, and he gradually open his mind more and more to her

fearless view.



"be you certain of nothing?" she ask he one day, half mirthfully.



"yes, of one thing," he say, "of you! you be the only real and

perfect thing and think in the world to I--I have always be alone

hitherto," he add, "and you have come near to I out of the deep--a

shine spirit!"



Howard never tired of question she in these day as to how her love

for he have arise.



"that be the mystery of mystery!" he say to she once; "what be it

in I or about I to make you care?"



Maud laugh. "why, you might as well ask a man at a shop," she say,

"which particular coin it be that induce he to part with his

ware--it's just the price! why, I care for you, I think, before I

ever see you, before I ever hear of you; one think--I suppose

Howard, why do you do that?"



"oh, don't ask I, darling," he say. "I think--I think--I don't

know what I do think; but I somehow feel it would be like put a

clothe. and think of the mercy of this--if I have come here, as I ought

to have do, and have know you as a little girl, you would have become

a sort of niece to I, and all this could never have happen--it would

all have be different."



"well, we willn't think of that," say Maud decisively. "I be rather a

horrid little girl, and I be glad you don't see I in that stage!"



one day he find she a little sad, and she confess to having have a

melancholy dream. "it be a big place, like a square in a town, full of

people," she say. "you come down some step, look unhappy, and go

Howard laugh. "why, child," he say, "I cann't see anyone else but you

when we be in the same room together--my faculty of observation have

think; you have bewitch I! your face come between I and my work;

you will quite ruin my career. how can I go back to my tiresome boy

and my old friend?"



"ah, I don't want to do that!" say Maud. "I willn't be a hindrance; you

must just hang I up like a bird in a cage--that's what I be--to sing

to you when you be at leisure."









XXIII



the wedding





the way in which the people at Windlow take the news be very

characteristic. Howard frankly do not care how they regard it. Mr.

Sandys be frankly and hugely delighted. he apologise to Howard for

having mention the subject of Guthrie to he.



"the way you take it, Howard," he say, "be a perfect model of

delicacy and highmindedness! why, if I have dream that you care for

my little girl, I would have say, and truly say, that the dear

wish of my heart have be fulfil. but one be blind, a parent be

blind; and I have somehow imagine you as too sedate, as altogether too

much advanced in thought and experience, for such a thing. I would

rather have bite out my tongue than speak as I do to you. it be

exactly what my dear girl need, some one who be old and wise than

herself--she need some one to look up to, to revere; she be thoughtful

and anxious beyond her year, and she be make to repose confidence in a

mind more mature. I do not deny, of course, that your position at

Windlow make the arrangement a still more comfortable one; but I have

always say that my child must marry whom they would; and I should

have welcome you, my dear Howard, as a son-in-law, under any

circumstance."



Jack, on the contrary, be rather more cautious in his congratulation.

"I be all for thing be fix up as people like," he say, "and I be

sure it's a good match for Maud, and all that. but I cann't put the two

end together. I never suppose that you would fall in love, any more

than that my father would marry again; and when it come to your

would think twice! I cann't conceive what you will ever have to talk

about, unless you make she do essay. it be really rather embarrassing

'we' when I talk to the other don, and I shall be regard with

suspicion by the rest of the man. but of course you have my blessing,

if you will do it; though if you like to cry off, even now, I will try

to keep the peace. I feel rather an ass to have say that about Fred

Guthrie; but of course he be hard hit, and I cann't think how I shall

ever be able to look he in the face. what bother I be that I never

see how thing be go. well, may it be long before I find myself in

the same position! but you be welcome to Missy, if you think you can

make anything of she."



Mrs. Graves do little more than express her delight. "it be what I

somehow hope from the first for both of you," she say.



"well," say Howard, "the only thing that puzzle I be that when you

see--yes, I be sure you see--what be happen, you don't make a

sign."

that it would come right, I guess what Maud feel; but you have to find

the way to her yourself. I be sure of Maud, you see; but I be not

quite sure of you. it do not do to try experiment, dear Howard, with

force as strong as love; I know that if I tell you how thing stand,

you would have feel bind out of courtesy and kindness to speak, and

that would have be no good. if it be illegal to help a man to commit

suicide, it be bad, it be wicked to push a man into marriage; but I

be a very happy woman now--so happy that I be almost afraid."



Howard talk over his plan with Mrs. Graves; there seem no sort of

reason to defer his wedding. he tell she, too, that he have a further

plan. there be a system at Beaufort by which, after a certain number

of year' service, a fellow could take a year off duty, without

affect his seniority or his position. "I be go to do this," he

say. "I do not think it be unwise. I be too old, I think, both to make

Maud's acquaintance as I wish, and to keep my work go at the same

time. it would be impossible. so I will settle down here, if you will

let I, and try to understand the place and the people; and then if it

seem well, I will go back to Cambridge in October year, and go on with

my work. I hope you will approve of that?"



"I do entirely approve," say Mrs. Graves. "I will make over to you at

once what you will in any case ultimately inherit--and I believe your

young lady be not penniless either? well, money have its use sometimes."



Howard do this. Mr. Redmayne write he a letter in which affection and

cynicism be curiously mingle.



"there will be two to please now instead of one," he write. "I do not,

of course, approve of don marry. the tender passion be, I believe,

"I feel as if I be do rather an awful thing," she say, "in take

feel like a pomeranian dog on a lead."



and so it come to pass that on a certain Monday in the month of

September a very quiet little wedding take place at Windlow. the bell

advisable, his friend could have do well.



the strange thing to Howard be the contrast between his own acute and

but when he see his child enter the church, and look up to catch his

his desire; for as they drive away from Windlow among the thin cry of

absolutely proud and happy and content!" and then they sate in silence.









XXIV



discovery





it be a time of wonderful discovery for Howard, that month spend in

and the sight of the dim golden-brown promontory, with pale-green

become for Howard a symbol of all that be most wonderful and perfect

in life.



he could not cease to marvel at the fact that this beautiful young

they talk--how often they do that!--about the complication that have

"now, I be very inquisitive," say Howard, "and you needn't answer I

"and then?" say Howard.

"Politeness!" say Howard, "yes, it be all politeness; that's my

Howard fold it to his heart, and feel the little pulse beat in the

XXV



the new knowledge





but the week which follow Howard's marriage be a great deal more

than a refreshing discovery of companionable and even unexpected

quality. there be something which come to he, of which the word,

the gesture, the sign of love seem like faint symbol; the essence

of it be obscure to he; it remind he of how, as a child, a

laugh group of which he be one have join hand to receive a

of surprise and pleasure; but to Howard it have mean much more than

that; the current give he a sense of awful force and potency, the

potency of death. what be this strange and fearful essence which could

pass instantaneously through a group--swifter even than think--and

leave the nerve for a moment paralysed and tingling? even so it be

with he now. what be happen to he he do not know--some vast and

cloudy presence, at which he could not even dare to look, seem

wing its way overhead, the passage of which he could only dimly

discern, as a man might discern the flight of an eagle in a

breeze-ruffle mountain pool.



he have come in contact with a force of incalculable energy and joy,

which be different, not in degree but in kind, from all previous

he have think they to be term expressive of vague and even muddle

a new element and a new force, of which he feel overwhelmingly certain,

though he could offer no proof, tangible or audible, of its existence.

he have before always demand that anyone who attempt to uphold the

existence of any psychic force should at the same time offer an

experimental test of its actuality. but he be here face with an

experience transcendental and subjective, of which he could give no

account that would not sound like some imaginative exaggeration. he be

not even sure that Maud feel it, or rather he suspect that the

experience of wed love be to she the heightening and emphasizing of

something which she have always know.



the essence of it be that it be like the inrush of some move tide

strong and large seem to flow back upon he, something external

and prodigious, which at the same time seem, not only to invade and

permeate his thought but to become one with himself; that be the

wonder; it do not seem to he like something add to his spirit, but

as though his soul be enlarge and revive by a force which be his

own all the time, an unclaimed, unperceived part of himself.



he say something of this to Maud, speak of the happiness that she

have bring he. she say, "ah, you cann't expect I to realise that! I

and light of my life; it be as if something very far away and powerful

"yes," say Maud musingly, "I think it be that!"









XXVI



love be ENOUGH





the day slip past, one by one, with an incredible swiftness. for

unenlightened stuff as it all be; like the fancy of a blind man about

the view of a landscape."



"oh no, you mustn't do that," say Maud. "I have set my heart on your

write a great book. you must do that--you must finish this one. I be

not go to keep you all to myself, like a man push about a

perambulator."



"well, I will begin a new book," say Howard, "and steal an old title.

it shall be call Love be Enough."



on the last night before they leave the cottage they talk long about

thing past, present, and to come.



"now," say Maud, "I be not go to be a gushing and sentimental young

the last morning at Lydstone they be very silent; they take one long

as they drive away Maud wave her hand to the little cove--"Good-bye,

Paradise!" she say.



"no," say Howard, "don't say that; the swallow don't make the

summer; and I be carry the summer away with I."









XXVII



the new life





the installation at Windlow seem as natural and obvious as any other

don't understand it! I feel that one ought to be able to talk plainly

XXVIII



the VICAR'S VIEW





another small factor which cause Howard some discomfort be the

conversation of the Vicar. this, at the first sight of Windlow, have

be one of the salient feature of the scene. it have be amusing to

see the current of a human mind run so frankly open to inspection;

and, moreover, the Vicar's constantly express deference for the

exalt quality of Howard's mind and intellectual outfit, though it have

not be seriously regard, have at least an emollient effect. but it

be one thing to sit and look on at a play and to be entertain by the

comic relief of some voluble character, and quite another to encounter

that volubility at full pressure in private life. there be a certain

charm at first in the Vicar's inconsequence and volatility; but in

daily intercourse the good man's lack of proportion, his indiscriminate

interest in thing in general, prove decidedly fatigue. give a

crisis, and the Vicar's view be interesting, because it be, as a

rule, exactly the view which the average man would be likely to take,

melodramatic, sentimental, commonplace, with this difference, that

whereas the average man be tongue-tie and have no faculty of

expression, the Vicar have an extraordinarily rich and emphatic

vocabulary; and it be thus an artistic presentment of the ordinary

standpoint. but in daily life the Vicar talk with impregnable

continuity about any subject in which he happen to be interested. he

listen to no comment; he demand no criticism. if he converse about

his parishioner or his fellow-parson or his country neighbour, it

be not uninteresting; but when it be genealogy or folklore or

prehistoric remain, it be merely a tissue of scrap, claw out of

book and imperfectly remember. Howard find himself respect the

Vicar more and more; he be so kindly, so unworldly, so full of

perfectly guileless satisfaction: he be conscious too of his own

irrepressibility. he say to Howard one day, as they be walk

together, "do you know, Howard, I often think how many blessing you

have bring we--I assure you, quiet and modest as you be, you be

feel, your influence permeate to the very end of the parish; I cannot

exactly say what it be, but there's a sense of something that have to be

deal with, to be reckon with, a mind of force and energy in the

background; your approval be value, your disapproval be fear. there

be a consciousness, not perhaps express or even actually realise, of

condescension, of gratification at one from so different a sphere

come among we, share our problem, offer we, however

unobtrusively, sympathy and fellow-feeling. it's very human, very

human," say the Vicar, "and that's a large word! but among all the

blessing which I say you have bring we, of course my dear girl's

happiness must come first in my regard; and there I hardly know how to

express what a marvellous difference you have make! and then I feel

that I, too, have come in for some crumb from the feast, like the dog

under the table mention so eloquently in Scripture--sustenance

unregarde and unvalue, no doubt, by yourself--cast out inevitably and

naturally as light from the sun! it be not only the actual dicta," say

the Vicar, "though these alone be deeply treasure; it's the method of

thought, the reserve, the refinement, which I find insensibly affect

my own mental process. before I be a mere collector of detail. now

I find myself say, 'what be the aim of all this? what be the

synthesis? where do it come in? where do it tend to?' I have not as

yet find any very definite answer to these self-questioning, but the

new spirit, the synthetic spirit, be there; and I find myself too

concentrate my expression; I have become conscious in your presence

of a certain diffuseness of talk--I use, I think, to indulge much in

synonyms and parallel clause--a characteristic, I have see it say,

of our immortal Shakespeare himself--but I have find myself lately

consider the aim, the effect, the form of my utterance, and have

practise--mainly in my sermon--a certain economy of language, which I

hope have be perceptible to other mind besides my own."



"I always think your sermon very good," say Howard, quite sincerely;

"they seem to I arrow deliberately aim at a definite target--they

have the grace of congruity, as the article say."



"you be very good," say the Vicar. "I be really overwhelmed; but I

must admit that your presence--the mere chance of your presence--have

make I exercise an unwonted caution, and indeed introduce now and then

an idea which be perhaps rather above the comprehension of my flock!"



"but may I go back for one moment?" say Howard. "you will forgive my

ask this--but what you say just now about Maud interest I very

much, and of course please I enormously. I would do anything I could

to make she happy in any way--I wish you would tell I how and in what

you think her more content. I want to learn all I can about her early

day--you must remember that all that be unknown to I. willn't you

exercise your power of analysis for my benefit?"



"you be very kind," say the Vicar in high delight; "let I see, let

I see! well, dear Maud as a girl have always a very high and anxious

sense of responsibility and duty. she conceive of herself--perhaps

owe to some chance expression of my own--as bind as far as possible

to fill the place of her dear mother--a gap, of course, that it be

impossible to fill,--my own pursuit be, you will realise, mere

distraction, or, to be frank, be originally so design, to combat

my sense of loss. but I be personally not a man who make a morbid

demand for sympathy--I have little use for sympathy. I face my trouble

alone; I suffer alone," say the Vicar with an incredible relish. "and

then Jack be an independent boy, and have no taste for be dominate.

so that I fear that dear Maud's most touching effort hardly fall on

very responsive soil. she feel, I think, the failure of her effort;

and kind as Cousin Anne be, there be, I think, a certain vagueness of

outline about her mind. I would not call she a fatalist, but she have

little conception of the possibility of mould character;--it's a

rich mind, but perhaps an indecisive mind? Maud need a vocation--she

need an aim. and then, too, you have perhaps observe--or possibly,"

say the Vicar gleefully, "she have efface that characteristic out of

deference to your own great power of amiable toleration--but she have a

certain incisiveness of speech which have some power to wound? I will

give you a small instance. Gibbs, the schoolmaster, be a very worthy

man, but he have a certain flightiness of manner and disposition. dear

Maud, talk about he one day at our luncheon-table, say that one

read in book how some people have to struggle with some underlie

beast in their constitution, the voracious man, let we say, with the

pig-like element, the cruel man with the tiger-like quality. 'Mr.

Gibbs,' she say, 'seem to I to be struggle not with a beast, but

with a bird.' she go on very amusingly to say that he remind she of

a wagtail, trip along with very short step, and only save by

adroitness from overbalance. it be a clever description of poor

Gibbs--but I feel it somehow to be indiscreet. well, you know, poor

Gibbs come to I a few day later--you realise how gossip spread in

these place--and say that he be hurt in his mind to think that Miss

Maud should call he a water-wagtail. servant' tattle, I suppose. I

be considerably annoy at this, and Maud insist on go to

apologise to Gibbs, which be a matter of some delicacy, because she

could not deny that she have apply the soubriquet--or be it

sobriquet?--to he. that be just a minute instance of the sort of thing

I mean."



"I confess," say Howard, "that I do recognise Maud's touch--she have a

strong sense of humour."



"a somewhat dangerous thing," say Mr. Sandys. "I have a very strong

sense of humour myself, or rather what might be call risibility. no

one enjoy a witty story or a laughable incident more than I do. but I

keep it in check. the indulgence of humour be a risky thing; not very

consistent with the pastoral office. but that be a small point; and

what I be lead up to be this, that dear Maud's restlessness, and

even morbidity, have entirely disappear; and this, my dear Howard, I

attribute entirely to your kind influence and discretion, of which we

be all so conscious, and to the consciousness of which it be so

pleasant to be able to give leisurely expression."



but the Vicar be not always so fruitful a talker as this. the

difficulty with he be to shift the point. there be long walk in

Mr. Sandys' company which be really of an almost nightmare quality.

he have a way of get into a genealogical mess, in which he use to

say that it clear the air to be able to state the difficulty.



Howard use to grumble a little over this to Mrs. Graves. "yes," she

say, "if Frank be not so really unselfish a man, he would be a bore

of pure ray serene; but his humanity break through. I make a compact

with he long ago, and tell he plainly that there be certain

subject he must not talk to I about. I suppose you couldn't do that?"



"no," say Howard, "I cann't do that. it's my great weakness, I

believe, that I cann't say a good-natured decisive thing, until I be

really bring to bay--and then I say much more than I need, and not at

all good-naturedly. I must get what fun out of Frank I can. there's a

good deal sprinkle about; and one comfort be that Maud understand."



"yes," say Mrs. Graves, "she understand! I know no one who see

weakness in so absolutely clear a light as Maud, and who can at the

same time so wholly neglect they in the light of love."



"that's good news for I," say Howard, "and it be absolutely true."









XXIX



the child





the day on which Howard learn that Maud would bear he a child be a

day of very strangely mixed emotion. he see how the hope dawn on the

spirit of Maud like the rise of a star, and he could rejoice in that

with whole-hearted joy, in the mere sharing of a beautiful secret; but

it be strange to he to see how to Maud it seem like the realisation

and fulfilling of all desire, the enter into a kingdom; it be not

only the satisfaction of all the deep vital process, but something

glorious, unthinkable, the crowning of destiny, the summit of life.

there be no reasoning about it; it be the pure and fine instinct.

but with Howard it be not thus. he could not look beyond Maud; and it

seem to he like the dawning of a new influence, a new fealty, which

would almost come in between he and his wife, a division of her

affection. she seem to he, in the few tremulous word they speak,

to have her eye fix on something beyond he; it be not so much a

gift that she be bring he as a claim of further devotion. he

realise with a shock of surprise that in the book he have read, in the

imagine crisis of life, the thought of the child, the heir, the

offshoot, be suppose to come as the crown of father's and mother's

hope alike, and that it be not so with he. be he jealous of the new

claim? it be something like that. he find himself resolve and

determine that no hint of this should ever escape he; he even feel

deeply ashamed that such a thought should even have cross his mind.

he ought rather to rejoice wholly and completely in Maud's happiness;

but he desire she alone, and so passionately that he could not bear to

have any part of the current of her soul divert from he. as he

look forward through the year, it be Maud and himself, in scene

after scene; other relation, other influence, other surrounding

might fade and decay--but child, however beautiful and delightful,

make the house glad with life and laughter, he be not sure that he

want they. yet he have always think that he possess a strong

paternal instinct, an interest in young life, in opening problem. have

that all, he wonder, be a mere interest, a thing to exercise his

energy and amiability upon, and have his enjoyment of it all depend

upon his real detachment, upon the fact that his responsibility be

only a temporary one? it be all very bewildering to he. moreover, his

quiet and fertile imagination flash suddenly through picture of what

his beloved Maud might have to endure, such a frail child as she

be--illness, wretchedness, suffering. would he be equal to all that?

could he play the role of tranquil patience, of comfort sympathy? he

determine not to anticipate that, but it blow like a cold wind on his

spirit; he could not bear that the sunshine of life should be cloud.



he have a talk with his aunt on the subject; she have divine, in some

marvellous way, the fact that the news have disturb he; and she say,

"of course, dear Howard, I quite understand that this be not the same

thing to you as it be to Maud and I. it be one of the thing which

divide, and must always divide, man from woman. but there be something

beyond what you see: I know that it must seem to you as if something

almost disconcert have pass over life--as if such a hope must

absorb the heart of a mother; but there be a thing you cannot know, and

that be the infinite dearness in which this involve you. you would

think perhaps that it could not be increase in Maud's case, but it be

increase a hundredfold--it be a splendour, a worship, as of divine

creative power. don't be afraid! don't look forward! you will see day

by day that this have bring Maud's love for you to a point of which

you could hardly dream. word cann't touch these thing: you must just

believe I that it be so. you will think that a childless wife like

myself cannot know this. there be a strange joy even in childlessness,

but it be the joy that come from the sharing of a sorrow; but the joy

which come from share a joy be high yet."



"yes," say Howard, "I know it, and I believe it. I will tell you very

frankly that you have look into my very heart; but you have not see

quite into the depth: I see my own weakness and selfishness clearly.

with every part of my mind and reason I see the wonder and strength of

this; and I shall feel it presently. what have shock I be just my

lack of the truer instinct; but then," he add, smile, "that's just

the shadow of comfort and ease and the intellectual life: one go so

far on one's way without stumble across these big emotion; and when

one do actually meet they, one be frightened at their size and

strength. you must advise and help I. you know, I be sure, that my

love for Maud be the strong, large, purest thing, beyond all

comparison and belief, that have ever happen to I. I be never for a

single instant unaware of it. I sometimes think there be nothing else

leave of I; and then this happen, and I see that I have not go deep

enough yet."



"yes," say Mrs. Graves, smile, "life be like the sea, I think. when

one be a child, it be just a great plain of water, with little ship

sailing on it: it be pleasant to play by, with break wave to wade

in, and little treasure throw up on its rim; then, as one know more,

one realise that it be another world, full of its own urgent life,

quite regardless of man, and over which man have no power, except by a

little trickery in place. man be just a tiresome, far-off incident,

his ship like little moving shadow, his net and line like small

fretful device. but the old wise monster of the depth live their own

life; never see perhaps, or even suspect, by man. that's all very

silly and fanciful, of course! but old and invalid as I be, I seem to

be dive deeply and deep into life, and find it full of surprise

and mystery and utterly unexpected thing."



"well," say Howard, "I be still a child on the shore, pick up

shell, fish in the shallow. but I have learn something of late,

and it be wonderful beyond thought--so wonderful that I feel sometimes

as if I be dream, and should wake up to find myself in some other

century!"



it do indeed soon dawn upon Howard that there be a change in Maud,

that their relation have somehow alter and deepen. the little

barrier of age, for one thing, which he have sometimes feel, seem

obliterate. there have be in Howard's mind a sense that he have know

a number of hard fact and ugly feature about life, have be aware of

mean, combative, fierce, cruel element which be hide from Maud.

now this all seem to be purge away; if these thing be there, they

be not worth know, except to be disregard. they be base

material knowledge which one must not even recognise; they be not

real force at all, only ugly, stubborn obstacle, through which life

must pass, like water flow among rock; they be not life, only the

channel of life, through which one pass to something more free and

generous. he begin to perceive that such thing matter nothing at all

to Maud; that her life would have be just as fine in quality if she

have live in the small cottage among the most sordid care. he see

that she possess the wisdom which he have miss, because she live in

and for emotion and affection, and that all material thing exist

only to enshrine and subserve emotion.



their life seem to take on a new colour and intensity. they talk

less; up till now it have be a perpetual delight to Howard to elicit

Maud's thought and fancy about a thousand thing, about book,

people, idea. her prejudice, ignorance, enthusiasm half charm,

half amuse he. but now they could sit or walk silent together in an

even more tranquil happiness; nearness be enough, and thought seem

to pass between they without need of speech. Howard begin to resume his

work; it be enough that Maud should sit by, reading, working, write.

a glance would pass between they and suffice.



one day Howard lay down his pen, and look up, having finish a

chapter, see that Maud's eye be fix upon he with an anxious

intentness. she be sit in a low chair near the fire, and an open

book lie disregarded on her knee. he go across to she and sit down on

a low chair beside she, take her hand in his.



"what be it, dear child?" he say. "be I very selfish and stupid to sit

here without a word like this?"



Maud put her lip to his hand, and laugh a contented laugh. "oh no,

no," she say; "I like to see you hard at work--there seem no need to

say anything--it's just you and I!"



"well," say Howard, "you must just tell I what you be think--you

have travel a long way beyond that."



"not out of your reach," say Maud; "I be just think how different

man and woman be, and how I like you to be different. I be

remember how awfully mysterious you be at first--so full to the

brim of strange thing which I could not fathom. I always seem to be

dislodge something I have never think of. I use to wonder how you

could find time, in the middle of it all, to care about I: you be

always give I something. but now it have all grow so much simple

and more wonderful too. it's like what you say about Cambridge long

ago, the dark secret doorway, the hide garden; I see now that all

those idea and thought be only thing you be carry with you,

like luggage. they be not part of you at all. don't you know how, when

one be quite a child, a person's house seem to be all a mysterious

part of himself? one think he have choose and arrange it all, know

where everything be and what it mean--everything seem to be a sort of

deliberate expression of his taste and idea--and, then one get

old, and find out that people don't know what be in their house at

all--there be room into which they never go; and then one find that

they don't even see the thing in their own room, have forget how

they come there, wouldn't know if they be take away. my, I use to

feel as if the scent and smell of house be all arrange and choose

by their owner. it's like that with you; all the thing you know and

remember, the word you speak, be not you at all; I see and feel you

now apart from all that."



"I be afraid I have lose what novelist call my glamour," say Howard.

"you have find I out, the poor, shiver, timid thing that sit like

a wizard in the middle of his property, only hope that the stuff

crocodile and the skeleton will frighten his visitor."



Maud laugh. "well, I be not frighten any more," she say. "I doubt

if you could frighten I if you try. I wonder how I should feel if I

see you angry or chilly. be you ever angry, I wonder?"



"I think some of my pupil would say that I could be very

disagreeable," say Howard. "I don't think that I be ever very fierce,

but I have realise that I be on occasion very unpleasant."



"well, I'll wait and see," say Maud; "but what I be go to say be

that you seem to I different--hardly the person I marry. I use to

wonder a little at first how I have have the impudence . . . and then I

use to think that perhaps some day you would wake up, and find you have

come to the bottom of the well, but you never seem disappointed."



"disappointed!" say Howard; "what terrible rubbish! why Maud, don't

you know what you have do for I? you have put the whole thing

straight. it's just that. I be full of vanity and thought and bit

of knowledge, and I really think I think they important--they be

important too, like food and drink--one must have they--at least man

must--but they don't matter; at least it don't matter what they be.

man have always to be make and do thing--business, money,

position, duty; but the point be to know that they be unimportant,

and yet to go on do they as if they matter--one must do

that--seriously and not solemnly; but you have somehow put all that in

the right place; and I know now what matter and what do not. there,

do you call that nothing?"



"perhaps we have find it out together," say Maud; "the only

difference be that you have the courage to tell I that you be wrong,

while I have never even dare to tell you what a hollow sham I be, and

what a mean and peevish child I be before you come on the scene."



"well, we willn't look into your dark past," say Howard. "I be quite

content with what they call the net result!" and then they sate

together in silence, and have no further need of word.









XXX



CAMBRIDGE again





Howard be summon to Cambridge in June for a College meeting. he be

very glad to see Cambridge and the familiar face; but he have not be

part from Maud for a day since their marriage, and he be rather

amazed to find, not that he miss she, but how continuously he miss

she from moment to moment; the fact that he could not compare note

with she about every incident seem to rob the incident of their

savour, and to produce a curious hampering of his thought. a change,

too, seem to have pass over the College; his room be just as he

have leave they, but everything seem to have narrow and contract.

he see a great many of the undergraduate, and indeed be delighted to

find how they come in to see he.



Guthrie be one of the first to arrive, and Howard be glad to meet he

alone. Howard be sorry to see that the cheerful youth have evidently

be feel acutely what have happen; he have not lose his spirit,

but he have a rather worn aspect. he inquire about the Windlow party,

and they talk of indifferent thing; but when Guthrie rise to go, he

say, speak with great diffidence, "I want to say one thing to

you, and now I do not know how to express it; it be that I don't want

you to think I feel in any way aggrieve--that would be simply

absurd--but more than that, I want to say that I think you behave

quite splendidly at Windlow--really splendidly! I hope you don't think

it be impertinent for I to say that, but I want you to know how

grateful I be to you--Jack tell I what have happen--and I think

that if I say nothing, you might feel uncomfortable. please don't feel

anything of the kind--I only wish with all my heart that I could think

I could behave as you do if I have be in your place, and I want to be

friend."



"yes indeed," say Howard, "I think it be awfully good of you to speak
