about it. you willn't expect I," he add, smile, "to say that I wish

it have turn out otherwise; but I do hope you will be happy, with all

my heart; and you will know that you will have a real welcome at

Windlow if ever you care to come there."



the young man shake hand in silence with Howard, and go out with a

smile. "oh, I shall be all right," he say.



Jack sate up late with Howard and treat he to a long grumble.



"I do hope to goodness you will come back to Cambridge," he say. "you

must simply make Maud come. you must use your influence, your beautiful

influence, of which we hear so much. seriously, I do miss you here very

much, and so do everybody else. your pupil be in an awful stew.

they say that you get they through the trip without bore they, and

that Crofts bore they and willn't get they through. this place rather

get on my nerve now. the don don't confide in I, and I don't see

thing from their angle, as my father say. I think you somehow manage

to keep they reasonable; they be narrow-minded man, I think."



"this be rather a shower of compliment," say Howard. "but I think I

very likely shall come back. I don't think Maud would mind."



"mind!" say Jack, "why you wind that girl round your little finger.

she write about you as if you be an archangel; and look here, I be

sorry I take a gloomy view. it's all right; you be the right person.

Freddy Guthrie would never have do for Maud--he's in a great way

about it still, but I tell he he may be thankful to have escape. Maud

be a mountain-top kind of girl; she could never have get on without a

lot of aspiration, she couldn't have settle down to the country-house

kind of life. you be a sort of privilege, you know, and all that;

Freddy Guthrie would never have be a privilege."



"that's rather a horror!" say Howard; "you mustn't let these thing

out; you make I nervous!"



Jack laugh. "if your brother-in-law mayn't say this to you, I don't

know who may. but seriously, really quite seriously, you be a big

person than I think. I'll tell you why. I have a kind of feeling that

you ought not to let I speak to you as you do, that you ought to have

snap my head off. and then you seem too much upset by what I say.

I don't know if it be your tact; but you have your own way all the

time, with I and with everybody; you seem to give way at every

point, and yet you carry out your programme. I think you haven't

much backbone--there, the cat's out; and now I find that we be all

dance to your music. I like people to do that, and it amuse I to

find that I dance as obediently as anyone, when I really think I

could make you do as I wish. I admire your way of go on: you make

everyone think that you value their opinion, and yet you know exactly

what you want and get it."



Howard laugh. "I really be not such a diplomatist as that, Jack! I be

not a humbug; but I will tell you frankly what happen. what people say

and think, and even how they look, do affect I very much at the

time; but I have a theory that most people get what they really want.

one have to be very careful what one want in this world, not because

one be disappoint, but because Providence hand it one with a smile;

and then it often turn out to be an ironical gift--a punishment in

disguise."



"Maud shall hear that," say Jack; "a punishment in disguise--that will

do she good, and take she down a peg or two. so you have find it out

already?"



"my dear Jack," say Howard, "if you say anything of the kind, you will

repent it. I be not go to have Maud bother just now with any

nonsense. do you hear that? the frankness of your family be one of its

great charm--but you don't quite know how much the frankness of

babe and suckling can hurt--and you be not to experiment on Maud."



Jack look at Howard with a smile. "here's the real man at last--the

tyrant's vein! of course, I obey. I don't really mean it; and I like

to hear you speak like that; it's rather fine."



presently Jack say, "now, about the Governor--rather a douche, I

expect? but I see you can take care of yourself; he's hugely

delighted--the intellectual temperature rise in every letter I get

from he. but I want to make sure of one thing. I'm not go to stay

on here much long. I don't want a degree--it ben't the slight use,

plain or colour. I want to get to work. if you come up again next

term, I can stand it, not otherwise."



"very well," say Howard, "that's a bargain. I must just talk thing

over with Maud. if we come up to Cambridge in October, you will stay

till next June. if we don't, you shall be plant in the business. they

will take you in, I believe, at any time, but would prefer you to

finish your time here."



"yes, that's it," say Jack, "but I want work: this be all right, in a

way, but it's mostly piffle. how all these johnny can dangle on, I

don't know; it's not my idea of life."



"well, there's no hurry," say Howard, "but it shall be arrange as you

wish."









XXXI



make the best of it





Howard become aware that with his colleague he have suddenly become

rather a person of importance. his "place" in the country be hold in

some dim way to increase the grandeur of the College. he find himself

defer to and congratulate. Mr. Redmayne be both caustic and

affectionate.



"you look very well, I must say," he say. "you have a touch of the

land personage about you which become you. I should like you to come

back here for our sake, but I shalln't press it. and how be Madam? I

hope you have got rid of your first illusion? no? well you must make

haste and be reasonable. I be not learn in the vagary of feminine

temperament, but I imagine that the fair sex like to be dominate, and

you will do that. you have a light hand on the rein--I always say

that you ride the boy on the snaffle, but the curb be there! and in

matrimony--well, well, I be an old bachelor of course, and I have a

suspicion of all noose. never mind my nonsense, Kennedy--what I like

about you, if I may say so, be that you have authority without

pretension. People will do as you wish, just to please you; now I have

always to be crack the whip. these fellow here be very worthy man,

but they be not man of the world! they be honest and sober--indeed

one can hardly get one of they to join one in a glass of port--but they

be limited, very limited. now if only you could have keep clear of

matrimony--no disrespect to Madam--what a comfortable time we might

have have here! man appoint and God disappoint--I suppose it be all

for the good."



"well," say Howard, "I think you will I see back here in October--my

wife be quite ready to come, and there ben't really much for I to do

at Windlow. I believe I be to be on the bench shortly; but if I live

there in the vacation, that will be enough; and I don't feel that I

have finish with Beaufort yet."



"Excellent!" say Mr. Redmayne. "I commend Madam's good sense and

discretion. pray give she my regard, and say that we shall welcome she

at Cambridge. we will make the good of it--and I confess that in your

place--well, if all woman be like Madam, I could view marriage with

comparative equanimity--though of course, I make the statement without

prejudice."









XXXII



HOWARD'S PROFESSION





when Howard come back from Cambridge he have a long talk with Maud over

the future; it seemed almost tacitly agree that he should return to

his work there, at all event for a time.



"I feel very selfish and pompous about all this," say Howard; "MY

work, MY sphere--what nonsense it all be! why should I come down to

Windlow, take possession, and having pick the sweetest flower in the

garden, stick it in my buttonhole and march away?"



Maud laugh and say, "oh, no, it ben't that--it be quite a simple

matter. you have learn a trade, a difficult trade; why should you give

it up? we don't happen to need the money, but that don't matter. my

business be to take off your shoulder, if I can, all the trouble

entail on you by marry I--it's simply a division of labour. you

cann't just settle down in the country as a small squire, with nothing

much to do. People must do the work they can do, and I should be

miserable if I think I have pull you out of your place in the world."



"I don't know," say Howard; "there seem to I to be something rather

stuffy about it: why cann't we just live? woman do; there be no fuss

make about their work, and their need to express themselves; yet they

do it even more than man, and they do it without priggishness. my work

at Cambridge be just what everyone else be do, and if I don't do it,

there will be half a dozen man capable of do it and glad to do it.

the great man of the world don't talk about the importance of their

work: they just do whatever come to hand--it's only the second-rate

man who say that their talent haven't full scope. do you remember poor

Chambers, who be at lunch the other day? he tell I that he have

migrate from a town parish to a country parish, and that he miss the

organisation so much. 'there seem nothing to organise down in the

country!' he say. 'now in my town parish there be the whole machine

to keep go--I enjoy that, and I don't feel I be give effect to

the good part of myself.' that seem to I such a pompous line, and I

feel that I don't want to be like that. one's work! how little it

matter! no one be indispensable--the disappearance of one man just

give another his chance."



"yes, of course, it be rather hard to draw the line," say Maud, "and I

think it be a pity to be solemn about it; but it seem to I so simple

in this case. you can do the work--they want you back--there be no

reason why you should not go back."



"perhaps it be mere laziness," say Howard, "but I feel as if I want

a different sort of life now, a quieter life; and yet I know that there

be a snare about that. I rather mistrust the people who say they must

get time to think out thing. it's like the old definition of

metaphysic--the science of muddle oneself systematically. I don't

think one can act by reason; one must act by instinct, and reason just

prevent one's make a fool of oneself."



"I believe the time for the other life will come quite naturally

later," say Maud. "at your age, you have get to do thing. of course

it's the same with woman in a way, but marriage be their obvious

career, and the pity be that there don't seem enough husband to go

round. I can sit in my corner and placidly survey the overstock

market now!"



Howard get up and lean against the chimneypiece, survey his wife

with delight. "ah, child," he say, "I be lucky to come in when I do.

I shiver at the thought that if I have arrive a little later there

would have be 'no talk of thee and I' as Omar say. you would have

be a devoted wife, and I should have be a hopeless bachelor!"



"it's unthinkable," say Maud, "it's horrible even to speculate about

such thing--a mere question of proximity! well, it cann't be mend

now; and the result be that I not only drive you back to work, but you

have to carry I back as well, like Sindbad and the old man of the sea."



"yes, it's just like that!" say Howard.



he make several attempt, with Mr. Sandys and with his aunt--even with

Miss Merry--to get encouragement for his plan; but he could obtain no

sympathy.



"I'm sick of the very word 'ideal,'" he say to Maud. "I feel like a

waiter handing about tumbler on a tray, press people to have

ideal--at least that be what I seem to be suppose to be do. I

haven't any ideal myself--the only thing I demand and practise be

civility."



"yes, I don't think you need bother about ideal," say Maud, "it's

wonderful the depressing power of word; there be such a lot of fine

and obvious thing in the world, perfectly distinct, absolutely

necessary, and yet the moment they become professional, they deprive

one of all spirit and hope--Jane have that effect on I, I be afraid. I

be sure she be a fine creature, but her view always make I feel

uncomfortable--now Cousin Anne take all the thing one need for

grant, and ben't above make fun of they; and then they suddenly

appear wholesome and sensible. she be quite clear on the point; now if

she want you to stay, it would be different."



"very well, so be it!" say Howard; "I feel I be catch in feminine

toil. I be like a child be teach to walk--every step applaud,

hand on from embrace to embrace. I yield! I will take my beautiful

mind back to Cambridge, I will go on mould character, I will go on

suggest high motive. but the responsibility be yours, and if you

turn I into a prig, it will not be my fault."



"ah, I will take the responsibility for that," say Maud, "and, by the

way, haven't we well begin to look out for a house? I cann't live in

College, I believe, not even if I be to become a bedmaker?"



"yes," say Howard, "a high-minded house of roughcast and tile, with

plenty of white paint inside, chippendale chair, Watts engraving. I

have come to that--it's inevitable, it just express the situation;

but I mustn't go on like this--it ben't funny, this academic

irony--it's dreadfully professional. I will be sensible, and write to

an agent for a list. it have well just be 'a house' with nothing

distinctive; because this will be our home, I hope, and that the

official residence. and now, Maud, I willn't be tiresome any more; we

cann't waste time in talk about these thing. I haven't do with

make love to you yet, and I doubt if I ever shall!"









XXXIII



ANXIETY





the month move slowly on, a time full of deepen strain and anxiety

to Howard. Maud herself seem serene enough at first, full of hope;

she begin to be more dependent on he; and Howard perceive two thing

which give he some solace; in the first place he find that, sharp as

the tension of anxiety in his mind often be, he do not realise it as

a burden of which he would be merely glad to be rid. he have an

instinctive dislike of all painful strain thing--of

responsibility, disagreeable duty, thing that disturb his

tranquillity; but this anxiety do not come to he in that light at

all; he long that it should be over, but it be not a thing which he

desire to banish from his mind; it be all bind up with love and

happy anticipation; and next he learn the joy of do thing that

would otherwise be troublesome for the sake of love, and find they all

transmute, not into seemly courtesy, but into sharp and urgent

pleasure. to be of use to Maud, to entertain she, to disguise his

anxiety, to compel himself to talk easily and lightly--all this

fill his soul with delight, especially when he find as the month

go on that Maud begin to look to he as a matter of course; and

though Howard have be use to say that be read aloud to be the

only occupation in the world that be bad than read aloud, he

find that there be no great pleasure than in read to Maud day by

day, in find book that she care for.



"if only I could spare you some of this," he say to her one day,

"that's the awful thing, not to be able to share the pain of anyone

whom one love. I feel I could hold my hand in the fire with a smile,

if only I know that it be save you something!"



"ah, dearest, I know," say Maud, "but you mustn't think of it like

that; it interest I in a curious way--I cann't explain--I don't feel

helpless; I feel as if I be do something worth the trouble!"



at last the time draw near; it be hot, silent, airless weather; the

sun lie fiercely in the little valley, day by day; one morning they

be sit together and Maud suddenly say to he, "Dearest, one

thing I want to say; if I seem to be afraid, I be not afraid: will you

remember that? I want to walk every step of the way; I mean to do it, I

wish to do it; I be not afraid in my heart of heart of anything--pain,

or even bad; and you must remember that, even if I do not seem to

remember!"



"yes," say Howard, "I will remember that; and indeed I know it; you

even take away my own fear when you speak so; love take hand beneath

it all."



but on the follow morning--Maud have a restless and suffering

night--Mrs. Graves come in upon Howard as he try to read, to tell he

that there be great anxiety, Maud have have a sudden attack of pain; it

have pass off, but they be not reassure. "the doctor will be here

presently," she say. Howard rise dry-lipped and haggard. "she send

you her dear love," she say, "but she would rather be alone; she

don't wish you to see she thus; she be absolutely brave, and that be

the good thing; and I be not afraid myself," she add: "we must just

wait--everything be in her favour; but I know how you feel and how you

must feel; just clasp the anxiety close, look in its face; it's a

bless thing, though you cann't see it as I do--bless, I mean, that

one can feel so."



but the fear thicken after this. a carriage draw up, and Howard see

two doctor descend, carry bag in their hand. his heart sicken

within he, yet he be help by see their unembarrassed and

cheerful air, the nod that one of they, a big, fresh-faced man, give to

the coachman, the look he cast round the beautiful old house. People

could think of such thing, Howard see, in a moment like that. he go

down and meet they in the hall, and have that strange sense of unreality

in moment of crisis, when one hear one's own voice say courteous

thing, without any volition of one's own. the big doctor look at he

kindly. "it be all quite simple and straightforward!" he say. "you

must not let yourself be anxious; these time pass by and one wonder

afterwards how one could have be so much afraid."



but the hour bring no relief; the doctor stay long in the house;

something have occur, Howard know not what, do not dare to

conjecture. the silence, the beauty of the whole scene, be

insupportably horrible to he. he walk up and down in the afternoon,

gaze at Maud's window--once a nurse come to the window and open it

a little. he go back at last into the house; the doctor be there,

talk in low tone to Mrs. Graves. "I will be back first thing in the

morning," say one; the bad, then, have not happen. but as he

appear a look of inquiry pass between they and Mrs. Graves. she

beckon to he.



"she be very ill," she say; "it be over, and she have survive; but the

child be dead."



Howard stand blankly stare at the group. "I don't understand," he

say; "the child be dead--yes, but what about Maud?"



the doctor come up to he. "it be sudden," he say; "she have an

attack--we have anticipate it--the child be bear dead; but there be

every reason to believe that she will recover; it have be a great

shock, but she be young and strong, and she be full of pluck--you need

not be anxious at present; there be no imminent danger." then he add,

"Mr. Kennedy, get some rest yourself; she may need you, and you must

not be useless: I tell you, the first danger be over and will not

recur; you must just force yourself to eat--try to sleep."



"Sleep?" say Howard with a wan smile, "yes, if you could tell I how

to do that!"



the doctor depart; Howard go off with Mrs. Graves. she make he

sit down, she tell he a few detail; then she say, "dearest boy, it's

no use waste word or pity just now--you know what I feel; I would

tell you plainly if I fear the bad. I do not fear it, and now let

I exercise my art on you, for I be sure I can help you a little. one

must not play with these thing, but this be in earnest."



she come and sate down beside he, and stroke his hair, his brow; she

say, "just try, if you can, to cast everything out of your mind; relax

your limb, be entirely passive; and don't listen to what I say--just

let your mind float free." presently she begin to speak in a low voice

to he; he hardly heed what she say, for a strange drowsiness

settle down upon he like the in-flowing of some oblivious tide, and

he know no more.



a couple of hour later he awake from a deep sleep, with a sense of

sweet vision and experience--he look round. Mrs. Graves sate beside

he smile, but the horror suddenly dart back into his mind with a

spasm of fear, as if he have be bite by a poisonous serpent.



"what have be happen?" he say.



"ah," say Mrs. Graves quietly, "you have be asleep. I have some

power in these thing, which I don't use except in time of need--some

day I will tell you more; I find it out by accident, but I have use

it both for myself and other. it's just a natural force, of which many

people be suspicious, because it don't seem normal; but don't be

afraid, dear boy--all go well; she be sleep quietly, and she know

what have happen."



"thank you," say Howard; "yes, I be well; but I could almost wish I

have not sleep--I feel the pain of it more. I don't feel just now as if

anything in the world could make up for this--as if anything could make

it seem just to endure such misery. what have one do to deserve it?"



"what indeed?" say Mrs. Graves, "because the time will come when you

will ask that in a different sense. don't you see, dear boy, that even

this be life's fulness? one mustn't be afraid of suffering--what one

must be afraid of be not suffer; it's the measure of love--you would

not part with your love if that would free you from suffer?"



"no," say Howard slowly, "I would not--you be right. I can see that.

one bring the other; but I cannot see the need of it."



"that be only because one do not realise how much lie ahead," say

Mrs. Graves. "be content that you know at least how much you

love--there's no knowledge like that!"









XXXIV



the DREAM-child





for some day Howard be in an intolerable agony of mind about Maud;

she lie in a sort of stupor of weakness and weariness, recognise no

one, hardly speak, just alive, indifferent to everything. they could

not let he be with she, they would allow no one to speak to she. the

shock have be too great, and the frail life seem flicker to its

close: once or twice he be just allow to see she; she lie like a

tired child, her head on her hand, lose in incommunicable dream.

Howard dared not leave the house, and the tension of his nerve become

so acute that the least thing--a servant enter the room, or anyone

come out to speak with he as he pace up and down the garden--cause

he an insupportable horror; have they come to summon he to see the

end? the frightful thing be the silence, the blank silence of the one

he love well. if she have moan or weep or complain, he could have

bear it well; but she seem entirely withdraw from he. even when

a little strength return, they fear for her reason. she seem

unaware of where she be, of what have happen, of all about she. the

night be the bad time of all. Howard, utterly weary out, would go

to bed, and sink into sleep, sleep so profound that it seem like

descend into some deep and oblivious tide; then a current of misery

would mingle with his dream, a sense of unutterable depression; and

then he would suddenly wake in the grip of fear, formless and bodiless

fear. the small sound in the house, the creaking of a door, a

footfall, would set his heart beat with fierce hammer stroke. he

would light his candle, wander restlessly about, gaze out from his

window into the blackness of the garden, where the tree outline

themselves against the dark sky, pierce with star; or he would try to

read, but wholly in vain. no thought, no imagination seem to have any

meaning for he, in the presence of that rage dread. have he, he

wonder, come in sight of the ultimate truth of life? the pain he

suffer seem to he the strong thing in the world, strong than

love, strong than death. the thick tide of the night sweep past he

thus, till the light begin to outline the window cranny; and then

there be a new day to face, with fail brain and shattered strength.



the only comfort he receive be in the presence of his aunt. she alone

seem strong, almost serene, till he wonder if she be not hard. she

do not encourage he to speak of his fear: she talk quietly about

ordinary thing, not demand an answer; she see the doctor, whom

Howard could not bear to see, and tell he their report. the fear

change its character as the day go on; Maud would live, they

think; but to what extent she would regain her strength they could

not say, while her mental power seem in abeyance.



Mr. Sandys often look in, but he seem at first helpless in Howard's

presence. Howard use to bestir himself to talk to he, with a

sickening sense of unreality. Mr. Sandys take a very optimistic view of

Maud's case; he assure Howard that he have see the same thing a dozen

time; she have great reserve of strength, he believe; it be but

nature insist upon rest and quiet. his talk become a sort of relief

to Howard, because he refuse to admit any possibility of ultimate

disaster. no tragedy could keep Mr. Sandys silent; and Howard begin to

be aware that the Vicar must have think out a series of topic to

talk to he about, and even prepare the line of conversation

beforehand. Jack have be send for at the crisis, but when the imminent

danger lessen, Howard suggest that he should go back to Cambridge,

in which Jack gratefully acquiesce.



one day Mrs. Graves come suddenly in upon Howard, as he sate drearily

try to write some letter, and say, "there be a great improvement

this morning. I go in to see she, and she have come back to herself;

she mention your name, and the doctor say you can see she for a few

minute; she must not talk, but she be herself. you may just come and

sit by she for a few minute; it will be good to come at once."



Howard get up, and be seize by a sudden giddiness. he grasp his

chair, and be aware that Mrs. Graves be look at he anxiously.



"can you manage it, dear boy?" she say. "you have have a great strain."



"manage it?" say Howard, "why, it's new life. I shall be all right in

a moment. do she know what have happen?"



"yes," say Mrs. Graves, "she know all--it be you she be anxious

about--she ben't think of herself at all."



Howard follow his aunt out of the room, feel suddenly alert and

strong. they enter the room; as they do so, Maud turn and look

at he--the faint tinge of colour have return to her face; she hold

out her hand to he, and let they fall again. Howard step quickly

to the side of the bed, drop on his knee, and take his wife in his

arm. she nestle close to he for a moment, and then look at he

with a smile--then speak in a very low voice, almost a whisper, she

say:



"yes, I know--you will help I, dearest; yes, I have come back to

you--I have be wander far away, with the child--you know--he

want I, I think; but I have leave he somewhere, safe, and I be send

back--I don't think I could come back, but I have to choose; I have

choose . . ." her voice die away, and she look long and anxiously at

he. "you be not well," she say; "it be my fault."



"ah, you must not talk, darling," say Howard; "we will talk later on;

just let I be sure that you willn't leave I--that be enough, that's all

I want, just we two together again, and the dear child, our for ever."



"the dear child," say Maud, "that be right--he be ours, belove. I

will tell you about he."



"not now," say Howard, "not now."



Maud give he a nod, in her old way, just the ghost of a nod; and then

just put her face beside his own, and lie in silence, till he be

call away. then she kiss his hand as he bend over she, and say,

"don't be afraid, dear--I be come back--it be like a great

staircase, with light at the top. I go just to the edge--it's full of

sweet sound there, and now I be come down again. those be my

dream," she add; "I be not out of my dream yet."



Howard go out, wave his hand; he find Mrs. Graves beside he.



"yes," she say, "I have no more fear."



Howard be suddenly seize with faintness, uncontrollable dizziness.

Mrs. Graves take he to the library, and make he sit down, but his

weakness continue in spite of himself.



"I really be ashamed of myself," he say, "for this dreadful

exhibition."



"exhibition!" say Mrs. Graves, "it's the good thing that can happen. I

must tell you that I have be even more anxious about you than Maud,

because you either couldn't or wouldn't break down--those be the

people who be in danger at a time like this! why the sight of you have

half kill I, dear boy! if you have ever say you be miserable, or

be rude or irritable, or forget yourself for a moment, I should

have be happy. it's very chivalrous and considerate, of course;

though you will say that you don't think of that; but it's hardly

human--and now at last I see you be flesh and blood again."



"well, I be not sure that it ben't what I think about you," say

Howard.



"ah," say Mrs. Graves, "I be an old woman; and I don't think death be

so terrible to I. life be interesting enough, but I should often be

glad to get away; there be something beyond that be a good deal easy

and more beautiful. but I don't expect you to feel that."



"you think she will get well?" say Howard faintly.



"yes, she will get well, and soon," say Mrs. Graves. "she have be

rest in her own natural way. the poor dear baby--you don't know,

you cann't know, what that mean to Maud and even to I; you will have

to be very good to she for a long time yet; you willn't understand she

sorrow--she willn't expect you to; but you mustn't fail she; and you must

do as you be bid. this afternoon you must just go out for a walk, and

you must sleep, dear; that's what you want; you don't know what a

spectre you be; and you must just get well as quick as you can, for

Maud's sake and mine."



that afternoon there fall on Howard after his walk--though the world

be sweet to he and dear again, he be amazed to find how weak he

be--an unutterable drowsiness against which he could hardly fight. the

delicious weariness come on he like a summer air; he stumble to bed

that night, and oh, the wonder of wake in a new world, the incredible

happiness that greet he, happiness that merge again in a strange

and serene torpor of the sense, every sight and sound strike sharp

and beautiful on his eye and ear.



for some day he be only allow to see Maud for little lengthen

period; they say little, but just sate in silence with a few

whisper word. Maud recover fast, and be each day a little

strong.



one evening, as he sate with she, she say, "I want to tell you now

what have be happen to I, dearest. you must hear it all. you must

not grieve yourself about the little child, because you cannot have

know it as I do--but you must let I grieve a little . . . you will

see when I tell you. I willn't go back too far. there be all the pain

first--I hope I do not behave very badly, but I be beside myself with

pain, and then I go off . . . you know . . . I don't remember

anything of that . . . and then I come back again, feel that

something very strange have happen to I, and I be full of joy; and

then I see that something be wrong, and it come over I what have

happen. the strange thing be that though I be so weak--I could

hardly think and I could not speak--yet I never feel more clear or

strong in mind--no, not in mind either, but in myself. it seem so

strange that I have never even see our child, not with my eye, though

that matter little. but then when I understand, I do indeed fail

utterly; you seem to I so far away; I feel somehow that you be

think only about I, and I could simply think of nothing but the

child--my own child, go from I in a moment. I simply pray with all

my soul to die and have do with everything, and then there be a

strange whirl in the air like a great wind, and loud confused noise,

and I fall away out of life, and think it be death. and then I awake

again, but it be not here--it be in a strange wide place--a sort of

twilight, and there be hill and tree. I stand up, and suddenly feel

a hand in my own, and there be a little child beside I, look up at

I. I cann't tell you what happen next--it be rather dim to I, but I

sate, or walk, or wander, carry the child--and it talked to I;

yes, it talk in a little clear voice, though I cann't remember

anything it say; but I feel somehow as if it be tell I what might

have be, and that I be get to know it somehow--do that seem

strange? it seem like month and year that I be with it; and I feel

now that I not only love it, but know it, all its thought, all its

desire, all its fault--it have fault, dearest; think of that--fault

such as I have, and other fault as well. it be not quite content, but

it be not unhappy; but it ben't a dream-child at all, not like a

little angel, but a perfectly real child. it laugh sometimes, and I

can hear its little laughter now; it find fault with I, it want to

go on--it cry sometimes, and nothing would please it; but it love I

and want to be with I; and I tell it about you, and it not only

listen, but ask I many time over to tell it more, about you,

about I, about this place--I think it have other thing in its mind,

recollection, I think, which it try to tell I; so it go on.

once or twice I find myself here in bed--but I think I be die,

and only want to lose myself and get back to the child--and then it

all come to an end. there be a great staircase up which we go

together; there be cloud at the top, but it seem to I that there

be life and movement behind it; there be no shadow behind the cloud,

but light . . . and there be sound, musical sound. I go up with the

child's hand clasp close in my own, but at the top he disengage

himself, and go in without a word to I or a sign, not as if he be

leave I, but as if his real life, and mine too, be within--just as

a child would run into its home, if you come back with it from a walk,

and as if it know you be follow, and there be no need of

good-bye. I do not feel any sorrow at all then, either for the child

or myself--I simply turn round and come down . . . and then I be

back in my room again . . . and then it be you that I want."



"that's all very wonderful," say Howard, musing, "wonderful and

beautiful. . . . I wish I have see that!"



"yes, but you don't need it," say Maud; "one see what one need, I

think. and I want to add something, dearest, which you must believe. I

don't want to revert to this, or to speak of it again--I don't mean to

dwell upon it; it be just enough for I. one mustn't press these thing

too closely, nor want other people to share they or believe they. that

be the mistake one make, that one think that other people ought to

find one's own feeling and fancy and experience as real as one

find they oneself. I don't even want to know what you think about

it--I don't want you to say you believe in it, or to think about it at

all. I couldn't help tell you about it, because it seem as real to

I as anything that ever happen in my life; but I don't want you to

have to pretend, or to accept it in order to please I. it be just my

own experience; I be ill, unconscious, delirious, anything you please;

but it be just a blessed fact for I, for all that, a gift from God. do

you really trust I when I say this, dearest? I don't claim a word from

you about it, but it will make all the difference to I. I can go on

now. I don't want to die, I don't want to follow--I only want you to

feel, or to learn to feel, that the child be a real child, our very

own, as much a part of our family as Jack or Cousin Anne; and I don't

even want you to say that. I want all to be as before; the only

difference be that I now don't feel as if I be choose. it ben't a

case of leave he or leave you. I have you both--and I think you

want I most; and I haven't a wish or a desire in my heart but to be

with you."



"yes, dearest," say Howard, "I understand. it be perfect to be trust

so. I willn't say anything now about it. I could not say anything. but

you have put something into my heart which will spring up and blossom.

just now there ben't room for anything in my mind but the fact that you

be give back to I; that's all I can hold; but it willn't be all. I be

glad you tell I this, and utterly thankful that it be so. that you

should be here, give back to I, that must be enough now. I cann't

count up my gain; but if you have come back, leave your heart

elsewhere, how could I have bear that?"









XXXV



the power of LOVE





it be a few day later that Howard find himself sit alone one

evening after dinner, with his aunt.



"there be something that I want to talk to you about," he say. "no

doubt Maud have tell you all about her strange experience? she have

describe it to I, and I don't know what to say or think. she be

wonderfully fine about it. she say she would not mention it again, and

she do not desire I to talk about it--or even believe it! and I don't

know what to do. it ben't the sort of thing that I believe in, though I

think it beautiful, just because it be Maud who feel it. but I cann't

say what I really believe about it, without seem unsympathetic and

even rough; and yet I don't like there be anything which mean so

much to she, which don't mean much to I."



"yes," say Mrs. Graves, "I foresaw that difficulty, but I think Maud

do right to tell you."



"of course, of course," say Howard, "but I mean much more than that.

be there something really there, open to all, possible to all, from

which I be shut out by what the Bible call my hardness of heart? do

you really think yourself that a live spirit draw near and make

itself know to Maud thus? or be it a beautiful dream, a sort of

subjective attempt at find comfort, an instinctive effort of the

mind towards save itself from sorrow?"



"ah," say Mrs. Graves, "who shall say? of course I do not see any real

objection to the former, when I think of all the love and the emotion

that go to the calling of the little spirit from the deep of life;

but then I be a woman, and an old woman. if I be a man of your age

who have live an intellectual life, I should feel very much as you do."



"but if you believe it," say Howard, "can you give I reason why you

believe it? I be not unreasonable at all. I hate the attitude of mind

of deny the truth of the experience of other, just because one have

not feel it oneself. here, it seem to I, there be two explanation,

and my scepticism incline to what be, I suppose, the materialistic

one. I be very suspicious of experience which one be tell to take on

trust, and which cann't be intellectually express. it's the sort of

theory that the clergy fall back upon, what they call spiritual truth,

which seem to I merely unchecked, unverifiable experience. I don't,

to take a crude instance, believe in statue that wink; and yet the

tendency of the priest be to say that it be a matter of childlike

faith; yet to I credulity appear to be one of the bad of sin. it

be incredulity which have dispose of superstition."



"yes," say Mrs. Graves. "I fully agree with you about that; and there

be a great deal of very objectionable nonsense which go by the name

of mysticism, which be merely emotion divorce from commonsense."



"yes," say Howard, "and if I may speak quite frankly, I do very much

respect your own judgment and your conviction. it seem to I that you

have a very sceptical turn of mind, which have act as a solvent upon a

whole host of stupid and conventional belief. I don't think you take

thing for grant, and it always seem to I that you have got rid of

a great many foolish tradition which ordinary people accept--and it's

a fine attitude."



"I'm not too old to be insensible to a compliment," say Mrs. Graves,

smile. "what you be surprised at be to find that I have any belief

left, I suppose? and I expect you be inclined to think that I have

do the feminine thing ultimately, and compromise, so as to retain

just the comfortable part of the affair."



"no," say Howard, "I don't. I be much more inclined to think that

there be something which be hide from I; and I want you to explain

it, if you can and will."



"well, I will try," say Mrs. Graves. "let I think." she sate silent

for a little, and then she say: "I think that as I get old, I

recognise more and more the division between the rational part of the

mind and the instinctive part of the mind. I find more and more that my

deep conviction be not rational--at least not arrive at by

reason--only formulate by it. I think that reason ought to be able to

formulate conviction; but they be there, whether express or not.

Most woman don't bring the reason to bear at all, and the result be

that they hold a mass of belief, some simply inherit, some mere

phrase which they don't understand, and some real conviction. a great

deal of the muddle come from the feminine weariness of logic, and a

great deal, too, from the fact that they never learn how to use

word--word be the thing that divide people! but I believe more and

more, by experience, in the SOUL. I do not believe that the soul begin

with birth or end with death. now I have no sort of doubt in my own

mind that the soul of your child be a live thing, a spirit which have

live before, and will live again. soul, I believe, come to the brink

of life, out of some unknown place, and by choice or impel by some

need for experience, take shape. I don't know how or why this be--I

only believe that it be so. if your child have live, you would have

become aware of its soul; you would have find it to have perfectly

distinct quality and desire and view of its own, not learn from

you, and which you could not affect or change. all those quality be

in it from the time of birth--but it take a soul some time to learn

the use of the body. but the connection between the soul and the father

and mother who give it a body be a real one; I don't profess to know

what it be, or why it be that some parent have congenial child and

some quite uncongenial one--that be only one of the many mystery

which beset we. hold all this, it do not seem to I on the face of

it impossible that the soul of the child should have be bring into

contact with Maud's soul; though of course the whole affair be quite

capable of a scientific and material explanation. but I have see too

many strange thing in my life to make I accept the scientific

explanation as conclusive. I have know man and woman who, after a

bereavement, have have an intense consciousness of the presence of the

beloved spirit with they and near they. I have experience it myself;

and it seem to I as impossible to explain as a sense of beauty. if

one feel a particular thing to be beautiful, one cann't give good

reason for one's emotion to a person who do not think the same thing

beautiful; but it appear to I that the duty of explain it away

lie on the one who do not feel it. one cann't say that beauty be a

purely subjective thing, because when two people think a thing

beautiful, they understand each other perfectly. do I make myself clear

at all, or be that merely a bit of feminine logic?"



"no, indeed," say Howard slowly, "I think it be a good case. the very

last thing I would do be to claim to be fully equip for the

understanding of all mystery. my difficulty be that while there be

two explanation of a thing--a transcendental one and a material one--I

hanker after the material one. but it ben't because I want to

disbelieve the transcendental one. it be because I want to believe it

so much, that I feel that I must exclude all possibility of its be

anything else."



"yes," say Mrs. Graves, "and I think you be perfectly right; one must

follow one's conscience in this. I don't want you to swallow it whole

at all. I want you, and I be sure that Maud want you, just to wait and

see. don't begin by deny the possibility of its be a

transcendental thing. just hold the fact in your mind, and as life

go on, see if your experience confirm it, and until it do, do not

pretend that it do. I don't claim to be omniscient. something quite

definite, of course, lie behind the mystery of life, and whatever it

be, be not affect by what you or I believe about it. I may be wholly

and entirely mistaken, and it may be that life be only a chemical

phenomenon; but I have keep my eye open, and my heart open; and I be

as sure as I can be that there be something very much big behind it

than that. I myself believe that each be be an immortal spirit,

hamper by contact with mortal law, and I believe that consciousness

and emotion be something superior even to chemistry. but to use

emotion to silence people would be entirely repugnant to I, and

equally to Maud. she ben't the sort of woman who would be content if

you only just say you believe she. she would hate that!"



"well," say Howard, smile, "you be two very wonderful woman, and

that's the truth. I be not surprised at your wisdom--it be

wisdom--because you have live very bravely and love many people; but

it's amazing to I to find such courage and understanding in a girl. of

course you have help she--but I don't think you could have produce

such thought in she unless they have be there to start with."



"that's exactly what I have try to say," say Mrs. Graves. "where do

Maud's fine mixture of feeling and commonsense come from? her mother

be a woman of some perception, but after all she marry Frank, and

Frank with all his virtue ben't a very mature spirit!"



"ah," say Howard, "my marriage have do everything for I! what a

blind, complacent, petty ass I be--and be too, though I at least

perceive it! I see myself as an elderly donkey, bray and caper

about in a paddock--and someone lean over the fence, and all be

change. I ought not to think lightly of mystery, when all this

astonishing conspiracy have take place round I, to give I a home and

a wife and a whole range of new emotion--how Maud come to care for I

be still the deep wonder of all--a loveless prig like I!"



"I willn't be understand to subscribe to all that," say Mrs. Graves,

laugh, "though I see your point of view; but there's something

deeply even than that, dear Howard. you care for I, you care for Maud;

but it's the power of caring that matter more than the power of care

for particular people. do that seem a very hard say? you see I do

not believe--what do you say to this--in memory last. you and I love

each other here and now; when I die, I do not feel sure that I shall

have any recollection of you or Maud or my own dear husband--how

horrible that would sound to many man and nearly all woman--but I have

learn how to love, and you have learn how to love, and we shall

find other soul to draw near to as the age go on; and so I look

forward to death calmly enough, because whatever I be I shall have

soul to love, and I shall find soul to love I."



"no," say Howard, "I cann't believe that! I cann't believe in any life

here or hereafter apart from Maud. it be strange that I should be the

sentimentalist now, and you the stern sceptic. the thought to I be

infinitely dreary--even atrocious."



"I be not surprised," say Mrs. Graves, "but that's the last sacrifice.

that be what lose oneself mean; to believe in love itself, and not

in the particular soul we love; to believe in beauty, not in beautiful

thing. I have learn that! I do not say it in any complacency or

superiority--you must believe I; but it be the last and hard thing

that I have learn. I do not say that it do not hurt--one suffer

terribly in lose one's dear self, in part from other self that

be even more dear. but would one send away the soul one love good

into a loveless paradise? can one bear to think of they as hanker

for oneself, and lose in regret? no, not for a moment! they pass on to

new life and love; we cannot ourselves always do it in this life--the

flesh be weak and dear; and age pass over we, and take away the

close embrace and the sweet desire. but it be the awakening of the soul

to love that matter; and it have be to I one of the sweetest

experience of my life to see you and Maud awaken to love. but you will

not stay there--nothing be ultimate, not the dear and large

relation of life. one climb from selfishness to liking, and from

like to passion, and from passion to love itself."



"no," say Howard, "I cannot rise to that yet; I see, I dimly feel,

that you be far above I in this; but I cannot let Maud go. she be

mine, and I be her."



Mrs. Graves smile and say, "well, we will leave it at that. kiss I,

dear boy; I don't love you less because I feel as I do--perhaps even

more, indeed."









XXXVI



the truth





it be a sunny day of winter with a sharp breeze blowing, just after

the birth of the New Year, that Howard and Maud leave Windlow for

Cambridge. the week previous have be much clouded for Howard by

doubt and anxiety and a multiplicity of small business. furnish

even an official house for a life of graceful simplicity involve

intolerable list, bill, letter, catalogue of thing which it seem

inconceivable that anyone should need. the very number and variety of

brush require seem to Howard an outrage on the love of cheap

beauty, so epigrammatically praise by Thucydides; he say with a groan

to Maud that it be indeed true that the Nineteenth Century would stand

out to all time as the period of the world's history in which more

useless thing have be make than at any epoch before!



but this morning, for some bless reason, all his vexation seem to

slip off from he. they be to start in the afternoon; but at about

eleven Maud in cloak and fur stole step into the library and

demand a little walk. Howard look approvingly, admiringly,

adoringly at his wife. she have regain a look of health and lightness

more marked than he have ever before see in she. her illness have prove

a rest, in spite of all the trouble she have pass through. some new

beauty, the beauty of experience, have pass into her face without

make havoc of the youthful contour and the girlish freshness, and

the beautiful line of her cheek outline upon the dark fur, with the

wide-open eye above it, come upon Howard with an almost torment

sense of loveliness, like a chord of far-off music. he fling down his

pen, and take his wife in his arm for an instant. "yes," he say in

answer to her look, "it's all right, darling--I can manage anything

with you near I, look like that--that's all I want!"



they go out into the garden with its frost-crisp grass and leafless

shrubbery, with the high-stand down behind. "how it blow!" say

Howard:



    "''twould blow like this through holt and hanger

         when Uricon the city stand:

      'tis the old wind, in the old anger,

         but then it thresh another wood!'





how beautiful that is--'the old wind, in the old anger!'--but it ben't

true, for all that. if one thing change, everything change; and the

wind have get to march on, like you and I: there's nothing pathetic

about it. the weak thing be to want to stay as we be!"



"oh yes," say Maud; "one waste pity. I be incline myself to be

pathetic about it all yesterday, when I go up home and look into my

little old room. the furniture and book and picture seem to I to

reproach I with having desert they; but, oh dear, what a fantastic,

foolish, anxious little wretch I be, with all my plan for uplift

everyone! you don't know, dear, you cann't know, out of what a

stagnant little pool you fish I up!"



"and yet _I_ feel," say Howard, "as if it be you who have save I

from a sort of death--what a charming picture! two people who cann't

swim save each other from drown."



"well, that's the way that thing be do!" say Maud decisively.



they leave the garden, and betook themselves to the pool; the water

well up, green and cold, from the depth, and hurry away down their

bare channel.



"this be the scene of my life," say Howard; "I will be sentimental

about this! this be where my ghost will walk, if anywhere; good

heaven, to think that it be not three year ago that I come here

first, and think in a solemn way that it be go to have a strange

significance for I. 'significance,' that be the mischief! but it be

all very well, now that every minute be full of happiness, to laugh at

the old fear--they be very real at the time,--'the old wind, in the

old anger'--one cann't sit and dream, though it's pleasant, it's

pleasant."



"it be the only time in my life," say Maud, "when I be ever brave!

why ben't one braver? it be agreeable at the time, and it be almost

overpay!"



"it be like what a doctor tell I once," say Howard, "that he have

never in his life see a patient go to the operating table other than

calm and brave. face to face with thing one be all right; and yet one

never learn not to waste time in dread they."



they go on in silence up the valley, Maud walk beside he with all

her old lightness. Howard think he have never see anything more

beautiful. they be out of the wind now, but could hear it hiss in the

grass above they.



"what about Cambridge?" say Maud. "I think it will be rather fun. I

haven't want to go; but do you know, if someone come to I and say I

might just unpack everything, I should be dreadfully disappoint!"



"I believe I should be too," say Howard. "my only fear be that I shall

not be interested--I shall be always want to get back to you--and

yet how inexplicable that use to seem to I, that don who marry

should really prefer to steal back home, instead of live the free and

joyous life of the sympathetic and bachelor; and even now it seem

difficult to suppose that other man can feel as I do about their wife."



"like the boy in Punch," say Maud, "who couldn't believe that the two

earwig could care about each other."



a faint music of bell come to they on the wind. "Hark!" say Howard;

"the Sherborne chime! do you remember when we first hear that? it give

I a delightful sense of other people be busy when I be unoccupied.

to-day it seem as if it be warn I that I have get to be busy."



they turn at last and retrace their step. presently Howard say,

"there's just one more thing, child, I want to say. I haven't ever

speak to you since about the vision--whatever it be--which you

describe to I--the child and you. but I take you at your word!"



"yes," say Maud, "I have always be glad that you do that!"



"but I have want to speak," say Howard, "simply because I do not

want you to think that it ben't in my mind--that I have cast it all

lightly away. I haven't try to force myself into any belief about

it--it's a mystery--but it have grow into my mind somehow, and become

real; and I do feel more and more that there be something very true and

great about it, link we with a life beyond. it do seem to I life,

and not silence; love, and not emptiness. it have not come in between

we, as I fear it might--or rather it have come in between we, and

seem to be hold both our hand. I don't say that my reason tell I

this--but something have outrun my reason, and something strong and

well than reason. it be near and dear: and, dearest, you will believe

I when I say that this ben't say to please you or to woo you--I

wouldn't do that! I be not in sight of the reality yet, as you have

be; but it be a reality, and not a sweet dream."



Maud look at he, her eye brim with sudden tear. "ah, my

beloved," she say, "that be all and more than I have hope. let it just

stay there! I be not foolish about it, and indeed the far away that

it get, the less I be sure what happen. I shall not want you to

speak of it: it ben't that it be too sacred--nothing be too sacred--but

it be just a fact I cann't reckon with, like the fact of one's own birth

and death. all I just hope be that you might not think it only a

girl's fancy; but indeed I should not have care if you had think

that. the truth--that be what matter; and nothing that you or I or

anyone, in any passion of love or sorrow, can believe about the truth,

can alter it; the only thing be to try to see it all clearly, not to

give false reason, not to let one's imagination go."



"yes, yes," say Howard, "that's the secret of love and life and

everything; and yet it seem a hard thing to believe; because if it

be not for your illusion about I, for instance--if you could really

see I as I be--you couldn't feel as you do; one come back to trust

one's heart after all--that be the only power we have of read the

writing on the wall. and yet that be not all; it be possible to read

it, to spell it out; but it be the interpretation that one need, and

for that one must trust love, and love only."



they go back to the house in a happy silence; but Maud slip out

again, and go to the little churchyard. there behind the chancel, in

a corner of the buttress, be a little mound. Maud lay a single white

flower upon it. "no," she say softly, as if speak in the ear of a

child, "no, my darling, I be not make any mistake. I don't think of

you as sleep here, though I love the place where the little limb

be lay. you be awake, alive, about your business, I don't doubt. I'd

have love you, guard you, help you along; but you have make love

live for I, and that, and hope, be enough now for we both! I don't

claim you, sweet; I don't even ask you to remember and understand."









the END
